While the griddle and dishes air-dried, I entered the shopping mall to scrounge for “anything useful.”

“I want tableware. I also need cleaning tools… What else would I need for guests?”

Lumbering along, I peered into shops one by one.

Then one store caught my eye.

“…Watches, huh.”

They no longer ran.

But their technical value was undeniable.

In that case, how about a hand-wound pocket watch?

I moderately wrecked the entrance and squeezed inside.

The shop had already been looted, but I checked the back room too.

After rummaging a while, I found all the wristwatches totaled.

There were mantel clocks, but none would run—and I couldn’t make them run.

With key-wound pocket watches I felt a “maybe…,” but I couldn’t find the crucial item.

I dragged out box after box and checked the contents until I finally found what I was after.

“Ah, I think this is…”

I even recognized the logo on the pocket watch.

It was from the manufacturer in those TV commercials: “Still fine after 100 years.”

That boded well, so I took out all five pocket watches left in the box.

“Whether they run or not, I can check later… Let’s grab a few other samples.”

I snatched two or three compact mantel clocks and put them in a basket before leaving the watch shop.

Next, as I headed to secure tableware, I realized something.

It was truly “now you notice?” stuff, but most high-value, easy-to-carry items had been looted.

Naturally, I had already confirmed that all the silverware was gone, but I hadn’t checked any jewelry stores yet.

It was surely hopeless, but hidden caches or vault contents might have survived.

Thinking I might trade with elves or the Furon Council someday, I figured it was best to bring back some valuables.

I decided to gather necessities first, then search, so I made the rounds and collected what I wanted.

Before long, yet another “mountain” had formed in the mall.

At this now-customary sight, I couldn’t help but give a wry smile.

Rather than “I want this and that,” the pile was the result of hauling anything that “looked decent.”

Which meant I now had to select only what I truly needed from it.

After about an hour of sorting, I found I needed nothing beyond what I had planned.

What on earth had possessed me to bring pet supplies and a cat-ear headband?

I must have grabbed things in the heat of the moment; I couldn’t remember.

(Anyway, a pocket watch will do as a return gift for Number Six… and I’ll hit the bookstore just in case.)

I picked up the now-dry griddle and widened the entrance to a small bookshop I was heading for.

Inside, I squeezed through the cramped stacks and hunted for books.

When I picked up a cat photo book, I remembered.

“Ah right, the pet supplies were for Dame Oppai.”

To be blunt, it was for mischief.

I had predicted that if I gave Number Six a return gift, Dame Oppai would say, “What about me?”—so I planned to hand her the pet supplies.

It was meant to carry the message, “You’ve got nothing better to do than take care of a pet, right?”—but I couldn’t find a cat-tail accessory and gave up halfway.

Those pet supplies were the remnants of that plan.

At any rate, I grabbed fashion magazines in decent condition and photo books of cats and dogs.

“…I can’t quite picture her being thrilled.”

Maybe books were unnecessary.

I decided to pick something for myself and looked for new releases that had come out while I’d been sleeping.

Choosing copies in good condition, I stood and read—and by the time I noticed, a lot of time had passed.

The sun was near its zenith; I must have been reading for two hours.

I returned the manga to the shelf, went back to the mall, and packed the sorted tableware and household goods into my backpack.

Seeing I still had room, I moved on.

Target: a jewelry store or a bank—in short, a sweep for leftovers.

I wanted to check whether any precious metals or gemstones remained, but not knowing the locations, I could only search.

So I wandered the ruined city.

At the same time I checked for goblins; if any idiots hadn’t fled yet, I culled them on sight.

After marching the main street for a while, I found a bank—its looting was obvious.

Inside was a disaster as well; two centuries had made it worse, to the point you could scarcely tell what it had been.

I picked my way over rubble and shattered glass toward the back, but as expected, the vault was open and there seemed nothing portable left.

I trudged back out and resumed walking the cracked main thoroughfare.

Two more hours of exploration turned up no jewelry store.

Instead, I found a large ransacked house; when I entered, I discovered an unopened safe.

I smashed it and obtained several rings and other jeweled adornments.

It wasn’t what I’d planned, but my instincts weren’t bad—I left the city of Eilqueel.

So this haul was: tableware and daily goods, folding chairs, and accessories.

And the headliners—pocket watches. Not a bad lineup.

As a bonus, I also brought back the cat-ear headband—call it a bit of charm.

Even if Number Six refused, if I sweet-talked Dame Oppai she’d probably wear it.

(Elves with cat ears… now that’s new. Damn, I should’ve searched for more of that sort of gear while I was at it.)

I considered going back to look, and while scanning the mall I got an idea.

“That’s it. If there are clothes in good condition, I’ll bring those.”

Then, if a guest ever needed a change, I could casually offer them.

Picturing Number Six in a uniform, I changed plans and started another sweep.

There was still space in my backpack; neatly folded clothes would let me cram in a decent number.

Just then, something caught my eye.

“…Underwear?”

Careless of me.

Clothes were one thing—but I’d forgotten the essentials.

Underwear—the thing that would make an elf’s already illicit figure even more dangerous.

(I’d forgotten. Chasing naked bodies at the river, I’d overlooked the glory of underwear.)

I regretted it.

If only I’d thought of it sooner—had I brought women’s underwear back, I could have enjoyed Dame Oppai’s inevitable misunderstanding all the more.

But it wasn’t too late even now.

Trusting there would be a “next time,” I wedged my upper body into the shop and began careful selection, piece by piece.

Naturally, anything on display was no good.

I needed to find intact stock in the storeroom.

I shoved shelves aside to make a path and reached the back room.

From the outside, it must have looked awful—a monster rummaging through women’s underwear—but I was past the point of caring.

I secured plenty, though it wouldn’t all fit in my pack, so I pared it down—

Because, come on, I even found black garters as a clincher. No way I was leaving those.

All that remained was how to get someone to model the set.

On the run back to base with my backpack, that was all I could think about.

I thought it through.

If staying on the battlefield shortened the time I could remain human, then I should go the opposite way.

Which meant I needed healing—comfort—right now.

So this lingerie would surely extend my lifespan as a human.

+++

# **96**

Back at my base, I grilled meat while thinking.

Facing the thorny problem of “How do I get Number Six to wear the underwear I collected?” without an answer yet, I committed the unthinkable mistake for a craftsman—I overcooked the meat.

That was how deep in thought I was, but unlike Dame Oppai, Number Six radiated an intellectual air; if I tried verbal sparring with her, I would be countered for sure.

Therefore I considered what was needed to have her wear it casually, without spotting my ulterior motive.

(It was best to exploit circumstances—that was the tried-and-true method. Then the optimal plan was to hand over a full set when her clothes got wet. But how to engineer that? Hand it to her when she was at the river? That would be way too unnatural. So what situation would soak her clothes through?)

Unfortunately, it did not rain often here.

Even if it did, the forest served as a natural umbrella, so it was hard to imagine Number Six getting wet enough to need a change of clothes.

Talking her into it would be difficult.

If exploiting circumstances was also difficult, then all that remained was to ask, “Please wear this.”

“If only it were that easy…”

I muttered as I chewed slightly charred meat.

My pepper supply had dwindled a lot; I needed to consider replenishing it.

I still had plenty of salt, but come to think of it, I still hadn’t met my goal of improving my food situation.

Too many problems.

I had meant to solve them one by one, but new ones kept cropping up.

(No, more than “problems,” it was my desires. My memories of Imperial days shaped who I was now. Whatever I compared to, I ended up back there.)

I had brought back books in the end, but if asked whether they would “be useful,” I regrettably could not say.

As for technical books, I had a plan to take them to the Furon Council, but unless I knew their current level of civilization, a fair trade would be impossible.

Also, considering “who” might step forward on their side—even against a monster, the fearless woman would absolutely try to talk circles around me if I faced her.

Sincerity only applied between humans.

I returned my thoughts to the task and kept eating.

A straight-up fight left me at a disadvantage.

Worse, if I botched it, she would realize I saw her that way.

That, above all, I had to avoid.

I had to keep interacting with them as a monster, nothing more.

Otherwise it would be hard to sustain Number Six’s interest as a member of the Natural Harmony Committee.

“How about giving it as a present?”

I swallowed a well-chewed mouthful and murmured.

Fortunately, the underwear I had brought back had been in sealed boxes, so it still looked brand-new.

Each piece had been carefully packaged—probably expensive stuff.

“If I frame it as just another type of clothing and gift it along with other items… could that work?”

If I played up the “I’m a monster; I don’t really get these things” angle, it might fly.

No—wouldn’t that be too contrived?

I wrestled with this through the end of the meal, but thinking calmly, I realized: “What would it look like if a monster gave someone underwear?” Result: I scrapped the idea.

However, while reading to kill time at night, I saw a gravure model in a swimsuit and had a flash.

“That’s it! I’ll claim I thought it was like a swimsuit.”

If so, there should be no problem—I was very pleased with myself and slept soundly that night.

The next morning—no way. I clutched my head and abandoned last night’s foolish line of thought.

(No, maybe it wasn’t terrible. But whether Number Six would wear it was another matter.)

At this point, I would settle for Dame Oppai—that compromise flashed through my mind.

But then my earthly desires handed me a new plan.

(That’s right. If it’s “by the way,” it won’t seem suspicious!)

Yes—if I made Number Six, my true target, the “by the way,” nothing would look odd.

I would put Dame Oppai front and center to conceal my real objective.

Conveniently, Ashiru had mentioned her goal of “becoming a model.”

So I would help with that.

I remembered that the facility’s consolidated bookshelf included fashion magazines.

If I tucked one in, it would justify gifting underwear—and then I could include a set for Number Six “as an aside” without raising eyebrows.

I had already researched bra sizes and secured sets for Number Six and Number Eight.

Unfortunately, sizes above No. 9 were essentially custom order, so I couldn’t get those—but I would mix in sizes for No. 5 and No. 7 as camouflage, erasing the oddity of “he knew our exact sizes.”

Both of them knew I had seen them naked; if they thought, “He brought a range that’s roughly our size,” I would win.

(…This could work. I could hand them over without worry!)

The remaining question was whether they would actually wear them—but that was up to fate.

As for seeing them wear it, I ranked the difficulty low, thinking, “Can’t I just watch at the river?”

That might mean I could no longer see them naked… but that was where the little brats came in.

If they could yank off panties, getting a bra or two shouldn’t be a problem.

So I immediately prepared a gift box and carefully laid the underwear at the bottom.

I included the fashion magazines in the box, but stored the pocket watch in its case in my backpack.

That one was for a direct handoff, separately.

All that remained was to write lines on paper in advance, and preparations would be complete.

I had split items into “for gifting” and “for guest changes,” so even if the plan failed, there would be a next time.

For now, since I hadn’t had breakfast yet, I went hunting.

I surfaced and searched the woods for prey.

In about thirty minutes I bagged a fine deer, returned, butchered it, and—as always—started yakiniku.

Watching the griddle, I drafted the text for the notes.

Once I had the content mostly settled, the meat was gone, and I began cleaning up.

I left dishes to air-dry and finished five notes; I tore off one and slipped it into the box.

In brief, it said: “It wouldn’t be fair to give only Dame Oppai something, so I put in a set for Number Six too. If it’s well received, feel free to tell me what you think. I’ll bring more next time I’m coming anyway.”

These were barter items, so I hoped they would add some color to the elves’ meals.

As for the jewelry… I decided not to use it this time.

Thinking it over, I didn’t know the prices for that sort of thing.

And it wasn’t wise to bring out too much at once.

For those reasons, I postponed it.

With cleanup done, I packed up and set out for the elves’ village.

If I took my time, it would be about when Number Six was at the river.

Since I was at it, I would walk while checking the wild plants I had learned.

Taking it easy like that meant I later had to sprint full tilt for a bit.

That’s what I got for taking it too easy.

Near the river, I slowed down and said hello.

Waiting for me by the river were Number Six and the children hiding behind her.

Apparently the brats had yet to claim victory; Number Six’s damp chemise clung snugly to her skin.

(White, so it was perfectly see-through. This was also great!)

I gave a little nod her way with an inward thumbs-up.

Seeing that, she exhaled in relief, pressing a hand to her chest.

(Oh, right, there was that alarm-like thing. I see—so she moved the children behind her.)

I wondered if she had no escorts, but there were no monsters here that would trouble elves.

There was a “monster” named Argos, but I was behaving like a gentleman, so all was safe.

“Did you read the letter?”

Number Six called out to me from there.

I nodded, took out the note I had prepared, and stepped into the river.

“These were excellent fruits. I’d like more—do you have a surplus? Also, I have something to give you.”

After showing that note, I added one more line there:

“It isn’t urgent. Take care of things here first.”

Having read it, Number Six called the children over.

I kept my distance and washed myself downstream, then caught fish—drawing the kids’ eyes the entire time.

Thanks to that, the brats never made a play at Number Six, and the river lesson ended with her not stripped at all.

After she finished changing, she called to me, and I left the site.

When I noticed, I had quite a haul of fish swimming in a simple stone-bordered pen; when I pointed at it, she scolded me:

“If you’re not going to eat them, let them go.”

Apparently I was the one in the wrong—the kids were laughing.

When I trudged over to break the pen, one of them ran up, perhaps because they finally lowered their guard.

“You look lame!”

I snatched the brat’s leg with a quick tail-flick and sentenced him to the “upside-down hanging punishment.”

He didn’t cry or apologize; he whooped with delight.

Kids were incomprehensible—this was enough to send their excitement soaring.

The upside-down brat swung and squealed, while Number Six flustered over what to do.

Scoring goodwill points with the elven children, I walked alongside her.

It seemed she wanted me to come along to the village.

I had missed the timing to give her the pocket watch… well, I could do it after we arrived.

+++

# **97**

Because we mostly matched the children’s pace, we headed toward the village at a leisurely speed.

One kid was still dangling from my tail and having a blast, and maybe because their guard dropped along the way, other kids started to cling to me, saying, “Carry me, carry me.”

With a sigh, I let a few ride on my shoulders, and the little punks immediately got cocky.

One of them put his hands on the knobby parts of my back and started to climb.

Taking advantage of my leaning forward, the boy who reached the “summit” thrust a finger at the sky and basked in applause.

What, did he think he was a mountaineer now?

Anyway, I snagged the overexcited brat with my tail and swung him lazily left and right, and soon a little line had formed.

I was forced to admit I had underestimated children’s adaptability.

By the time we reached the village, the kids had grown awfully fond of me.

(No—was it more like as a “piece of playground equipment”? Or did they just add me to their favorites list as a “toy”?)

Parting from the children, who called out “We want to play more,” I let Number Six guide me through the village.

When the children were finally out of sight, she stopped and stared at me.

“To be honest, I’m surprised. I thought you would keep more distance from the children.”

Now that she said it, that certainly would have been easier.

That said, thanks to that I got to see Number Six’s smile like this, so as payment for my labor it was not bad.

I took the memo pad from the strap on my arm and let the pen glide.

“I just let them do as they pleased.”

Playing it off as if I were hiding embarrassment at being seen from an unexpected angle, I drew a chuckle from Number Six.

Just the reaction I wanted.

If so, the gift offensive should go smoothly too.

Thinking that, we walked under startled stares for ten minutes—and arrived, presumably, at her house.

Any thought like “Whoa, I’m getting a little nervous” vanished in an instant.

Because her home was enormous.

(I knew she came from a fairly influential family, but did it really have to be this big?)

It was a two-story wooden building, old but with a garden, a storehouse, and what looked like three separate annexes.

And there was one more—a large building that looked like a training hall, which seemed to be where we were headed.

My huge body certainly could not fit inside a house.

So I was shown into that building; ignoring the elves inside who gaped at me, I sat down wherever.

The floorboards creaked and groaned, and the ceiling did not have much clearance either, so it could not be helped.

Still, sitting on the ground after being invited in felt a bit off.

Told to wait there for a while, I lounged around under the stares of the men training.

I checked my gifts, took the water tank from my pack, and drank.

No one thought to bring me a drink—clearly I wasn’t exactly welcome.

In fact, the mere fact Number Six could invite such a vicious-looking monster reminded me of her standing.

After a short wait, Number Six returned, having changed clothes.

It was the usual elf outfit, but with her figure it looked like something else entirely.

Behind her, Dame Oppai—gaunt but with a body that made clothes look even more like something else—peeked out.

Perfect, I thought with a grin inside.

The moment Dame Oppai spotted me, she pointed and tried to shout something, but a woman behind her poked her in the side, and she emitted an impermissible “geh-boh!” sound.

“Glad to see you’re lively.”

When I flashed the quickly written words, she raised a fist, only for an elbow to jab her ribs again; she collapsed, groaning, and stayed down.

“Anyway, I’m sure you have things you want to ask, but let me give you a present first.”

I showed the prepared note and first handed over the case with the pocket watch.

Number Six opened it, checked the contents, and frowned.

“Is this… a relic of the old Empire?”

I nodded to her clouded expression and realized I had chosen poorly.

“I thought it would make a good conversation piece.”

“We would prefer not to have such items brought in.”

After reading my note, Number Six replied; then the woman behind her briefly explained the situation.

“In the war with the Empire, we suffered no small number of casualties. So there’s a tendency to shun items that use that technology.”

I already knew this.

In short, I had forgotten that this world effectively rejected science.

I could not help but give a wry smile.

This made the underwear gift a little shaky too.

I almost regretted not bringing the jewelry—things felt perilous.

“I understand these items aren’t welcome.”

Showing that note, I beckoned Dame Oppai.

I hadn’t been able to seize the advantage, but I was not the sort to back down over that.

She approached with a dubious look; I gently held out the box I had prepared, but she was too wary to check inside.

Prompted by Number Six, she finally opened it, tilted her head at the contents, and looked confused.

When I pointed at the book inside, she carefully picked it up and flipped it open while still keeping an eye on me.

I waited until then, drew out the prepared note, and placed it gently atop the open page.

“I got these for you since you said you wanted to be a model. Take as many as you like.”

After reading the note, Dame Oppai flipped through the book and then pulled a black set of underwear from the box.

Starting with black—decent taste.

I wanted to praise her, but that would go to her head.

Best to keep quiet and watch; she stood, clenched the black set, and strode toward me.

Then, with a shriek of “Kii-eee!” she punched me in the face as I sat.

Naturally she clutched her hand and rolled on the floor in pain.

I swallowed the line “That’s why you’re Dame Oppai,” and only tilted my head as if to say, “Why punch me?”

“You’re up to something again, aren’t you!”

Her impossible perceptiveness made me question whether she was really “Dame Oppai,” but then I glanced at the women behind her and decided it was the fruit of their training.

“I simply wanted to see how far you, as you are, could go.”

Of course I meant it in the “bad” sense, but Dame Oppai, frowning, was already on the verge of being fooled.

Yes, “Dame Oppai” suited her just fine.

“Could you stop playing with Ashiru?”

Uh-oh—rebuked by Number Six.

True, the problem child seemed improved with a measure of discipline.

Perhaps she didn’t want her undone.

(Well, fine. There’s no way training alone makes her decent. One day I’ll have her posing in underwear before me.)

I casually suggested,

“There are plenty—would you care for some as well?”

But Number Six made an X with her arms and, with a smile, answered coolly,

“No, thank you.”

For the record, the men in the training hall sagged visibly at that line.

So, there were those who got it.

This even opened the option of subtle pressure from them—perhaps there were still chances?

Just as I seriously began considering that possibility, Number Six cleared her throat to reset the mood and spoke up.

“I’ll be blunt. Do you know what’s under that underground site?”

It was more direct than I expected, but within my assumptions.

So my pen moved without hesitation over the memo.

When I handed it to her, she read it aloud.

“‘I can’t enter the depths, so I haven’t seen it with my own eyes. But I have a fair idea what’s there,’ is it…”

Likely the answer she expected. Her voice grew a little flat, and the atmosphere in the hall seemed to shift.

A brief hush—then Number Six broke it.

“Then, do you know what that thing is?”

Silently, I shook my head.

I knew—but I could not tell.

As I stayed quiet, I heard words from Number Six I could not ignore, spoken with a grave expression.

“What was there amounted to an affront to life—it is no exaggeration to call it that. We have judged that it must be erased from this world. Not ‘toying with’ life, but remaking it—no, it was an abominable act to create a new life, a new species. We cannot overlook it. Since a taboo lies there, we must act. Will you help us?”

+++

# **98**  

I met eyes that looked straight at me — and I understood at a glance that there was no way I could bend her will.

But I also had my own circumstances, so I couldn’t accept that unconditionally.

And I could no longer reveal the reasons, indeed I needed to keep the truth hidden.

(A taboo, huh… That damn hag made a fuss about that before. If this falls under the same category, no matter how I think about it I’m probably a candidate for eradication.)

What I wanted help with and what they intended to do did not line up at all.

With the current atmosphere that felt like they might wipe out that underground facility entirely, there was no way I could agree to the possibility that my base would be destroyed.

Then again, stubbornly refusing might arouse suspicion.

So I chose my words carefully and tested the waters with a slightly extreme conclusion:

“So, does that mean you intend to destroy my base?”

“In some cases, we think that would be necessary.”

She affirmed it flatly, and I fell silent as I stared up at the ceiling.

Apparently the term “taboo” carried far more weight than I had expected.

What purpose lay behind this hardline stance towards me?

(Do they think I’ll accept it… or are they prepared to crush me even if it means eliminating me?)

If it was the former, they were being overly presumptuous; if the latter, they didn’t understand my capabilities.

Or perhaps they were prepared to go that far — but I couldn’t see a reason that would demand such measures.

The key word was “taboo” — and because I didn’t know the weight behind that word, I couldn’t make sense of her attitude.

I closed my mouth, which had started to open, and put pen to the memo again.

“What is a taboo?”

There were many things I wanted to ask, but this question would most simply reveal the result.

Number Six read the memo and everyone around fell silent.

The flowing quiet answered,

“This is not something you can talk about lightly.”

(I figured it was probably classified for the elves or something equivalent.)

In short, I couldn’t get an answer.

When I decided that, Number Six spoke.

“A taboo is something dangerous that could change the order of the world — that is how we define it.”

I tilted my head at the way she put it.

That phrasing made it sound as if the original meaning was different, and as I suspected she continued.

“The original meaning was different. It began with knowledge from another world that once nearly destroyed the world — that was the start of ‘taboo,’ and over the millennia that we elves watched the world it changed… what it means now and what it meant in the past are not the same.”

Her outlandish words almost made me blurt, “Are you sane?” but the explanation wasn’t finished, so I kept quiet and gestured for her to go on.

“And the reason we intervened in the Fullretos Empire was because of taboos from the past.”

I was surprised she mentioned the Empire, but if what she said was true it explained the extraordinary level of technology that set them apart from neighboring countries.

It was hard to accept at face value, but there was no reason to bring that story up here unless there was cause, and the fact that it had been a civilization clearly beyond the norm remained unchanged.

If so, her otherworldly words gained a little more credibility.

“Why tell me this story?”

“If we assume that you have acquired various kinds of knowledge through Imperial books and other sources, then your perspective would inevitably be that of that nation. So we judged that telling you everything would be most effective in persuading you.”

The phrase that first came to mind was “a straight-up fight,” but that probably wasn’t her intent.

By presenting sincerity up front she must have taken “me as a person” into account — a generous assessment for someone who had become a monster.

I felt both pleased and embarrassed, and decided to accept the compliment.

“We tried to root out the seeds of taboo from the Empire… but the result was dreadful. Everything was engulfed in flames; despite great sacrifice, we still could not eliminate it all.”

I felt a slight shock at being included in her words.

At the same time, the Empire had been destroyed — well, it had effectively blown itself up — and the elves were undeniably a major factor in the cause.

As a recruit still in training, I had no claims of avenging comrades or anything like that, but in retrospect the elves were positioned as enemies of the Empire.

(Maybe I got a little too close.)

I didn’t pause to reflect on my lack of sense; instead I shook my head, recalling the many beautiful naked forms of the women I had seen and stored in my mind.

After all, it was a story from two hundred years ago; even if they were former Imperial people, from the human point of view it was a past event.

And as a monster, it would be inappropriate for me to intervene.

So I thought it wasn’t my place to say anything.

Having resolved to live fully in the present, I needed their cooperation by any means necessary.

Specifically, I wanted to see more risqué scenes.

Or rather, if things went well, perhaps my wish to be with them could even be granted.

My intentions were obvious, yet this grim face was almost always a poker face.

(There was no fear of being found out. So then — ah, no. I’d forgotten the worst-case scenario.)

It was a classic lose-lose.

Since I didn’t know how much longer I could keep my human reason intact, I couldn’t stay long.

But if I thought about it the other way—

‘Short-term would be fine, right?’

I thought.

And what did I see — a wonderful prospect spread out before me!

Aside from the meals, I could read elven books in my spare time.

Their way of dealing with those brats wasn’t bad either, so it was certain I would continue associating with them.

That meant I could join the skirmishes at the river — in other words, I would have an excuse to touch the seemingly well-guarded body of Number Six.

And that wasn’t all.

From having peeked — or rather witnessed — them bathing in the river regularly, it went without saying that elves were clean, and they played in the river for fun and games.

I could take part in that too.

(Wait, do elves have a bathing custom?)

Since they went to the river often to bathe, it seemed possible — no, I hoped fervently that it was true.

Public baths would be even better.

I wanted the elf ladies to kindly teach me about bathing in detail.

Better yet, if there were something like an “elf bath” that I could join, that would be perfect.

“Um… are you listening?”

A frosty voice, like a splash of cold water, cut through my daydreaming.

Damn — my fantasies had run a little wild.

I curled my body apologetically and quietly handed Number Six a memo that read “Please say it again,” and with a sigh she began to tell me the parts I had missed.

But think about it.

With the Empire gone, I could no longer see all the things I should have seen — like gravure idols and such — not everything, but many things were no longer accessible.

So it wouldn’t be a sin for those women to give me a little service in their stead.

I wrapped up that line of thought and focused on Number Six’s story for the time being.

I asked her to skip the parts she had gone back over and to bring the story back to the course of the war with the Empire.

“We searched for the root of the Empire’s taboo. But we couldn’t find it. Everything ended that day — when the ground trembled and flames devoured the city.”

It was probably the explosion that happened in the Imperial capital.

Accident or not, I pictured the ruins of the capital that had become a desert and looked up at the ceiling.

Perhaps the weapon capable of causing an explosion on that scale was the “taboo” they were seeking.

But that was something I could no longer know.

“In the end we couldn’t identify the source — the ‘person who possessed knowledge from another world’ — nor find what he had created; we only increased the number of sacrifices in vain.”

Watching Number Six speak with a tragic expression, I almost reached out my hand.

“Why would the elves get involved?”

“Why would the elves start a war?”

I had my whispered questions from when I was still human answered.

But even so nothing would change, and since it was already history, hearing more wouldn’t give me any power to act.

“Only one thing. Some argue that, whether out of the madness of war or because some lost their sanity and proclaimed ‘explosions are art,’ that led to the scenes of that day — and some still agonize, wondering if our choice was wrong.”

Sorry — that actually was sane.

Among the Empire’s engineers there were indeed some who made bizarre weapons.

Knowing the fact that every so-called new weapon was made and then inevitably exploded as part of the routine, I felt like someone telling me a ridiculous family anecdote, and it made me uncomfortable.

How much easier would it be if I could just say, “Don’t worry about it”?

I looked back up at the ceiling and let Number Six’s words wash over me.

The more detailed the content, the more embarrassed I was to listen.

Internally tormented by this unexpected assault of shame from Number Six, I simply yearned for the story to end.

+++

# **99**

The shameful hell that had seemed like it would go on forever finally ended, and I pinched the cup of tea timidly offered by the elf who could still be called a girl and downed it in one gulp.

I set the cup—too small for me—on the floor, folded my arms, looked up at the ceiling, and tried to summarize Number Six’s statements in my head.

(An elf who viewed what was in my dwelling as dangerous. From the elves’ perspective, this “taboo” was knowledge from another world that once nearly destroyed the world, and they couldn’t ignore it. The history that led to war with the Empire two hundred years ago was also triggered by that taboo. The outcome looked much the same even if seen differently from what I knew. And —)

It was incomprehensible because she was saying the cause of that “taboo” might have originated with the elves themselves.

I thought, “Did she really have to be so honest?” but the image of pure Number Six was spectacularly shattered—perhaps they were trying to make me complicit by having me cooperate with full knowledge.

Or more accurately, the bit of innocence that had remained was gone.

From what I’d overheard of the conversations when I first came under their control, I’d guessed she was pretty competent.

So I should revise my perception.

Right, let’s get back to “taboo” and put it into a neat summary.

• Originally it indicated knowledge from another world, but now it denotes things so dangerous they could destroy the world.

• The war with the Empire was sparked by existing “otherworldly knowledge,” but they couldn’t identify its ultimate source.

• It’s highly possible that the origin of the “taboo” lay with the elves.

That was the gist.

As for the last point, Number Six said:

“It began when an elf, called a genius who believed absolutely in the existence of a ‘different world,’ opened that door, and afterward people were born who held memories of the otherworld.”

They hadn’t observed such phenomena before; it was only confirmed after he forced large-scale experiments and died. The most convincing theory now was that a distortion arose in the world and caused such things to happen.

After that, nations among humanity began to appear that had clearly different technological systems from their neighbors.

At first the elves only watched, unsure what it meant; but when the Empire — which had conquered the southern continent — expanded northward, they received shocking information.

There existed a person who called himself a “reincarnator,” the key figure behind the Empire’s rapid expansion.

Though famed as a genius inventor, he was also a strategist excellent at internal affairs and military matters; with his rise, the Empire’s dramatic leap became the famous tale known today as the “Record of the Fall of the Ranu Empire,” but behind it was someone possessing otherworldly knowledge.

And she said our Fullretos Empire likely had such a “reincarnator,” too.

Indeed, the Empire’s advanced technology, completely different from neighboring nations, would appear so, and given the circumstances it was a possibility worth considering.

If the elves had been able to identify the true source, I might have simply nodded along, but the circumstantial evidence was a bit thin.

After all, it was all in the past, so I didn’t need to be convinced to that degree, but having lived through that era, I still had feelings about the elves’ unreasonable intervention.

That said, I couldn’t say what I wanted to say; in the current situation, where I might be the object of the “taboo,” silence was the way to survive.

Sorry to my ancestors, but bearing the weight of the Empire alone — no, bearing it as a single beast — was too much.

Fortunately, the people had fled south and founded a new nation; having removed one obstacle, they would likely expand northward again.

They would make contact eventually, so I concluded I could just dump it on the future where I was gone and sighed deeply, lowering my gaze to face Number Six.

“Have you reached an answer?”

She asked quietly, but the question felt like pressure.

Cooperate with her and help erase the Empire’s traces — or keep my base and distance myself from the elves; that was the choice before me.

There was an option to “fulfill my duty as an Imperial soldier,” but, to be frank, it would gain me nothing, so there was no reason to choose it.

It came down to my feelings — though the reality that I couldn’t live long complicated things.

So it wasn’t surprising that my thoughts leaned toward “let’s use the elves for our benefit.”

I nodded slowly and quietly, then wrote on the memo and handed it to Number Six.

She read the paper that said, “I will cooperate — with conditions,” and nodded to show her acceptance.

Whether that was trust that I wouldn’t say anything strange, or compensation for taking my dwelling — I didn’t know which carried more weight — but Number Six nodded even before I stated the terms.

Maybe a cultural difference was at play, but she was remarkably composed when dealing with a fearsome monster like me.

I made two demands.

One: if the elves discovered similar facilities in the future, they would inform me of their locations.

Two: when they destroyed such facilities, they would take me along.

In short, I wanted them to find my “third person” for me.

Since Number Six had some reason to associate with someone who looked entirely like a monster, it wouldn’t be wrong to have them help extend my life, even a little.

She tilted her head at my conditions but didn’t press me on the reason; things proceeded more smoothly than I expected.

Probably there was some plausible reason I didn’t know about.

I chose the route of cooperating with the elves.

Now mattered more than then.

I believed the result would likely be better.

Still, housing me was problematic.

“Think carefully. This is the monster that devoured that ‘nightmare,’ you know?”

Said a male elf who looked like an attendant, and Number Six made an “ah” face.

That little absent-mindedness was endearing.

But putting me in an elven village was objectively impossible.

(I expected it too, but hearing it spelled out made me think, “Yeah, that’s impossible.”)

My dream of cultural exchange with the elves would have to wait.

Besides, it would be a race against time as long as I remained alive.

I let out a gruff “gyaa-gyaa” and a few people visibly backed away in fear.

I wasn’t so delicate as to be hurt, but children usually had more flexible thinking — or rather greater adaptability — than adults.

After some discussion, it was decided I would stay here for the night… but there weren’t many buildings I could enter, and with low ceilings I might accidentally break things, so I would spend the night outside.

Also, elves from other clans had been watching the grounds from around the perimeter, so sneaking out was impossible; I would spend the night bored outside.

With no bed to prepare, there was nothing to do but read, so they brought several books from the study.

They made a light source by conjuring a magic orb and covering it with a paper box.

Still, I felt a little guilty that someone had to come by every hour to refresh the spell.

+++

# **100**

Dawn broke.

That said, it was still dim, and about three hours had passed since the elves had last cast their light magic.

I thought I’d limited it enough so they wouldn’t have to stay up too late, but some had kept watch over me without sleeping, so my consideration was wasted.

Since I had already asked, there was nothing to be done; once the lights went out, I simply gazed at the starry sky in a daze.

I picked up the book I had set aside — no point straining to read in the dark — and continued where I had left off.

Though I still wasn’t fully accustomed to the elven language, the books they had brought were mostly for children, so reading them wasn’t difficult.

Still, I was rather astonished that, seeing my appearance, an elf had decided to hand me “children’s books” as reading material.

(Well, true, I’d only been studying Elvish for a short while… but whether this kind of content could really kill time is questionable.)

If only it had been a little more literary, it would have felt fitting — but a monster reading picture books at night made for a rather horror-like scene.

So I wrote on a memo, “Do you have any scholarly books?” and held it up toward the elves secretly keeping watch.

After a while, a female elf nervously brought three books, set them just within my reach, and hurried away.

(That was Number Two.)

She didn’t need to be that afraid, I thought, watching with my somewhat more precise observational skills, improved by accumulated data.

Opening the books, I found they were about magic, mathematics, and agriculture.

I immediately chose the one on magic.

(Agriculture would be useful too, but that’s for when I have a secure lifespan. Right now, this is the most useful knowledge.)

So I began reading enthusiastically… only to find I couldn’t understand it at all.

(This wasn’t an introductory book. It was clearly intermediate level or higher.)

I wanted to slam it shut and say, “Who could understand this?” but restrained myself.

For someone like me, a complete novice in magic, it was too much.

I briefly thought, “If I read an introductory book, maybe I could use magic,” but whether the elves would allow that was doubtful.

Number Six might approve, but convincing outsiders of the clan? Not a chance.

Not wanting to trouble them too much, I gave up.

Besides, if asked, “Could an Imperial even use magic?” I would have to shake my head.

Plenty would say, “You won’t know until you try,” but nearly every Imperial had already tried once.

In their early teens, everyone searched things like “magic, beginner’s guide,” and gave it a shot.

Of course, it might have been wrong, so perhaps elven texts could have led me to the right path.

But this world surely wouldn’t allow someone like me to wield magic.

Since I was aiming to coexist with the elves, I had to avoid putting them at a disadvantage.

(Except for Dame Oppai. She doesn’t count.)

In fact, that bust should serve as the sacrifice to smooth relations between me and the elves.

Honestly, I couldn’t think of any other effective use for that low-spec elf.

I trusted in the elves’ wise judgment.

Still, I kept trying to glean useful information from the magic book.

Nothing practical for me, but I realized one thing: thanks to my connection with the one who bound me with control magic, I had no trouble understanding the magical terminology.

I had no intention of being grateful to that hag, but it was a nice side effect I could use.

After a while, I gave a small growl — “gwaa” — and shut the book with a thud.

I’d finished one, but the principles and logic still eluded me.

I could understand the meaning of the words, but nothing more.

The obvious conclusion was, “I need to start with an introductory text.”

By then the sun was up, and the good smell of breakfast drifted over.

(I wonder what elves usually eat…)

Naturally, I hoped they’d prepared some for their guest.

If not, I’d have to go hunting — so it was safe to assume they had.

But I couldn’t just gorge myself.

Even if it was my first civilized meal in ages, to eat like a beast would ruin all the credibility I’d built up.

As the saying goes, “When in Rome, do as the Romans do.” I needed to respect elven manners.

That would certainly improve Number Six’s opinion of me.

She was someone worth befriending; raising my favorability with her could only help.

I flipped through the agriculture book too, but with too many unknown terms, I quickly closed it.

Lying back and looking at the sky, I heard footsteps; an elf male emerged from the manor and approached.

Sensing him with my hearing, I set the book aside and turned my gaze to signal him to speak.

“What about food?”

He asked, keeping a cautious distance, close enough to handle things if needed but no closer.

I pretended to think, then took out my memo pad and wrote in Elvish.

Pinching the finished paper between my fingers, I showed him:

“The same as yours, please.”

He looked doubtful, then returned to the manor.

(Oh? Did they not plan to serve me breakfast? Or did they just not expect me to want the same?)

‘Well, either way,’

I thought, waiting for elven breakfast.

Curious about what they normally ate, I was a little excited.

After about fifteen minutes, they brought out bread, soup, and salad.

(…Seriously? It’s so normal I don’t know how to react.)

Maybe the taste was special — so I tried half of the tiny piece of bread that for me was just a bite.

It had a chewy texture, better than what I remembered of bread.

But it was too small.

I couldn’t enjoy the texture fully; overall, it was “about what I expected” — both as good as I hoped and disappointing.

Still, it was something I would have enjoyed back when I was human.

Next, the soup: seasoned lightly with stock from dried vegetables and mushrooms, plus salt.

With no spoon fitting my big hands, I drank straight from the bowl, but didn’t spill.

As breakfast, it was perfectly fine — but again, the portion was tiny.

I could have swallowed it in one go.

Finally, the salad: just washed and cut raw vegetables.

Also bite-sized for me. From this, I learned elves were light eaters.

(Oh… maybe when I asked for “the same,” they gave me the same amount. That explained his look earlier.)

I realized what his reaction meant.

Back in training days, even as a human, I could have eaten double that.

Now, in this body, it was more like a snack.

If my grasp of Elvish had been better, maybe I could have avoided the misunderstanding.

I finished the breakfast, but while it was my first civilized meal in a while, the portion was definitely too little.

Maybe I should ask for seconds — just to see how the elves would react.

+++

# **101**

“It seems you’ve taken a liking to our food.”

In the end I asked for seconds, and Number Six actually came over just to say that line.

I’d been a little nervous, wondering, “Do I seem shameless?” but she smiled, so it was probably fine.

From the Natural Harmony Committee’s perspective, this could even be seen as a discovery that might become a foothold for coexistence between a monster like me and them, so there’d be no reason for hard feelings.

Still, between the fruit and the meals, I wondered if they were getting the impression that I often demanded food.

I worried, “Will they think I’m the glutton character?” but if that happened I could use it for camouflage in various ways; even so, image mattered.

(A monster that looks like a vicious horror-game boss but is secretly a glutton… no, if I rebrand it as being a “gourmet,” that might sound more like a strong, refined type.)

Even without that, the gap with my appearance could add approachability, and if things went well, it might even open a route to cultural exchange.

Both approaches had merits, and I struggled to decide how to handle this going forward.

I sipped the slightly larger helping of soup, and mushrooms far too small for my body went down my throat without much chewing.

I had a new thought: ingredients weren’t enjoyable unless they were large enough.

Then came a much bigger loaf of bread than before as my seconds.

I tore off half of it and stuffed it into my mouth, savoring the gentle sweetness that spread and the pleasantly chewy texture.

That texture only existed when a human hand had been involved.

“This texture is fun. I could get addicted to it.”

I wrote something like that on a slip of paper and showed it to Number Six, and she nodded as if to say, “I see.”

Frankly, after a life of mostly meat and fish, having a staple like bread was quietly precious.

I liked it enough to suggest bartering my catches for more.

“It changes this much just by changing the size,” I thought, and felt my cheeks loosen a little at the discovery.

Now that breakfast was over, it was time to talk with Number Six.

Losing my base would honestly hurt, but I had nodded for unavoidable reasons.

Still, this matter was, in short, a “favor” I was doing.

Even with conditions, I had accepted the elves’ proposal.

So next it was their turn to accept mine.

That said, it was still early to make the ask, but thinking “nothing ventured, nothing gained,” I let my pen run across the memo.

“…You want to use magic?”

Reading the memo, Number Six pretended to think for a moment.

No matter her personal principles, I didn’t expect this to be accepted this time.

By proposing it in advance, I’d earn the trust to match and obtain magical know-how.

If it didn’t work, so be it — but if an elven magic expert taught me, there should be a chance.

“Understood. If you’re fine with me as your teacher.”

I froze for a moment at the unexpected consent.

(…From a positional standpoint, that seemed impossible. Does Number Six harbor rather dangerous ideas? Or does she simply think there’s no chance I’ll succeed?)

A bit surprised, I quickly wrote “Are you sure?” on the memo and showed it, and she nodded casually with a simple, “It’s fine.”

Fundamentally, there was still the possibility that “I can’t use magic,” so I asked about that; by elven standards there were no cases of being unable to use it, so honestly she couldn’t say.

So whether I could use it or not would only be known by trying, and it sounded like Number Six had accepted half out of curiosity.

“Since it’s a rare opportunity, may I record the process of you learning to use magic?”

I had no reason to object, so I agreed immediately.

If anything, objectively checking the progress might speed up my mastery.

Compared to the benefit of being able to use magic, a little information leakage was trivial.

Having it on record wouldn’t hurt me, and since there were no others of my kind, problems were unlikely.

For a final check, I showed a paper asking, “No issue with your position?” and she tilted her head as if to say, “Why would that matter?”

I’d overthought it; apparently it was just my needless worry.

Buoyed by the possibility that I might be able to use magic, I hurried through the rest of breakfast and, in high spirits, headed toward the dojo.

I figured, “If we’re gonna practice, it should be over there,” but Number Six stopped me, saying here was fine too.

Apparently I’d gotten carried away.

She went to fetch teaching materials; I pretended not to hear her murmur, “Sooner or later, anyway,” and waited quietly on the spot.

So I waited an hour — which felt a bit long, but perhaps that was the time sense of a long-lived race.

Number Six still didn’t appear, but then, she was a big shot here.

She couldn’t spend all her time on me.

Just then, a man came over carrying a book.

“Here,” he said, and when he got close enough, tossed it to me.

“She says to read that first.”

With only that, he left.

It was clear no one besides Number Six welcomed me.

(Or rather, that was normal…)

The Harmony Committee really was an exception.

More importantly — the magic book.

I didn’t expect to “snap” into being able to use magic, but I wanted to at least get the basics in shape before she returned.

At least that much should be doable — or so I thought.

(No, really — writing about “feel it intuitively” doesn’t help.)

It was definitely an introductory book.

But the part about “sensing magic power” was fatally nonsensical.

Especially the line, “When you feel X in your body, that is mana,” made my brain refuse to comprehend.

It felt like being scammed by some spiritualist con.

(I’m completely stuck at the door. And I can’t see any path forward.)

I recalled a line from an action movie: “Don’t think. Feel.”

“Even if you tell me to feel what can’t be seen…” I grumbled, then tried to psych myself up and focus — but I couldn’t sense this mana at all.

Meditating as the text instructed, same result, of course.

And since it ended like that, I started trying my own methods.

Clearly, I needed someone who could use magic to teach me hands-on.

Hands-on enough to press close until I could feel that so-called mana — maybe even some skin-to-skin contact?

(As a former Imperial with no ties to magic, getting through this wall alone seemed difficult.)

With no moves left, I decided to wait quietly for Number Six.

As I looked forward to being tormented by the forbidden teacher-student dynamic, I realized something.

(Oh — it’s about time to head to the river… so what happens now?)

Would we have a classroom session by the river with the kids?

It wouldn’t look great for an adult (monster) to take lessons among children, but I wasn’t in a position to complain.

When it came to magic, I was a novice below grade school level.

“Learning among the kids suits me,” I mocked myself — and then a revelation descended on me.

(Classes with the kids at the river — which means I can team up with those little punks… I mean, my allies!)

In other words, it would be possible to legally get Number Six out of her undergarments.

The moment I arrived at that truth, I stood up from the nonexistent seat.

+++

# **102**

“I’d appreciate it if you didn’t move around.”

Right after I stood, I heard the voice of the elf who had been watching me.

It seemed they wanted me to stay put here, but that would do nothing to stop my desires.

That said, charging in and whiffing would be bad.

So I lowered my hips again, cranked my fighting spirit and lecherous will to full, and used every sense to locate Number Six.

After a short focus, I concluded Number Six was still inside the manor, and decided to wait here a bit longer.

I supposed I was getting less and less human, but I was already a monster, so too late now.

For the record, I confirmed her presence because Dame Oppai was calling her name in a pitiful voice.

From the clinging plea of, “Please, Lady Rusheru! Anything but that!” I inferred she’d messed something up again.

If it wasn’t time yet, there was no need to rush.

I sat stolidly and picked up the beginner’s manual again.

I had already crammed in a certain amount of knowledge while pretending to be under control magic.

The problem was not knowing whether that knowledge actually covered the basics.

Now, by reading this “Guidebook for Magic Beginners,” I seemed to have solid basics.

Even so, I couldn’t sense any sign I could use magic; I couldn’t even grasp a toehold on the required “interference with mana,” and I was tripping over the prior step of “sensing mana.”

At this point, it felt impossible to use magic on my own; with nothing else to do, I resigned myself to rereading the manual.

I kept half an ear on the manor while flipping pages, but little sank in.

No wonder — things had been oddly hectic inside for a while.

From the voices, someone was apparently “coming,” which seemed to be the cause.

(I see. My being here brought out a village bigwig.)

That meant Number Six, presumed to be the local representative, had to deal with them, so I shouldn’t expect a trip to the river with the kids.

The only question was whether that bigwig would come to me or not… and the voices made it clear they absolutely would.

“Ugh, hassle,” I yawned wide and struck a suitably welcoming pose for a reluctant visitor.

In other words, a “see, I’m diligently studying” vibe.

(Behold this aura of intellect. You came to nitpick, I’m sure… but know that cheap tricks won’t work on me.)

Except I was reading a children’s primer.

If it were the intermediate-looking book from yesterday, I might still look cool, but I’d already returned those; this was all I had.

Anyway, I waited like that… I could tell they were coming, but they were painfully slow.

Judging from surrounding voices, the one coming to see me was quite elderly.

(Ah, that. The classic elder — a big shot is making an entrance.)

With that guess, the scent of trouble thickened, but there was no running now.

More than anything, “learning magic” was on offer — unless the risk outweighed that, I wasn’t backing down.

After a bit, an old man appeared in the corridor leading to the garden, accompanied by Number Six and two elf maids; I slowly closed my book and raised my face.

He looked younger than I expected, with long white hair.

But in his hand he held the scabbard of a longsword with at least a one-meter blade.

“This the one?”

At the old man’s chin-pointed words, Number Six nodded quietly.

You could call it aura, perhaps?

Even I felt the pressure radiating from this old man.

(It’s not my imagination. This geezer is no ordinary one. This feeling is… the same kind? That strong?)

I watched him without letting a single movement slip by, waiting for him to approach.

He snorted at my vigilance, stepped into the garden — and spectacularly missed the step and fell.

“Guh—! Ow, ow, ow… my back, my back again…!”

As I stared, dumbfounded, the two maids rushed to him.

They tried to help him up from either side, and I saw his face clearly at that moment.

The angle hid it from the nearby maids and from Number Six behind him, but from my distance I had a perfect view of his profile.

(…This guy.)

Both of the old man’s hands were firmly grabbing the maids’ bottoms.

In fact, he was kneading them.

Taking advantage of the commotion, he was kneading both their butts quite thoroughly.

One look at that slovenly grin and you knew — it was absolutely on purpose.

An unmistakably deliberate harassment move.

Then he slid the hands that had seized their rears as if stroking, wrapped their waists, and slowly stood.

(Is he… showing off!?)

The fact that those around him tolerated it shocked me even more.

In other words, this dirty old man occupied a position where blatant harassment was allowed.

“I can’t let my guard down,” I warned myself.

Who could have predicted we were the same kind — in that sense?

At least Number Six wasn’t the victim; if she left the maids to handle him out of caution toward this lech, then kudos to her.

“Phew, forgive me. I put in a bit too much spirit.”

Cackling, he took up the fallen sword from a maid and walked toward me.

From that setup, I half-expected him to go one step further and grope their chests, but since he didn’t — this lech was clearly an ass man.

And I, needless to say, was a breast man.

I loved butts too, of course, but my scales tipped decisively; I doubted this old man and I would ever see eye to eye.

That farcical interlude made bracing for whatever this lecher wanted seem a bit silly, but that didn’t mean I would underestimate him.

Because an ominous feeling hadn’t left me since earlier.

(Because of that…)

My gaze was drawn to the sword in his hand.

At a glance, it was a slightly long longsword in an ornate scabbard.

But I could tell, whether I liked it or not.

That thing was dangerous.

Ever since it came into view, I couldn’t stop fixating on it.

With each step the old man took toward me, my alertness ratcheted up.

He was holding the scabbard now, but if his other hand reached the hilt, I honestly wasn’t sure I could remain still.

I felt tingling air cling to my skin and raised a hand to signal, “Don’t come any closer.”

“Oh? Better than a beast — you do notice.”

So saying, the old man stopped, drove the scabbard into the ground, and gripped the hilt.

Seeing that, I flung the book aside and shifted into a fighting stance.

“Elder Schvard! I did not authorize anything of the sort!”

Number Six raised her voice, but the old man, called Schvard, acted like he didn’t hear her and drew the blade.

I looked at the naked steel and thought:

(My hunch was right. Also — what is that? Just looking at it gives off a pounding, awful unease.)

This old man was likely extremely skilled.

Then I realized something.

(Huh? Schvard… that name…)

I’d heard it.

And if I recalled right — yes, it was the only warrior’s name that came up when I brought Dame Oppai back to my base and tried to pry out elven inside info.

Even Dame Oppai knew the name — and the strongest elf stood before me.

“I suppose I should give my name, monster. I am Schvard Corner. I failed to cut the ‘Nightmare’ a bit, and I’m worried my edge has dulled. So, since you ate my prey, I felt like cutting you a little. Care to indulge me to that extent?”

Pointing at the forest with his free hand, the old man smiled only with his mouth.

+++

# **103**

In the end, I had no choice but to go along with the old man.

Why?

Simple — because Number Six yielded.

I wanted to ask, “What about my own will?” but it seemed the matter had been elevated to something elves as a whole couldn’t refuse, and Number Six bowed her head.

“I’ve been swinging a sword for, what, three hundred and fifty years now? Somewhere along the way they started calling me ‘Sword Saint.’ I was wildly popular back then.”

As we walked to the designated open field, the old man talked.

I wasn’t interested in an old man’s yarns, but if he’d lived over 350 years, he must have fought in the war with the Empire.

I figured tales from that time might be worth hearing.

“‘Both hands full of flowers’? Hah, I had bouquets. Back then you could squeeze a chest or stroke a rump and they’d just squeal — no slaps flying. It was the perfect era.”

But no matter how long I waited, all I heard was his bragging.

And since it was mostly lechery, listening made me envious… no, outrageous.

“Still, in the end I never advanced beyond relying on this thing… no, that was the conclusion I reached. However much I swung a blade, I realized I’d grow far stronger by leaning on its power.”

Even so, when I kept listening, he shifted to hard-luck stories.

He ignored my hope for “Anything about the Empire yet?” and we neared what looked like the chosen clearing.

Tapping the nasty-looking sword still in its scabbard, the old man glanced back at me.

“Then an anomaly showed up that this wouldn’t work on. The ‘Nightmare.’”

“And? Are you going to tell me what that is?”

Since I was walking with baggage, I wrote in my memo as we moved and showed it to him.

Perhaps because my letters got wobbly, it took him a moment, then he answered happily.

“Oh, this is what they call a ‘Spirit Sword.’ In other lands they say ‘Holy Sword’… but this is the original. Nowadays the clergy use them like props and it’s pitiful… but in my hands it’s a different beast.”

I hadn’t thought it was just a magic sword; apparently it was something else entirely.

I’d never seen or heard the term “Spirit Sword.”

I had heard of holy swords; maybe once they passed into human hands, their treatment changed over time.

The problem was, I knew nothing of its power.

It was undoubtedly some magical thing.

Frankly, I could no longer hide my wariness toward a sword that radiated such abnormal pressure.

“So, I have to confirm whether this works on you. I’m venerated as a guardian of the elves, you see? If I don’t check, it causes problems.”

The logic I understood.

Being dragged into it wasn’t fun, but the elder was trying to fulfill his duty properly.

As a former Imperial soldier, if he invoked duty, brushing him off felt wrong.

Accepting why Number Six had to yield, I decided to treat this as earning another favor.

“Here should do, eh?”

The elder turned toward me and, in the same motion, yanked the Spirit Sword free and tossed the scabbard away.

I set my bundle down by Number Six and stepped forward to close the distance.

(Ugh… maybe I rushed this…)

As I approached, the Spirit Sword began to glow.

Small lights drifted around its wielder, and a dazzling old man appeared.

The problem was that the pressure I’d been feeling swelled even more.

“Then here I come.”

I braced — maybe I should assume Emperor-class — and in that instant the old man vanished.

I knew at once it was high-speed movement, and the slash released in that split-second of opening targeted my neck with perfect precision.

Because I had taken a stance that lowered my head position, an upward cut could now reach.

He went for that, but I caught his position from the sound of his feet braking out of the dash and barely dodged.

Of course I didn’t stop at just dodging.

I arched back hard and, backflipping away, whipped a tail strike.

I slammed it into the old man’s exposed side — but the “direct hit” felt like smacking a feather duvet.

(Barrier magic!)

I couldn’t think of anything else on the spot — and it was probably right.

The Spirit Sword came at me again as I landed.

He was skilled at aerial control; I parried the onrushing blade with a fist and was about to counter — when he fired magic.

He released one hand from the swing, and before my counter could land, compressed air smashed into my face.

My upper body snapped back, my arm cut empty air — and the Spirit Sword crashed in.

I used the recoil, letting my body keep arcing back, and drove a kick into the sword-swinging elder.

Again that muffled “foomph” sensation.

A nasty defensive spell — but it couldn’t fully kill the kick’s force, and Elder Schvard was blasted far to my right.

Not that he went flying for free; a thin red line opened on my right arm and bled.

Since I had the chance, I tried something I’d wanted to do.

I bellowed “Gah!” for show and flexed my right arm.

Feigning that my monstrous regeneration had clamped the blood off with muscle, I wiped a thumb through it and licked it.

Blood had already stopped from the cut, and I crooked a finger — “come on, then” — but the old man was already charging me.

“Dammit, read the room!” I cursed inwardly, and threw a shot as he leapt into my range — but the instant my hand brushed him, his figure blinked out.

(Afterimage!?)

No, just plain magic.

You could fool the eyes, but not hearing or smell.

His approach — an illusion or light refraction — was neat, but I had his position.

I wasn’t about to fight in his range out of kindness; I fixed him squarely ahead, took one step back with a grin — and an explosion went off at the tip of my nose.

In other words, he blasted my snout with explosive magic.

The boom jammed my auditory tracking; for a moment flame stole sight and smell.

In that instant he closed in, and the Spirit Sword emerged from the fire.

(I knew that was coming.)

The Spirit Sword’s pressure was overwhelming.

Once you got used to it, you couldn’t help but tell where it was coming from.

So I pinpointed it, grabbed for the arm beyond the flames, and tried to end it.

But another explosion.

He forced a course correction; I grabbed air.

Staggering but landing on his feet, Elder Schvard lowered his blade.

“Enough. That’s enough.”

With a wave, the old man went to fetch the discarded scabbard.

His tidy exit made me suspicious, but he sheathed the sword with a “I’ve done what I needed to” and left.

I’d just started to get into it, so it felt incomplete — but I had a rough read on the elves’ top combat power.

(Threat level: below Emperor. The Spirit Sword is dangerous, but if it doesn’t hit, it’s nothing. And the wielder’s an old body… skilled, but age drags him down.)

If we’d kept going, he would definitely have bottomed out in stamina.

He probably stopped to avoid that — I should call it crafty.

I couldn’t completely read his true strength — but that went both ways.

I never once attacked first.

He couldn’t have measured my combat power like this.

(Still, that Spirit Sword…)

A weapon that not only cut me but might even slice me clean through.

Maybe it was what caused that “tank cut in half” I saw on the news.

Number Six hurried over and checked my wound, but there was no trace of it, and no burns from the fire either.

I just wrote “That was pretty fun” on a scrap and showed it, and the men standing behind her gave dry laughs.

Honestly, despite the mortal danger, I’d enjoyed it.

It was a little scary how well I was adapting to this body — but for now, I’d think about how to have this debt paid back.

+++

# **104 - A Sword Saint’s Grumblings**

I didn’t wanna do this.

Honestly? I was terrified.

He killed the “Nightmare” and ate it? Are you stupid.

Even if elves call it our natural enemy because magic doesn’t work on it, even with that discount it was one nasty monster, okay?

I’m the one who actually fought it, and no joke, magic doesn’t work on that thing.

I thought, “They must’ve exaggerated the report,” went in, and nearly died.

Write the report accurately for once.

Not “effects extremely faint,” but “doesn’t do squat,” okay… seriously, I nearly died.

Even without that immunity, it’s a pretty troublesome monster.

And the report said he one-sidedly killed and ate it, right?

And you wanted me to go fight him?

Yeah yeah, I don’t have veto power, I get it.

I don’t wanna be the elves’ guardian anymore.

Also, how long were you planning to make a 388-year-old geezer work?

Because I was clan chief you’d think— fine, it’s my fault I slacked on training a successor.

Couldn’t be helped, could it!?

Only the chosen can wield the Spirit Sword!

Ah— yes… yes, I did limit “chosen” to cute girls… I’m truly sorry.

Wait, my wounds aren’t healed yet, and my back still hurts… okay, fine, they healed ages ago. I lied because the nurse girl’s butt was perfection.

Couldn’t be helped, right?

She said she’s a fan who loves my biography!

She took such devoted care of me, and even when I patted and squeezed she chalked it up to accidents so I had a field day— okay, don’t tell on me!

I’ll go, I’ll have a proper bout!

A report?

Yeah, yeah, I’ll file it… as long as I do it properly, right?

Sigh— back in the day they called me “big bro” and looked up to me… I’m heartbroken.

Ah, yes, I’m going now— right now!

Man… isn’t there any man out there who can take my place?

So anyway, they shooed me out like this and— what was it again?

Right, Argos. I ended up going to slice him a little.

He was captured with control magic and they studied him, right?

Naturally, that info— wait, you don’t have it?

No no no, that’s crazy.

Why not?

Oh, Argos burned it, and nothing remains?

Not by accident?

He actually went back and burned the lab?

…He’s a lot smarter than I was told, huh?

Ahh, so that’s why the Forshna girl is fixated on him— makes sense.

That clan’s… well, unlucky or complicated— if they mesh in a good way, fine.

So— even if you don’t have lab records, you did bring some intel, yes?

Mm-hmm, credit to my own clan, you didn’t miss a beat.

Hmm… was this doable?

I’m impressed you even got another country’s battle records, but… what’s this “even low-grade magic swords can hardly scratch it” supposed to mean?

Oh, literally that? So it’s that tough.

Then hit it with magic— oh, magic didn’t work either.

But that’s human magic, right?

Oh, there was someone who could use third-tier-equivalent spells?

Hoh— so humans have those too… wait, he punched it and it was unscathed?

Sorry, that makes no sense— the details are “as written”?

Yeah, that makes less sense.

Ah— so because magic doesn’t work well on him either, this becomes “a job for me,” huh?

Don’t just say “that’s right.”

For the record, the Spirit Sword’s effect drops hard against targets that ignore magic, okay?

And you’re foisting this dangerous beast on a 388-year-old?

Sure, I admit I’m “strongest in the elven village” right now.

But that’s only with this sword.

And I’ve been declining for decades… compared to my prime it’s tear-worthy.

You wanna hear about that?

You do?

Back in the war with the Empire I was on a tear— those “tanks,” right?

The moving steel boxes with cannons.

I cleaved one in two with a single stroke— the Imperials panicked like crazy.

Know what the Empire used to call me then?

Ehh, you want me to say it?

Really?

I wanted to be the one to say it…

Ah, time’s up— mm, thanks for the escort.

Ugh, I don’t wanna go.

So I trudged over, exchanged a few words to pass through, and the guy’s reading a book.

A beginner’s manual on magic, no less.

I wanted to say, “Come on, seriously?” but then ran into a bigger problem.

He’s wrong.

He sat there unfazed even though I was blasting him with the Spirit Sword’s pressure full on.

No no no— this was, like, an anti-monster special, okay?

Why was he calm under that pressure?

Normally you frenzy or run.

Why were you calmly sitting there?

Was he dense?

Or just so monstrous he can tank it?

Ugh… I wanted to do this even less.

So I faked an “accident” to buy time.

And, while I was at it, I checked the young lady’s backside— mm, youth is fine.

I tried to get him to drop his guard by showing weakness… but he didn’t. To not fall for my Oscar-worthy acting— not bad.

So I kicked things off from the high ground.

I am the Sword Saint… the “guardian of the elves,” so I have to do it.

I know, so Miss, stop glaring at me!

As expected, he didn’t flinch at my boss aura.

Actually, his eyes were glued to the Spirit Sword.

Monster or not, he was very wary of this thing.

Sigh— hard to fight like this.

He was more wary of the sword than of me.

We couldn’t brawl there, so I changed venues.

I managed to talk the young lady around… honestly, I wish she hadn’t yielded.

On the way, I tried various topics to coax him to relax, but his gaze stayed on the Spirit Sword.

How was I supposed to cut someone who’s this on guard?

So I just laid it out.

And spilled my own objective while I was at it.

And that fired him up.

I shouldn’t have said anything.

At that point I decided, “Gotta land one by a cheap trick.”

The opener: max out the Spirit Sword’s buff, use an illusion to muddy my initial movement, slash-and-run— except he reacted clean and dodged.

Expecting a counter, I nailed his face with a snap wind-blast, but he used the recoil and smacked my flank with his tail.

“Your tail stretches?!”

I spat at the miscalculated length as I blew away.

Ah— I jumped, not “got blown away,” right— important distinction.

With barrier magic up I shouldn’t have taken damage, but even a brush told me how sharp that strike was.

But leaping in that big body was a bad move, let me say.

With magic, changing course midair was easy.

I planned to punish the landing, but he parried my blade with a fist and even went for a counter.

“What kind of reaction speed is that?!”

I snapped and blasted him with wind again.

His fist cut empty air; I took the chance and slashed, but the feel was off.

Then he psyched himself up and instantly “healed” the cut, wiggled his finger, and grinned “come at me.”

I admit, I got a little mad.

So I got a little serious.

Yeah, just a little— if I got really serious I could handle it, but that wasn’t the mission.

I closed in the same way as my opener and layered illusions.

Works great on monsters.

But the results were lousy.

The explosive spell into a strike— he’d pegged my position perfectly, and I nearly got grabbed— chilled my blood.

Thanks to that, I calmed down.

Anyway, I did cut him once.

The wound was smaller than I expected and healed right away, but a cut’s a cut.

That means I fulfilled my duty.

Therefore, no more need to fight.

Okay, stop! That’s the end of this bout!

And that’s how I turned and walked back.

Good thing I left with my back bared; we didn’t resume fighting, thank the stars.

You saw, right?

I landed a solid stroke, yeah?

You can add this to my list of exploits.

Evaluation?

Ahh… in my prime I might have managed.

Honestly, I don’t think I’d win one-on-one.

Right, thanks for the hard work.

I’m beat too, so I’m going home to rest, okay?

Oh, one more thing… I want you to confirm something about that monster— he’s not the type that gains the abilities of those he eats, right?

Yeah, like the “Gluttonous Chimera” in fairy tales— it acquires the powers of what it devours.

The feeling when I cut him— the resistance— well, it was like that, more or less, with variations in intensity but unmistakable.

“What” was it like? The Nightmare, obviously.

I’ll ask one last time.

That monster… it’s fine to leave him alone?

Not my problem if you do, got it?

+++

# **105**

We lost sight of the Sword Saint’s back as he left, and we headed back to the manor.

On the way, Number Six watched the scar on my arm with keen interest, but by the time we returned, not even a trace remained, and even I had to marvel at this body’s healing ability.

But letting her look so closely had been a mistake.

Number Six stared and stared so cutely that I forgot to cover it up.

I should have wrapped it with cloth or something to hide it, but once it healed, it was already too late.

The elves now knew about my healing ability, and they would realize that all that fighting spirit in the bout earlier had been meaningless.

“Well, maybe I don’t need to overthink it that much,” I told myself, trying not to dwell on the embarrassing blunder and instead hoping to see how they would make it up to me.

It would be best if they offered something on their own; if not, I would have to think about how far I could push my demands.

(Given who he is, it would be hard to keep the fight fee low. He was basically the strongest in the elven settlement; I couldn’t make reckless demands…)

If it was too cheap, it could be seen as belittling him; if it was too high, they obviously wouldn’t be pleased.

And if I set it high, it would imply I had felt my life was in danger, which would contradict how I had acted and could make it look like I had tried to deceive them.

Because this called for a perfect middle ground, I really wanted them to bring it up.

That was what I thought, but we returned to the manor without anyone saying anything, leaving me feeling very ill at ease.

As I was mulling over what to do, Number Six came over carrying a small bundle.

“We’re taking the children to the river. Would you like to come with us?”

The river event — at last, making its entrance.

My worries flew off somewhere for the moment.

All right, the Number Six Theater at arm’s length that I had dreamed of was about to begin.

So I thought — but there was also an adult elven woman with Number Six.

“My name is Meriare.”

She said with a graceful bow; she was the slender type, and after that she didn’t speak to me again.

It seemed this “Number Two” was a perfectly ordinary elf.

So I avoided getting involved carelessly and just plodded along while entertaining the kids all the way.

(Yeah, this was going to be a lecture.)
