I had observed them by the river many times, so I knew this was their outdoor study pattern.

The women carried more baggage than usual, so I guessed their teaching materials were inside.

By the way, the errand at my base would only begin after the men finished preparing, so it might take a few days.

“In that case, there should still be chances,” I thought, restoring my lowered spirits to normal.

Anyway, we reached the river while I lightly fended off the brats clambering on me and hanging from my tail.

I set down the little girl sitting on my bent tail and plucked the idiot standing triumphantly on my shoulder and dropped him to the ground.

In the open-air classroom that made use of nature, the children used suitably sized stones as chairs, and once everyone was seated, the two women began the lesson.

The kids, who were at the age where they wanted to play, grumbled, but knowing it was useless, they kept quiet and listened.

So the class began — and this time they had me as a guest.

So they decided to do a light review.

Summarized simply, it went something like this:

• The vastness and preciousness of nature, and its cycles

• Elves must never forget that they themselves are part of the above

• Botany (mainly the properties of plants used for food or medicine)

Those three were the main points.

Unfortunately, there seemed to be no discussion of magic, which made it a bit dull, but I listened sincerely to the part about edible plants available here.

However, among the things that could be gathered around here, none were said to have particularly good flavor, which disappointed me a little.

In the middle of the talk, one brat asked, “What about this guy?” — whether to count me, a monster, as part of nature.

I didn’t like his attitude, so I tapped his head with my tail.

“Monsters are part of nature too. But we know far too little about their ecology. With so little we understand, it’s very hard to get along with them.”

She wrapped up with the conclusion that we mustn’t recklessly make contact.

Those were Number Six’s words — and I hadn’t expected to hear something so sensible from someone in the Natural Harmony Committee.

It seemed she held a fairly realistic position within the organization.

(Hmm… perhaps that’s why she valued ties with a monster like me, one she could actually talk to.)

At any rate, it was a stroke of luck to learn that no one assumed unconditionally that “things will improve just because there’s a monster like me.”

After the lesson, I watched Number Six calm and scold the children (mostly boys) who wanted to play in the river, but since they didn’t approach the water, nothing happened; the brats’ scheme was neatly crushed.

And of course my expectations were neatly dashed too.

But tomorrow existed, so I withdrew quietly for today.

On the way back, I drew closer to Number Six to avoid the kids trying to play with me.

As I expected, the brats didn’t step within arm’s reach of Number Six, and couldn’t pester me.

Facing a serious teacher, they wouldn’t dare act out — my plan worked.

I showed a memo reading “It would be troublesome if you let your guard down too much,” and Number Six laughed as if amused.

“How was the lesson?”

I answered simply,

“The edible plants were useful information.”

The books I had were divided by regions that were too old, and hard to use.

In that respect, the explanations with real samples today were very clear, and the parts to eat and how to harvest them were truly “useful.”

The taste didn’t seem promising, so whether my diet would improve was another matter; still, simply having more ingredients was welcome.

After that sort of walk back, we parted from the children and returned to the manor — and found something that hadn’t been there when we left.

(…A stable?)

No — size aside, it was far too shoddy to call a stall.

It was basically just a roof and side walls.

I supposed it meant “sleep here,” but it was slapdash to a fault.

Well, it had been built in a short time, so I wasn’t about to nitpick, though it was hard not to think they could have done better.

Since I’d only use it a few days, spending more on it would be silly.

In other words, the workmanship reflected my value in the eyes of the average elf.

I checked just in case, and indeed it had been made for me to sleep in.

(…So I was to sleep on straw.)

Compared to the bed at my base, it was laughably crude.

I sat on the straw provided, but it was, unfortunately, insufficient to support this weight and rock-hard body.

When I asked for more, they said that was all they had.

Apparently there was very little livestock here, so that was the only straw they could spare.

With nothing to do, I lay in my “bed” and spaced out.

But that was camouflage; in reality I sharpened my senses and eavesdropped on conversations inside the manor.

From that, I learned one thing.

It seemed they would head to my base the day after tomorrow.

Preparation itself would be finished sometime tomorrow, but for scheduling reasons they would likely postpone to the next day.

Which meant that tomorrow I could finally participate in the Number Six Theater.

Like an elementary schooler before a field trip, I felt I might be too excited to sleep tonight.

That said, with the sun still high overhead, it was too early to sleep.

While I idled away time eating the lunch they brought, Number Six came over.

When I asked her business, she replied, “I promised I would teach you magic.”

I reflected on how I had been so focused on tomorrow that the promise had slipped my mind.

I also had to think about that Sword Saint geezer incident; even as a monster, I still had no shortage of things to worry about, and I smiled wryly inside.

(But my longed-for magic was right in front of me. Time to focus on that.)

If it went well, I would gain even more power.

I’d remembered that humans usually chanted when using magic; I couldn’t help but listen to the explanation with interest.

Apparently, with enough training, even humans could cast like elves without chant—but given their lifespans, few ever reached that level.

If they tried, most mages could “just spill” fire or water, but they’d burn out quickly that way.

So humans made chanting the basic method and stopped training for chantless casting—so the elves said.

# **108**

# **109**

After handing over the prey I had hunted, I began practicing magic once again.

Magical energy naturally regenerated over time, and although the rate could be improved slightly through training, the total amount of mana one possessed showed almost no change regardless of effort. Because of the principle that it always tried to return to its original state, training to increase it was mostly considered a waste of time.

I had been told that even if one made great efforts to raise their capacity from 100 to 101, neglecting practice for just three days would return it to its original level. Hearing that had completely killed my motivation.

There was even a record of someone who had trained for ten years only to lose all progress in three days, so nowadays no one bothered to train it anymore.

This was the same for every race, which was why humans, who had low mana capacity, regarded mages as “those blessed with talent.”

Because of all that, my options when it came to using magic were extremely limited. Either employ it for small, daily conveniences, or as a surprise attack to create an opening in combat—essentially two choices.

And so, I began my training in earnest to master that single strike.

(First, I need to give form to what’s in my head. Efficiency can come later.)

Because my goal was clear, I could actually enjoy the process of working toward it.

And with an excellent instructor, even someone without much talent could make progress. Naturally, I was advancing smoothly.

“I suspected as much… you’re more dexterous than you look.”

What I had done was simple: I used magic to “breathe fire.”

It wasn’t anything like a dragon’s breath—just a short burst of flame, like blowing out a puff of fire.

Still, that single attempt consumed about half my total mana.

Efficiency was essential, and I clearly needed to move on to the next stage.

I could now also “ignite the tip of my tail,” meaning I could trigger magic from almost any point on my body.

The problem was that it still took far too long.

About twenty seconds of buildup to breathe fire, and ten seconds to use magic through the tail.

That was already much faster than before, but it was still unusable in actual combat.

At the very least, it needed to activate instantly.

I exhaled slowly, focusing on steadying my breathing and restoring my mana.

Mana recovered best when left untouched.

So, if I simply stayed still in a natural state of rest, my small reserves would fully recover in less than an hour.

Mana deficiency symptoms generally appeared once a person’s reserves dropped below a certain threshold, so I deliberately pushed myself that far to learn my limit—a useful experiment.

For reference, in my case, symptoms appeared once I fell below ten percent, whereas for elves, the benchmark was closer to five percent.

Apparently, humans were about the same, leading to speculation that “body size might be a factor.”

“We forced you into an unwanted fight.”

That subtle sourness came from using a lean cut of meat.

Without saying “delicious,” I simply nodded in silent approval.

No words were necessary—my actions spoke for themselves.

“How is the taste?”

I held up a note that said simply, ‘Delicious,’ and kept eating quietly.

“Why do you imitate humans?”

‘Did I use the wrong etiquette?’ I wrote, partly to buy time while thinking of an answer.

‘According to books, “Culture is rooted in the land. When you visit a land, follow its customs.” It said that customs often have logical reasons—ignoring them can cause illness or worse. So, I follow them to stay safe.’

Tomorrow would bring new things to enjoy.

“I doubt I’ll sleep tonight,” I thought, like an excited child unable to rest before a school trip.

Still, since I hadn’t slept the night before, I figured I should rest while I could.

# **110**

Still, the fact that I was gradually becoming able to distinguish sounds more precisely filled me with a strange sense of progress.

Though, since that progress came from my monster instincts, I couldn’t help feeling a bit conflicted about it.

(If I had to guess, they’re probably bringing in tools meant to destroy my base or something like that…)

Judging by the sound when whatever it was hit the floor, it seemed fairly heavy.

I couldn’t help but feel a flicker of curiosity about what exactly they were preparing.

In other words, it had to be some kind of elven weapon—or something equivalent.

There was no doubt it was a magical device, and depending on its power, it could very well pose a threat to me.

It couldn’t possibly be something mundane like a digging machine, and realizing I’d get to witness the extent of elven magical technology gave me a strange sense of excitement.

Thinking about it that way, sacrificing my base might not be such a bad trade.

In any case, departure was scheduled for tomorrow, so I’d have to wait until then.

For today, I would devote my morning to magic training—and prepare myself for another Number Six performance.

But first, breakfast.

The joy of having delicious food prepared without lifting a finger was beyond words.

Until it arrived, I quietly focused on practicing mana control.

I’d realized after a full day of training yesterday that, as long as I didn’t actually expend mana, I could keep going for quite a long time.

That said, the mental fatigue from continuous concentration was real.

So, I planned to do control training for a while, then move on to practical exercises that involved casting, taking short breaks after using a certain amount of mana.

During recovery, normal activity wasn’t a problem, so it was best to handle daily routines during that time.

This time management made me feel a bit like one of those “no-life gamers” I’d seen in news reports—people who organized their entire day around arcade reservation slots.

I hadn’t sunk that far, of course, but the truth was that I was starved for entertainment.

Though training magic gave me a sense of enjoyment, it was undeniably necessary for my growth.

One might ask, “Do you really need to become stronger?” But considering there was still a third numbered one unaccounted for—someone potentially stronger than me—and remembering that “Spirit Sword” I’d seen yesterday, which could probably cleave me in half, the answer was clearly yes.

Still, what I could accomplish with magic was quite limited.

Even so, it remained an area with room for growth, and as a means of surprising opponents, it was perfectly suited to me.

The question was how to use it most effectively.

I still hadn’t found the answer.

The “breathing fire” trick I showed yesterday was basically just that—a trick.

Some monsters already breathed fire, so there was nothing new about it. At best, others would simply think, “Ah, this one breathes fire,” and move on.

But what if I used it differently?

Among humanoid monsters like goblins, there were rare individuals who could use magic.

Yet if I, a creature that looked so distinctly monstrous, were to use magic—it would be utterly unexpected.

From that, I arrived at one guiding principle:

“Use ordinary magic in extraordinary ways.”

Easier said than done.

I was still a novice; coming up with surprising applications wasn’t going to happen overnight.

So for now, I decided to continue practicing and refining, even if the purpose remained unclear for a while.

Since I already had a clear goal, there was no need to rush. I enjoyed my breakfast politely while savoring the uneasy stares of the elven woman nearby.

I hadn’t expected her expression to shift from shock to disgust in just one day.

(You could at least look a little impressed.)

Look at this impeccable table manner.

Never mind that there was no actual table, and that the “utensil” in my hand was a kitchen knife.

It was the sort of scene that would make a police officer pale and ask, “What crime scene is this?”

Since the knife was made for humans, the handle was small, forcing me to concentrate not to break it.

I’d spent a fair bit of time practicing just to handle it properly.

After finishing breakfast, I neatly gathered the dishes onto a tray and carried them back to the mansion myself.

Even if I was technically their guest due to circumstance, I preferred to act with self-reliance and proper manners.

There was still some time before we were to head to the river, so I resumed my magic training.

As I sat cross-legged, hands spread in a meditative pose, I sensed someone approaching.

There was only one elf who ever sought me out.

Sure enough, Number Six appeared before me.

“Our preparations are complete. We’ll be heading to your base now. Are you ready?”

A startled “Gah?” escaped my mouth before I could stop it.

‘Wasn’t that supposed to be tomorrow?’ I wrote.

“Our preparations finished sooner than expected.”

For a moment, I worried that maybe I had eaten too much of their food, but she added, “The men worked hard through the night,” and I imagined the elves laboring diligently to impress her—feeling both reassured and oddly disappointed.

Still, missing today’s Number Six performance was a genuine loss.

“I was looking forward to that,” I muttered, standing up and grabbing my things.

Well, time to witness the elves’ secret early.

And that secret, in a word, was a box.

Each was about the size of a portable clothing trunk—black, heavy, and radiating presence where it sat on the ground.

(Five in total, huh? I really hope they’re not just bombs…)

As a weapon, they were intriguing, but they didn’t exactly match the graceful image I had of elves.

I pointed at one of the boxes, curious to see how it worked.

“Don’t touch that,” an elf warned.

So much for nonverbal communication.

There was no explanation, and soon the elves finished packing the boxes into special backpacks and began walking.

They seemed quite heavy; the carriers were all well-built individuals.

Including armed guards wielding swords and bows, several porters, a few hooded figures who looked like mages, and Number Six herself, the total count came to twenty-four.

When I tried to move ahead and lead the way, I was told my position was at the rear.

“Not worried about being attacked from behind?” I wondered aloud.

Number Six’s calm answer: “We’d have no chance of winning against you anyway.”

Even without a written note, she understood me perfectly. If only the other elves could do the same.

And so, walking slowly to match their pace, we reached the river.

Thinking that, if not for the hardworking men, I might’ve been enjoying a peaceful morning by the water instead, I couldn’t help but glance at them with faint resentment.

The elves used magic to gracefully leap over the river, even with their heavy loads.

I waded through it with splashing steps, joining the back of the group on the other side.

Since this was an area I frequently passed through, monsters rarely approached.

I left vigilance to the elves and continued walking toward the base—training magic along the way.

+++

# **111**

Let me be blunt.

Slow—and boring.

It went without saying that “advancing while keeping watch on the surroundings,” perfectly normal in this great forest, slowed the party’s pace; but if you asked whether it was truly necessary, the answer was: not really.

That was only from my point of view. For the elves, this was the forest where their natural enemy had once lurked—bad enough to earn the name “Forest’s Nightmare.”

No matter how advantageous this lush terrain was for them, moving without caution simply wasn’t an option.

Because the area had been left alone for so long, even they didn’t know its current state; crossing the river and pushing forward like this demanded real courage and preparation.

On top of that, they were carrying heavy loads this time, so they also had to ensure the cargo’s safety—another reason to be extra careful.

Given those circumstances, I had no grounds to complain about the slowness. I might have said, “Leave the guarding and scouting to me,” but they had their own roles.

Carelessly taking those roles away could have unpredictable, negative consequences later.

They had their own way of doing things, and meddling would be unwise. So I accepted the situation.

As usual, I lumbered along at the rear, using the position to practice magic without worrying about eyes on me—until I sensed the malaise that came from dwindling mana and stopped to recover.

In other words, I was bored.

I let my gaze drift to the women’s backsides from time to time, but their outfits weren’t the type to show any lines—plain, functional clothes that prioritized ease of movement over sex appeal.

With nothing to look at and tired of matching the elves’ slow pace, I stared at their backs and mulled things over.

(I always imagined elves were forest naturals—leaping from tree to tree—but they’re just walking on the ground. Kind of a letdown. We’ll reach the base before nightfall at this pace, but it’ll probably be dark by then…)

Considering I was practically the master of this forest, did we really need to be this cautious?

Besides, I had already wiped out most of the monsters in this area, so moving more boldly shouldn’t be a problem.

As for why I cleared them out: obviously, because they might interfere with my surveillance work.

I didn’t want that line of questioning examined too closely, so I kept my mouth shut and followed along.

While I was zoning out, thinking, “This is dull,” I idly tracked a fluttering butterfly with my eyes—and noticed a female elf who had turned around was staring at me with a strange look.

The moment I noticed, she quickly averted her gaze and faced forward again.

Got you with the gap moe, didn’t I? I smirked inwardly.

That was how bored I was. Eventually enough time passed for my mana to recover, and I resumed training while walking.

Repeating that cycle, we ate preserved rations on the move at midday, took one break, then headed straight for our destination again.

The forest thickened, and it was dim even before sunset, when someone murmured:

“…It may be obvious, but with Argos here, monsters probably won’t come near us, will they?”

I swallowed the words, “You just noticed?” and checked everyone’s reactions.

Apparently, they’d been wondering why we hadn’t encountered a single monster; scouts and guards seemed to have been exchanging signals.

I hadn’t noticed a thing, which meant they were likely using hand signs; even that eluded me, which only proved how formidable elven coordination could be.

We paused a bit while I trained, and from the snippets I overheard, I gathered that “there were no enemies nearby.”

It seemed they decided to pick up the pace from here, moving at nearly double speed.

Thanks to that, by sundown we were close enough to spot the base.

So I told Number Six I’d break off to secure dinner alone.

Lighting my fingertip as a torch, I showed a note, slipped into the woods, and soon after the group reached the base, I returned with a deer in hand.

Leaving the bleeding and butchering to them, I went down into the underground part of the base and gathered what I needed.

(It was a good base, even if only for a short while.)

Feeling a twinge of sentiment, I began to stack the books I deemed necessary—then my hands stopped.

I hesitated over what to do with the adult magazines I’d collected.

But those could be found again in town.

There were more important things now than books.

Eyeing the empty spice containers, I stuffed the backpack with daily necessities like salt and towels.

Might as well make use of the cooler boxes, too.

I didn’t forget the spoils—the weapon and shield—but those, unfortunately, I could only carry by hand.

For the moment, I took just that much upstairs: saber and shield in hand, a large backpack with a shovel and iron griddle hooked to either side, and different-sized cooler boxes slung from each shoulder—an outfit that made no sense.

Naturally, the elves all recoiled when they saw me.

The reason was the sword and shield in my hands.

The saber’s grip was a bit small for me, so I wrapped unused cloth around it to make it fit; I’d done the same to the shield’s handle.

Ignoring someone’s mutter—“You use weapons?”—I set my load down. Judging by the smell, dinner would still take a while, so I handed over just the griddle and headed back underground.

Next on the list: bedding.

I’d stacked many layers to support my huge body, and I really didn’t want to make another round trip to town just for that.

Depending on the location of the next base, but in terms of distance, this spot was ideal.

Ideally, I wanted the next base not far from here; it would be foolish to bury the bed materials now.

So, after two trips up and down to bring all the bedding to the surface, I found myself drawn by the smell of meat on the griddle and ended up standing behind the woman cooking.

She let out a small “Eek,” so I must have startled her; I made sure to look only where I should and moved to the cooking area at ground level.

When I came back with dishes and such, an elf blurted, “Why do you even have that stuff?”

Fair point—if you visited a monster’s lair and found it stocked with tableware for guests, you’d react the same.

Even Number Six wore an exasperated look.

Thus, while everyone ate their portions of grilled meat, there I was—dipping mine in a tiny dish of salt. Clearly the odd one out.

“That’s just wrong!”

At last, one of them couldn’t take it and shouted.

“Do you want some salt?”

I offered the small dish, now flecked with meat juices. The man sagged to his knees and hands.

Apparently, he didn’t want what I’d used, so I took out the fresh salt container from my backpack and set it down gently.

Ignoring his muttered, “No, that’s not the point,” I salted another piece of meat and popped it into my mouth.

It didn’t pair especially well with the preserved rations on offer, but having more variety was a good thing.

Anyway, after dinner, I handed the tools to the elven woman who’d been grilling and pointed out where to wash the griddle.

Argos shows no mercy, even to guests.

Of course, I had the dishes washed too; I pointed with a “Gag-ga” at a man who happened to be nearby.

Satisfied that everything was in order, I heard Number Six call out to me.

“We’ll pitch the tents just before the underground entrance. Is that all right?”

So they wouldn’t start work immediately, but begin in the morning.

Even elves needed rest, and magic or not, working in the dark wasn’t efficient.

In that case, I hadn’t needed to haul the bedding up today. Chastising myself for rushing, I gave Number Six permission to set up.

As for me, “In that case,” I thought, and started carrying the bedding back underground.

Sleeping alongside a giant like me would be hard on elven hearts.

They wouldn’t need to stand watch as much, but rotating between underground and surface was still better than having “no escape route.”

After shuttling the bedding down and finishing the job, Number Six—waiting for me—posed a question.

“Do you use weapons?”

She pointed at the massive saber and the rectangular shield I’d taken from the Emperor.

Unfortunately, I hadn’t used them even once since obtaining them.

I could have answered, “I don’t,” but with the items sitting right there, that would be hard to sell.

So I held up a note that said only, “Still training.”

“Then where—and how—did you get those?”

That line of questioning gave me pause.

Now that she said it, it would be stranger not to wonder where and how those were made.

They were clearly sized for something other than humans.

Given their plain, somewhat crude construction, it wasn’t unreasonable to think I had made them.

But I didn’t have that kind of skill, and letting them think I did would cause problems later.

I knew I came off like a civilized poser, but being credited with real craftsmanship would complicate things.

With my current dexterity, making such items was out of the question anyway.

So I gave the safest—and truthful—answer: “Spoils of war.”

“In other words, you fought the monster that used them and took them?”

It stood to reason—only a monster would wield weapons that size, and if I called them spoils, the conclusion followed.

Which also meant: I had fought the monster that made those weapons and seized them.

…Ah. That might’ve been the wrong answer.

+++

# **112**

Let me put it plainly.

If they asked, “Did you take it from them?” I could only answer, “Yes,” and I hinted—ever so slightly—that I had “eliminated a dangerous threat.”

“If a monster can wield gear that large… was it an ogre?”

“A large orc.”

As she glanced at the note I showed her, looking thoughtful, I began writing a somewhat longer explanation.

When Number Six saw me start writing, she simply waited in silence for me to finish adding details.

“There were no orcs in this area. None to the east either—that much I confirmed. With such a huge stretch of land inhabited mostly by goblins, it was easy to accept that ‘this whole region is goblin territory,’ but the reality was different. Orcs had gathered in the southern part of the forest—so many that their number was nearing ten thousand. Ruling them from the top was an orc boss—wielder of those very weapons.”

Not wanting to keep her waiting too long, I tore off the page there and handed it to Number Six.

While she read, I continued writing the rest.

The pen had once felt too small for my large hands, making tiny letters a chore—but after this much practice, I had improved.

My Elven script should be getting better too. It might take time before anyone says, “Your handwriting is elegant for someone your size,” but that day wasn’t far off.

“The problem was that this orc horde had turned south, waging war on a human nation. If we let them be and allowed their sphere of control to keep expanding, the forest’s resources would eventually be exhausted. Even without that, conflict would become increasingly inevitable. So I crushed the head that held them together—while it could still be struck.”

Number Six’s face clouded as she read the next page I handed over.

“So this is what comes of leaving the forest to nature…”

As an elf, she must have had complicated feelings about the consequences of non-intervention.

In fact, Number Six’s expression was openly pained as she pondered.

“Normally the goblins and orcs would fight and keep each other’s numbers down, so things wouldn’t escalate like this. But this time, a unifier appeared on the orc side. We have knowledge of what happens when a unifying individual appears among goblins—but what happens when it’s orcs?”

Orcs didn’t multiply as fast as goblins, but in the right conditions they could still grow in number frighteningly fast.

Unlike goblins, however, orcs required far more specific conditions; in Imperial history they were rarely seen—uncommon unless created intentionally.

“So even for us elves, an unfavorable situation was beginning to take shape… that is what you mean.”

With that, the conversation ended.

I watched Number Six stand and leave, then considered sleeping underground—but a worry occurred to me.

(If I’m the only one sleeping below, they could always bury me alive.)

I didn’t think they would, but they certainly could. So that night, I stayed outside, training magic till morning.

Fortunately, I had slept normally the night before, so there was no problem.

Still, I had better keep a bit of distance from the elves sleeping on the surface.

It was only natural that nearly all of them were wary of me; if I stayed too close, they might not get proper rest—and that would hurt tomorrow’s work.

Thus the night passed quietly, and before I knew it, the sky had grown light.

I had trained with full focus, but I still couldn’t feel any visible improvement.

When I returned to the base, the elves were already bustling, lowering the devices they had brought into the underground level.

As one would expect, they handled them with the care of people moving precision instruments.

From my perspective, it was a height I could reach with a small hop and outstretched arms, but for the elves it was about as high as a two-story building.

They strapped each one with ropes so it wouldn’t drop, signaling between above and below as they eased it down.

One unit had apparently already been sent down; I could see three still above.

Catching sight of me, Number Six gave a small nod. I pointed and offered, “Want a hand?”

She waved lightly and answered, “We’re fine.”

They were treating them like delicate machinery; clearly, they didn’t want me handling them.

Waiting around was dull, so I headed out to hunt breakfast.

At their pace, just getting everything underground would take quite a while.

I should have time to eat.

So I secured some game, dressed it, and flowed straight into a solo grilled-meat party—when I felt the workers’ eyes on me from across the way.

(Even elves want meat after hard labor, huh…)

I limited my own portion and left some for them.

I’d once done short-term grunt work just to get a limited edition item, and I could still remember how amazing grilled meat tasted after that.

For the record, I used magic to ignite the fire this time.

Controlling a small flame had become easy enough for me.

At this rate, I’d soon have no need for that little fire-starter tool.

So I grilled and snacked at a leisurely pace while practicing magic, and without any trouble, the elves finished moving all the equipment underground and returned to the surface.

I beckoned them over and pointed at the griddle and the thinly sliced meat I’d saved.

“No, we’re not eating.”

One of the men said casually.

I tilted my head at him like I couldn’t believe it, but the women pleaded,

“Please let them rest.”

Not enough youth in these guys, I muttered inwardly—but then, elves lived three hundred years or more.

They also spent a long stretch looking young; it was obvious they weren’t the age they appeared. It was entirely possible they were in the middle-aged cohort—the sort who chose rest over meat.

In that case, I wouldn’t push. I grilled what was left and polished it off myself.

After cleaning up, I noticed they were about to start the devices, so I called for a halt.

“Did we forget something?”

At Number Six’s words, the work stopped.

Choosing my words carefully, I put pen to paper.

“I want to see what’s down there. I have a pretty good idea what it is, but I want to see with my own eyes what the elves deem ‘forbidden’—to decide whether it truly deserves such fear. Sorry for the trouble, but could you bring it up?”

Everyone frowned at that.

I said I could come along into the underground as well, but their expressions stayed uniformly opposed.

I thought persuasion might take time—but here Number Six relented.

Apparently, not everyone present had seen the spider-man failure, and she had judged that “everyone should see it at least once.”

There was also, it seemed, a hope that if I held “old Imperial values,” I would understand. Whether Number Six was being guileless or calculating in saying that so frankly was hard to judge.

With a word from the person in charge, the whole group began moving downstairs.

We were abandoning a base that—even if only a little—still had electrical power.

I had my reservations, but the small number of usable appliances meant its practical value wasn’t high, and with no guarantee how long the generator would last, we had to consider cutting it loose.

It was simply “a choice we would have made eventually,” made now.

I told myself that to make peace with it, but this was still the place where I had spent the longest stretch since taking on this form.

I couldn’t pretend I felt nothing. I found myself wandering the rooms without purpose, stretching my hand to touch things as if by reflex.

Since it was all going away anyway, I told them, “Take anything you want,” being generous. Everyone except the few sent to secure the spider-man failure began picking through the place.

They had been curious, as expected; most obviously, the bookcases were stripped bare.

…Hey. Which one of you took the adult magazines?

+++

# **113**

The pickings turned out to be more lucrative than expected, and some of the elves were still rummaging around for anything made of metal.

If memory served, the elves didn’t own any mines and relied almost entirely on imports for mineral resources.

Their metallurgical skill should have been on par with the Kingdom of Canaan, so as long as they had metal, they could melt it down and rework it.

Elves scavenging for junk—now that was a rare sight.

Number Six’s instruction—“Keep it reasonable”—snapped them out of it, but it was clearly hard to leave a treasure trove sitting right in front of them.

Even aside from Imperial technology, the raw materials you could pull from a single research facility were evidently tempting.

Time passed, and the team sent to fetch the spider-man failure returned, dragging the specimen with a rope looped around it.

They clearly didn’t want to touch it directly, but I wished they’d treat something this valuable a bit more carefully.

And so everyone came face-to-face with the spider-man failure.

It looked even more grotesque than the ones I knew.

The human upper half was obviously different, but like before, there was no body hair at all, and the dried, shriveled flesh resembled a mummy.

The spider portion hadn’t changed much; only the junction with the human part felt slightly off.

According to the elves who’d brought it, some versions had the junction reinforced with metal—textbook “test subject” aesthetics.

I stroked my chin, stepped closer, and examined the thing from several angles.

I still balked at the idea of eating it, but it was something I had to try.

(If this solves the problem, I’ll have to cut ties with the elves… but life comes first.)

I doubted I could resist the drowsiness that followed eating.

And as for what they might do to me if I kept eating this in front of them—I didn’t even want to imagine it.

“This is why it’s ‘forbidden.’ Do you understand? Human–monster fusion… I don’t know what possessed anyone to conceive such cruelty, but leaving this—”

Her speech froze as I snapped off a spider leg and popped it into my mouth.

Number Six stared at me with an unprintable expression, speechless; the surrounding elves recoiled in disgust.

As for me—I’d braced myself, chewed… and, as expected, there was no reaction.

Which only strengthened the likelihood that my hypothesis was correct: if the specimen wasn’t alive, eating it did nothing.

(So I really do have to find the third one.)

My shoulders slumped, and I spat the spider leg out.

“What… are you doing?”

Number Six ventured a cautious question. I held up a note with a single word: “Bad.”

“Seeing that, why did you think you should eat it?”

One of the elves pointed at the failure, trembling, and asked. I wrote with a straight face: “Rare item. Miss this chance and I’d never know the taste.”

“Is that truly the only reason?”

Number Six pressed, uneasy.

Apparently I now had a reputation for eating anything.

Not wanting trouble the next time food appeared, I wrote something more reassuring.

“You know I’ve been more careful about meals lately. You also know—because of the books I read—that I’ve been trying new ingredients. Insects are food in some cultures; I read that too. It’s a bug-type monster. I just tried it.”

She read the note and looked up at me.

I gave a simple nod.

Watching her exhale in relief, I felt a pang of guilt for worrying her.

“In any case, please avoid such sudden actions. You’ll startle us.”

I nodded meekly at the cute, mildly scolding Number Six.

Then my eyes drifted to the junction on the spider-man’s body, and something clicked.

(Come to think of it—the predecessor to the Genetically Enhanced Soldier Project was the Chimera Project, wasn’t it? The name suggests the spider-man was created by that. Which means he was a test subject enrolled before me. If those were experimental stages, then his criminal personality… no—perhaps he was a death-row inmate. That would explain it.)

That line of thought fits.

A vicious criminal, used as a test subject—one of the few accidental successes—awakened in this era from cryo-sleep.

(For my fellow enhanced soldiers, I assume the recruits were military. But if other Chimera Project prisoners emerge into the world, the damage could be serious.)

I wondered if that situation could be turned to our advantage—but shook my head. Convenient scenarios like that didn’t just present themselves.

At any rate, we were ready to destroy the underground facility.

I had achieved my goal; it was time to say goodbye to this base.

The location was excellent, which made it painful to leave, but what we were gaining in exchange wasn’t small either.

We made a final check of the devices, and the elves filed out from underground.

It took time for everyone to reach the surface, likely out of consideration for me as I gazed around the base one last time.

After Number Six emerged, I followed her up, then sat down and quietly awaited the farewell.

“Begin.”

At her signal, the casters focused their minds to activate the device.

Before I could sense mana, I would’ve had nothing more to say than a vague impression.

But now, I clearly felt the dense, tightly compressed mana pouring from their joined hands—so heavy that my own refined mana felt flimsy by comparison.

And yet, nothing happened.

I was about to wonder if something had failed—when I noticed the change.

A vibration.

The earth was shaking.

Yes, this was—

(An earthquake!? And a big one! They’re going to collapse the underground by reproducing a localized quake!)

If this could be scaled up, capturing cities would be easy.

The devices were probably single-use and costly—but even so, I had glimpsed one facet of the elves’ weaponry.

It wasn’t a direct attack, but strategically—paired with elven magic—it would be devastating for infrastructure.

On the other hand, against a single powerful individual like me, it wasn’t effective.

This wouldn’t be their only option; they must have judged that this fell below the threshold that would make me feel threatened.

Sitting solidly on the trembling ground, I offered a silent prayer for those sleeping below, who were surely being buried at this very moment.

Criminals or not, they had been citizens of the Empire.

A prayer for their rest was the least I could offer.

The quake subsided at last. Someone used magic to check below and reported that the underground had collapsed successfully.

Nothing would disturb their sleep now.

I stood and hefted my backpack.

I couldn’t carry anything as bulky as the bedding, so I left it behind.

I needed to find a new base quickly. I exhaled—when Number Six said something unexpected.

“If you don’t mind, could you stop by our settlement once more?”

The surrounding elves stared, wide-eyed.

“With all that gear… can you carry it by yourselves?” I asked.

She pointed at the piles of spoils—plenty of it metal.

(Right… it did take them a long time to haul it up from below.)

So that’s what that was, I realized with a far-off look.

When she suggested “We’ll give up on some of it,” I vetoed it and smiled. “You got permission—so take responsibility and bring everything back.”

My suggestion to make multiple trips earned a practical rejection:

“We won’t provide an escort.”

And so, I ended up carrying part of the haul.

The payment was a single meal—hardly a fair trade—but given my earlier act as the gluttonous type, I couldn’t exactly refuse now.

Truly, this elf missed nothing.

+++

# **114**

I couldn’t say I didn’t see this coming.

To be honest, things had only held together because a person in the extremely peculiar position of the Natural Harmony Committee happened to have clout.

You could tell just by looking: there were almost no monsters in or around the elves’ settlement.

That was one reason I hadn’t planned to stay long, but now it was too late to matter.

“What is the meaning of this…? You will explain, won’t you, Kashial Zesat?”

“But of course, Rusheru Forshna.”

What awaited us on our return from my base—the Imperial ruin—was an encirclement by fully armed warriors.

They’d even kept a careful distance and taken positions where we couldn’t see them—annoyingly thorough.

I tried to count their number, but somewhere past thirty, the combination of their stealth and my boredom killed my motivation.

Our male escorts each reached for their weapons, ready to fight at any moment, but being surrounded put us at a clear disadvantage.

We were just about to cross the river into the forest; in this darkness, spotting elves inside the trees was nearly impossible.

The terrain advantage was obviously theirs, and the man who seemed to be commanding them was currently glaring at Number Six.

I hadn’t been particularly on guard, so I’d neglected non-visual detection—and the spot just beyond the river was exactly where one lets their guard down. That was probably why the elves with us reacted late.

(Looks like they even used concealment magic against their own kin. If I recall, he’s some bigwig… but elves don’t look old, and they’re all good-looking, so it’s hard to tell who’s who.)

Especially the men.

With women, you could at least tell by certain physical traits, but with men it came down to muscle build or what they carried.

And it didn’t help that they all wore similar clothes and hairstyles.

“We prioritize the settlement’s safety above all.”

“And this is your conclusion? Barbaric doesn’t begin to cover it.”

“Ha! Barbaric, you say!”

The dignitary laughed with his arms spread.

Thanks to the magical lights, I could see them clearly—which also meant they could see us, the targets, just as well.

And my gaze was fixed on one very specific point.

“You would level arrows at someone you could speak with, without even trying words first—and call that not barbaric?”

Unlike usual, there was a sharp edge in Number Six’s voice.

Naturally so; being surrounded by armed elves was not a situation she could overlook.

“How strange! Could it be that no report reached your people?”

Their exchange went in one ear and out the other.

Because among the armed elves facing us was a female elf in bikini armor.

I’d been loitering so calmly because I was watching her.

(Unmistakably bikini armor. Size two… three doesn’t exist, surely. I never thought I’d see one in the wild.)

Impressed—and thinking she looked like nothing but an exhibitionist—I observed her gear.

The two bigwigs kept fencing with words, but that didn’t concern me in the slightest.

Since an elf was wearing it, it definitely had magical effects.

Likely something like drawing attention—aggro management—but more importantly, I wished Number Six would wear that outfit.

(By sheer area, it’s extremely enticing. Underboob visible even from the front—perfection. From the side or below, not a single weak angle. Though from below… no good, my head’s too big to slip between her thighs!)

The fantasies didn’t stop. After Number Six, my next target was Boobs Lady; in my head, an elf in bikini armor hopped pointlessly up and down.

“Don’t claim you were not informed. That monster of yours—if it can take on the abilities of what it eats, there’s no reason to tolerate it. Yet you seem to hold views at odds with our settlement’s ideals.”

“That concern has been laid to rest. Argos has no such ability.”

I ignored their back-and-forth. In my mind, Boobs Lady, wobbling and weak-kneed, swung a sword at goblins; her chest bounced, her blows whiffed, and she wound up getting her rear slapped.

Reconstructing as faithfully as possible from that low-spec elf’s behavior, she was toyed with by three unarmed goblins, mocked, and finally, with one breast out, she panted and used the sword like a cane while they took turns spanking her in rhythm to clapping—an ignominious end.

For age-rating reasons, this simulation would not be screened any further.

“…Whatever happened, do you expect me to say ‘Understood’ to that? Even if you gained grounds for certainty, you cannot deny the possibility that the intelligent monster there is deceiving you. The fact you feel reassured means nothing. You don’t expect us to trust that, do you?”

Number Six ground her teeth at the man’s argument.

The situation was disadvantageous for her—and fundamentally, the Natural Harmony Committee’s ideal was overextended here.

(Hmm… to keep Number Six’s position from worsening, should I make the first move?)

With the fantasies finally done, I took stock.

I’d tuned out parts of their talk, but it wasn’t hard to imagine most of the problem was me.

So, I figured, if I withdrew, this could be settled… but as I carefully probed our surroundings, I noticed something.

(Hold on. That’s a Spirit Sword… no, not quite. Different—but something similar. And I sense four of them.)

Only because I could now perceive mana: there were four of those prickling, skin-clinging signatures I’d felt back then.

Different strengths, but likely variants.

The performance was surely lower, but there were four—and judging by range, they were for long-distance attacks.

(Which means bows… That story about armor-piercing shots killing a tank crew—yeah, I’ve heard that somewhere.)

If that wasn’t a tall tale, they were serious.

If they’d brought out weapons used in the war with the Empire, choosing to fight carelessly would not leave us unscathed.

“To bring out magic bows… do you have the Council’s authorization?”

“That sounds as if you’re shielding the monster behind you.”

At his jab, our men started forward with a “Why, you—!” but Number Six held them back with a hand.

At least that confirmed my suspicion.

Given her position, it was a big misstep—but since she did it for my sake, I couldn’t fault her.

Still, it wasn’t good for me to let her keep shielding me.

If those could pierce tank armor, I wouldn’t walk away unhurt—but that didn’t mean I could sit by and watch.

So I strode forward, pulling out my memo pad and writing as I went.

Ignoring Number Six’s attempt to stop me, I reached the opposing leader and showed him my note.

“Delivery.”

I flashed the paper and thumped the metal lump I’d carried onto the ground.

Then I turned to Number Six and started writing in Elven again.

There was chatter behind me, but I ripped the page free and showed it, ignoring them completely.

“Handle the hassle yourselves. I’m going to look for a new place to live. Feed me next time I come by.”

“Um, this does concern you, though…”

Number Six sagged at my note, but the leader behind her called to my back as I turned to leave.

“Where do you think you’re going, Argos?”

I let out a long sigh, retrieved the note I’d given to Number Six, and thrust it before the man’s eyes.

“Hmph. Running away?”

A textbook provocation.

Now that I could sense mana, it was obvious: if I moved, three in the back would do something.

And the four magic bows—what they intended was plain as day.

So I flipped the note and wrote one more line to show him.

“Three for bind-and-slow. Four magic bows.”

I set my hand to my throat and snorted, as if to say, I see right through you.

His cocky smile vanished, and he glared at me with loathing—brave of him to pick a fight at this distance, but he surely had magical defense gear and could block at least once, right?

Therefore I wouldn’t strike first.

If it came to it, I’d drop the perimeter before anything else.

And my we-know-what-you’re-thinking attitude just needled him further.

He was probably overreading my note—thinking I hadn’t written everything I knew—and that would keep him from acting rashly, but they had their own agenda.

They’d brought this many troops and those “magic bows” because they meant to finish me here.

I didn’t know if they were at full readiness, but they’d certainly prepared well.

Which meant I needed a guaranteed, unexpected move.

A small click of the tongue—and a moving arm—told me my precautions were right.

“Inn-dana?”

I moved the hand at my throat and offered a clumsy bit of Elven.

+++

# **115**

“Will that make you my enemy?”

I moved my fingers with the words and manipulated my throat to make it look as if I were speaking.

Since I had always communicated by writing, suddenly speaking fluently would have looked unnatural, so I deliberately used broken speech—that was a conscious choice, not because Elven pronunciation was hard or because I feared messing up when I finally spoke.

“He… spoke?”

The dignitary’s raised hand halted midair in shock. The members on Number Six’s side were likewise slack-jawed.

“An individual’s rampage. It doesn’t apply to this situation.”

I drew a clear line between that old hag and the current matter. As I said, the “control magic” hadn’t come through the will of the elven people as a race, so it could be read as “I don’t intend to be hostile”—but if a troop led by a clan power broker chose to attack, there was no room for interpretation.

By issuing a warning, I created a situation where, if he resorted to force, he would have to take responsibility for any subsequent damage caused by me.

That wouldn’t matter if he acted from unshakable conviction—but I had heard something that told me how he made decisions.

(This man was called Kashial Zesat. That Zesat—an ambitious clan that had recently risen in elven politics. I’d heard more than once about their intrigues, so it stuck with me.)

Would such a man really possess the conviction to eliminate a threat like me?

Answer: no.

Power-seekers tended to prize their own skins.

I meant to exploit that hesitation and put strength into my legs to break out of this disadvantageous encirclement—whether to run or fight, being surrounded was bad.

I had already mapped most of their positions; even accounting for magical concealment, I judged it unlikely there were traps.

I pulled one corner of my mouth up for a grin—there was a wet click as I tried—and just as I was about to spring back, Number Six stepped up beside me and spoke.

“We are in a contract with Argos.”

She smiled. I thought she’d stepped out just to say that, but there was weight behind it.

Her smile read, instinctively, as the smile of someone certain of victory, so I watched to see how the situation would shift.

Kashial’s face twisted with shock and confusion; clearly, her words carried something that could stop him.

“What Forshna seeks are the resources and materials harvested from the Old Empire. In exchange, we have promised to provide food.”

“Are you insane!?”

Bartering with a monster was madness.

But his reaction hinted there was another meaning—likely the true reason he was this rattled.

“Here and now, we recognize ‘Argos’ as a demi-human.”

Her declaration was quiet—and his fist trembled as if that was the one thing he most didn’t want to hear.

“Ridiculous… there’s no way—”

“There is.”

Cutting Kashial off, Number Six said it crisply—nothing like the flustered woman from the river when dealing with children—and pressed the argument to shut down rebuttal.

“‘Demi-human’ refers to a race capable of understanding speech and mutual communication, and that can engage in some form of economic exchange. Argos meets both conditions.”

“…We won’t accept that.”

“This isn’t about acceptance. It’s a matter of definition.”

To be honest, I was already losing the thread.

What difference did it make if I was treated as a monster or as a demi-human?

I learned the answer immediately—and, at the same time, I was forced to recognize how high Number Six stood within the Forshna clan.

“Furthermore, he has established his dwelling in the Fullretos Great Forest—this forest is his habitat.”

“You—!”

Kashial’s enraged shout said he’d grasped her aim.

“Argos is a neighbor. We claimed territory where someone already lived. Therefore, that declaration is void.”

“So that was your goal from the start!?”

At his step forward, it became clear that all of her prior moves with me had led to this.

(Huh? Then what happens to the Committee? And what exactly is going on here?)

The premise shifted.

But that was only his perspective; I still didn’t know the actual state of play.

“Fool! Humans will advance into the forest. If we don’t secure it first, why can’t you see that the next tragedy will repeat?”

“I don’t deny that humans will try to bring the forest under their sway. But will they succeed? Argos here, and the ‘Forest’s Nightmare’ that pushed us to crisis—both were born of this forest.”

“Ah, that does track,” I thought—since the Chimera Project and the Enhanced Soldier Project were born in former Imperial lands, naturally their results would appear in what had become the forest.

But that was my perspective as someone who knew the truth. To others, this forest would simply be a land that birthed new, dangerous monsters.

(So… Zesat wants to preemptively claim the forest for the elves to prevent a future war with humans. Forshna wants to torpedo that plan because trying it means fighting new monsters like me.)

The more I thought, the more both sides’ logic made sense.

“You are making one fatal mistake. Why did you assume there was only one ‘Nightmare’? Why didn’t you consider that something else might follow?”

One side stepped in; the other stepped back. I could call a winner.

“Why didn’t you consider the possibility that a threat greater than the ‘Nightmare’ has already been born in the Great Forest?”

With that single line, my doubts were resolved.

(Oh. That’s it.)

I finally understood her aim.

The Natural Harmony Committee wasn’t the point.

Having seen a monster called “Nightmare” and an out-of-spec being like me, she feared for elven survival.

So she chose to recruit me—since we could communicate—to prepare for the threats they would face.

That might not be the whole of it, but it was certainly part of her intent.

“What do you know! Do you claim that war is a lesser threat than mere monsters!?”

Her concern didn’t reach him.

Even if his head grasped it, his heart wouldn’t allow it.

(War is truly a curse…)

Which created a problem.

Conviction or no, he definitely carried hatred.

Even if its original target was gone, he would choose any means to prevent a repeat.

That was reason enough to pull a trigger.

I’d seen it at the time: people who lost loved ones in war, then enlisted for that very reason—they all had eyes like his.

(Since those who lived it are still alive, you can’t judge this by human lifespans.)

There was nothing I could say to that.

Resigned to “go with the flow,” I felt Number Six’s hand touch me.

“What is the worst case you imagine? The great war repeating? Why won’t you consider the possibility that we’ll be annihilated by threats born in this forest before we reach that future? Before the threat called ‘Nightmare,’ we had no options. At that point, we should have withdrawn from the Great Forest.”

“So you’re telling us to look the other way? You, who know nothing of that war, dare to pontificate?”

“It’s a different problem. Fortunately, someone we can talk to has appeared. We cannot afford to miss this opportunity.”

To say, in front of the monster you plan to use, that you intend to use him—bold didn’t begin to cover it.

(Declare the intent to use me to my face to minimize the trust penalty… my whole assessment of Number Six has flipped.)

Reviewing everything so far, a new worry surfaced.

(…Surely Number Six didn’t sit down at the table assuming from the start that I’d accept being “used,” right?)

The sticking point was their assessment of me—or rather, my intelligence.

There was a prime example: my abnormal speed at learning language.

Being under compulsion had supercharged my studies—and I’d worked hard not to let that be seen.

From the outside, I probably looked like a monster that had suddenly mastered Elven.

(Come to think of it, when I ordered “academic books,” they skipped the primers entirely.)

If that was their evaluation of me, I’d like it revised.

The continuing duel of words started to look like something else entirely.

Shut out of the conversation, I counted myself lucky—and prayed the crossfire of this war of tongues wouldn’t turn my way.

+++

# **116**

I had plenty of worries, but at present I had only one thing to think about.

“How do I make use of this situation?”

In short, it was a very simple question of how to break this stalemate and steer it toward my own gain.

(It irked me that the bikini armor had such an impact I missed parts of the discussion.)

There were some gaps in my information, but put simply it was “If we touch the Great Forest, we’ll face species-level danger because it contains monsters on the ‘Nightmare’ level” VS “If we leave it alone, a second Empire could arise, so we should declare the forest our national territory and nip that in the bud.”

Neither side intended to back down, and Number Six—who argued the former—seemed ready to drag me in to crush the latter, while the other side radiated a readiness to kill, as if words were no longer needed.

Practically speaking, it was a situation where “an attack could come at any moment.”

One reason was that a new condition had emerged: “If they killed me, the other side’s logic would collapse.”

(A literal, physical rebuttal. Punching problems away is supposed to end with one’s boyhood.)

Choosing such an uncivilized solution was barbaric enough without me.

And here they had a conveniently placed monster—me—but if I acted, I would contradict Number Six’s position.

In other words, a stalemate.

(It hurt that I couldn’t move first. But I also didn’t want them to seize the initiative…)

Even if weaker than the Spirit Sword, those bows—clearly its cousins—had me in their sights; a careless move could trigger instant battle.

“What action benefits me in this situation?” I asked myself, and laid out the conditions.

• My immediate safety.

• My future safety.

The fact that these two came to mind first said it all about the present.

Add to that: future trade, and shared use of the river as valuable geography—those were the profits I should aim for right now.

Which meant, at the moment, I could only achieve those goals by riding along with Number Six’s plan.

With a “good grief” attitude, I set down my pack, took something from it, and stepped between the two sides facing off.

Seeing me move, the Zesat elves raised their guard, ready to signal at any time.

Number Six tried to pull me back, but waiting wouldn’t turn this to our favor, so I intervened.

First, I lifted the item hooked on my finger to a height where he could see it.

“…What are you playing at?”

What I took from my pack was a gemmed necklace.

If memory served, in the magical nation of Seizeria, gems were valuable media used as catalysts.

If so, they ought to be valuable to elves as well.

Even if not, their monetary value was beyond doubt.

I could have joked, “It’s a bribe,” but that would make me a caught-in-the-act bagman.

So I persuaded him like this.

“I know objects like this and the materials we just hauled out have value in the elven settlement. Getting them safely—without casualties—is not a bad deal for you either. Given what they are, they could expand your defensive strength. If you insist on settling it here and now, I’ll resolve myself to fight… but will the losses be acceptable? If I may say one thing: you’re rushing. I understand your position, but this forest won’t fall to human hands so easily. You know that.”

I scribbled something to that effect, working hard to get it all on the paper.

A monster pinched an obviously ill-fitting pen between two fingers, wedged himself between two glowering elven dignitaries, and scrawled his case across a memo—before that surreal scene, even those two seemed to regain their composure. But I wanted it known: I wanted them calm because of the argument, not the sight.

“Read this text, properly.”

Someone snorted at my shaky Elven while I pressed my throat; by the sound’s location, I knew who.

I glanced back, marked the one stifling laughter, and locked on to his face in irritation.

Kashial watched the man dramatically dodge a pebble I flicked with my tail, let out a long breath, and turned on his heel.

“…For the record, I will not accept this.”

As the Zesat clan withdrew with those words, I tossed them the jeweled ornament.

“You forgot something.”

A small pity—but once they took it, they’d be accomplices too… or so I thought, until they threw it back.

My petty scheming wasn’t enough, apparently.

At any rate, I chose to be glad we’d escaped that situation.

It would be bad if, now that they were calm, they decided, “Killing you is still the fastest solution,” and the odds of that seemed annoyingly high.

I worried: “They might try an ambush,” but that event only triggered if I stayed in the elven settlement.

“Did you plan that?”

To Number Six’s question, I waved a hand: Not at all.

Even so, I understood the nature of his fears now; knowing that perspective meant I’d be pulled into elven internal conflicts down the line.

I had no desire to stick my neck into that.

“I’m going to look for a new base.”

I showed the note and turned my back on Number Six to leave.

The idea that “we should put some distance between us” was not wrong.

And I did, in fact, need a new base.

“Wait!”

I had my thoughts, but I didn’t look back.

If our relationship was one of mutual use, digging deeper was forbidden.

With her voice at my back, I leapt over the river.

The last thing I heard was, “Someday, for sure”—what it meant, I chose not to ponder now.

Still, there were portions where our interests aligned, and because of that, we would “surely” meet again, as she said.

In fact, I would certainly keep an eye on her—without being noticed.

(Ahh, so many regrets. Better this than being drawn into a mess… but still, regrets.)

A knockout-beautiful woman, cultured meals, the entertainment called magic, and that delightfully unfortunate multipurpose toy—too much to discard lightly.

I would have liked her help with my goals; if I wanted that, Zesat’s side would actually be more convenient.

But they had ample reason to take my life; there was no way we could join hands.

“Why is the world so unwilling to go my way…?”

Alone in the dark forest, I murmured to no one.

No answer returned—only the sound of insects, from somewhere unseen.

The next morning—I wandered the woods without a plan, and found no suitable place for a base.

Which was only natural; I hadn’t even looked at research facilities or their entrances.

I regretted not immediately discarding the naive idea that “if I just stroll around aimlessly, I might find something.”

“I could use a mapping function.”

At that mutter, a revelation descended on me.

“Right. Maybe, like before, I ‘actually have that ability,’ hidden?”

I acted at once—and the conclusion was: no, I didn’t.

(Yeah, this isn’t a game. How would they even install such a handy feature?)

Given my track record, I had to wonder “What if…,” but even my homeland’s tech hadn’t managed that.

I tried this and that—nothing.

“Honestly… searching a stupidly vast Old Imperial territory alone is impossible, any way you slice it.”

I would definitely need other hands.

I had selected the elves as that partner, but after this, that looked shaky.

Which meant I needed a different partner for cooperation.

(…The Council-State. There’s no one else.)

Having no options was its own headache.

Right now, the Furon Council-State was surely pushing north while subjugating orcs and monsters.

If I appeared in the middle of that—what would happen?

“Ugh—I already told them ‘You can claim up to the farm district’…”

Going now would invite the wrong impression, and if she was my counterpart, the timing would be terrible.

So I needed to kill some time.

I could look for a base, but I also lacked certain supplies.

I’d go restock those, then head south while searching; that would use time decently.

“Mm… I need time before dealing with the elves again—and the same goes for the Council-State.”

North it was, after all.

I would cull monsters at a sensible pace, and collect the reward in the Kingdom of Canaan.

+++

# **117**

I remembered this phenomenon being called some sort of “law.”

But as a thoroughly average gamer, I had a better name for it.

“Even like this, the desire sensor still worked, huh…”

I searched for a base near the elven settlement and found nothing, but when I turned north toward the Kingdom of Canaan, the thing I’d wanted turned up.

It was a structure overgrown with green—still retaining its original shape, which likely meant it had been built sturdy.

I’d returned to the old base once to grab only what I needed; while heading north, I noticed a patch where the trees thinned. When I went to check, I found this.

Around it lay objects already reclaimed by nature to the point of being unidentifiable, and the rubble wore a uniform coat of green.

To be safe, I walked the perimeter that appeared to have belonged to some facility.

Only one building had survived… and even that one had been badly eaten away by nature. Whether it was usable would depend on the inside.

(…From the shape, a warehouse? No—this was probably a former military site. Which would make it a garage, not a warehouse.)

The entrance was large enough even for my bulk to pass through, which led me to that conclusion.

I could have opened the shutter and confirmed it, but first I carefully checked the surroundings.

There was no guarantee the place wouldn’t collapse once I made an opening at the entrance.

This was a new base candidate I’d worked hard to find.

I wouldn’t lose it to some simple mistake; only after a thorough once-over did I pry the shutter up.

So I spent about thirty minutes circling the structure, inspecting the damage.

The verdict: the windows were broken, but otherwise it seemed mostly fine.

I wasn’t an expert, so I couldn’t be certain, but I felt “probably okay.”

Peeking through a window frame, I saw almost no supplies left inside, so I still couldn’t tell whether it had been a warehouse or a garage.

With that, I went “gag-gaa” and heaved the shutter up with a loud cracking creak.

I could speak normally now, but the growl slipped out of habit.

Balancing both sides and forcing it open by brute strength warped the shutter badly. I ducked under and stepped in.

“…Nature is hardcore.”

I’d assumed the broken windows would mean some degree of encroachment inside.

But the reality was about fifty percent worse than I’d pictured; I let out a long sigh.

From the window, it hadn’t looked that bad; seeing the whole interior changed the impression entirely.

(This would take time to make usable.)

Even so, a roofed building was precious.

Passing it up wasn’t an option.

I started searching—and, as expected, there was no underground facility.

I did find a few things, but nothing useful; the tools for vehicle maintenance and the spare tires told me this had been a garage.

The interior was large enough for a tank, with room for me to move around.

But it was still just a big garage.

Looking up from below at the warped ceiling, I thought it had survived not because it was well-built, but because it had been lucky—and I sighed at the thought of making it my main base.

“A temporary base, then.”

I figured there were multiple such military sites on the Republic side.

As a stopgap until I found a place in better condition, this wouldn’t be a wasted find.

I planned to search for anything usable, but judging by the state of things, I wasn’t expecting much.

Even so, “don’t search” wasn’t an option for me.

As long as even a sliver of possibility existed, I couldn’t ignore it.

It might not be my main goal, but I hoped there could be a hint leading me there.

Considering where enhanced soldiers like me would be deployed, it wouldn’t be strange to find leads in Republic-side military facilities.

For those reasons, I paced the carpet of weeds through the former installation… but found nothing promising.

I even carefully checked the footprints of former buildings in case there was a hidden room, but between that and clearing rubble, I resigned myself to testing the comfort of the temporary base tonight.

“My bedding set is still back there…”

It wasn’t something I could carry in one go, so the bedding and some daily goods remained at the ruins of the previous base.

I’d rigged the remaining surface portion into a makeshift storehouse so it wouldn’t be looted, but an ogre could clear rubble like that—hardly reassuring.

Then again, would an ogre bother clearing rubble be a “yes”? The answer was “no.”

The bed would be inadequate, but I’d use my luggage as a pillow and lie down.

Morning came; in a word, my impression of the temporary base was:

“No good.”

The problem: when the wind blew, the noise was awful.

There must have been just the right kind of gaps; coupled with the ceiling’s creaks, to these sensitive ears it was basically loud noise.

I’d been foolish to think “as long as it keeps out the rain and wind, it’s fine.”

So this settled into “a shelter I won’t use except in emergencies.”

It could at least serve as a materials cache, so it needed a marker.

Also, a patch of forest with fewer trees might be useful for something.

I tried to frame it positively, but that was all I could come up with.

Spending a whole day for this conclusion felt like a waste of time.

“…North, then.”

I scratched the back of my head and muttered.

I wanted spices first, but something else had my attention, so I prioritized that.

It was only a short detour—wouldn’t take long.

By the next morning, I’d found a highway, but it strayed from my destination.

I’d gotten confident at correcting my course on the run lately, so that stung a little.

I started east, and soon the place I wanted came into view.

(This made the third time here…)

There was nothing in front of the cave where the soldiers had been; there was even less sign of people than my first visit.

I scanned the area, but there weren’t even traces of anyone; at least within my detection range, no one was there.

I didn’t bother to ponder why they were gone.

Knowing wouldn’t help me.

“So, if I want spices, I’ll have to stake out that route again…”

Would the same trick still work?

If there had been losses and the cause hadn’t been removed, of course they’d use a different route.

Either way, I was still heading east.

Hoping nothing annoying cropped up on the way, I ran through the forest while keeping the highway within sight.

Progress was smooth for a while, but if memory served, I’d soon be near the newly founded town.

As a forward base for southern settlement, it would naturally attract mercenaries, and since they operated around it, passing by in broad daylight risked discovery.

(For my purposes, it would be bad if word got out that I was here. Wait till night, or use mimicry…)

Mimicry was handy, but time-limited.

Using it here wasn’t a bad choice, but if I got spotted, that ability might be exposed.

As before, invisibility didn’t matter if magical detection flagged me anyway.

So instead of moving during peak daytime earnings, even if I used the ability to slip through, the right call was to shift the timing.

I put distance between myself and the town, hid in the forest, and started magic practice.

For the record, I trained even while traveling; by now I could produce two flames simultaneously from my fingertips.

But my mana pool was still the same as ever.

Would the day ever come when I moved beyond “one-trick act”?

+++

# **118 - A Certain Elf’s Perspective (2)**

“The survival of one’s species is the highest priority for any living being.”

My father never wavered from his argument that we must prepare for the coming day.

He insisted that the elves should possess some means of countering overwhelmingly powerful races or individuals—such as dragons. Naturally, such words provoked the council, who knew all too well what reckless pursuit of power could bring.

Now I understand why his position grew precarious when he even suggested employing other races—humans or monsters—as tools.

That led him into contact with an unsavory organization… who could have foreseen it would cost him his life?

“He did not die for his beliefs, nor did he perish after achieving his ideals. As his child, can I call myself such and not mourn that regret?”

Silencing them had been easy.

Neither the Committee nor the Council had anticipated my parents’ deaths. Having driven a member of a great clan to his demise, they had no choice but to accept our demands.

And since the Council was glad to quiet the Committee’s endless complaints, they tolerated my words.

All of it—within someone else’s palm.

My will had no place in it.

To be honest, my father’s ideology never interested me much.

As an elf, perhaps he was admirable; as a husband and father who neglected his family for research and factional strife, he was not.

Dragging Mother into his death only made it worse.

Still, I never hated him.

“To preserve the elven race, he gave everything—and died before his work could bear fruit.” That would likely be how people describe my father.

And I, the woman who used his ideology to gain her current position—that would be my reputation.

Even so, I’ve never wished to escape this position.

Back then, I was still naïve enough to think, perhaps there’s something I could do.

I hadn’t expected it to come to this.

Sitting by the window, I lost myself in thought.

When that “Nightmare of the Forest” first appeared before us, everyone had thought, “Just another troublesome monster.”

I had thought the same.

But as the damage spread, I began to wonder if my father’s “coming day” referred to this very threat.

Yet the outcome, as we know, was that the “Nightmare” was driven away by the Sword Saint.

At the time, reports claimed it was slain, though unconfirmed. Later, when it reappeared, it was devoured by another monster.

And that monster, they said, controlled it using a forbidden art.

Those were turbulent days indeed.

In the end, we managed to form a decent relationship with that monster—that much was fortunate.

But I also began to feel my father’s fears were becoming reality.

With back-to-back appearances of beings capable of endangering the elven race, I reached one conclusion.

“I see. To fight a monster, use another monster.”

Nothing complicated about it.

If individuals existed capable of threatening even entire species, then all we needed was to pit one such against another.

By then, my course was nearly set.

But I would need to prove that the monster Argos could benefit the elves.

Without that proof, my proposal would never pass the Council of Sages.

“Did you know this would happen, Father?”

His image surfaced in my mind.

I still don’t know what drove him so far.

I have no way of knowing what he learned, or what exactly he feared—not even the outline.

Too much time has passed.

I detest the indifference I once showed.

(If I had spoken more with Father… would I have known something?)

Someone in the Council once said,

“Perhaps an age of change is coming.”

From our informants in human towns came reports of mutated orcs rampaging in the southern Council-State.

Which could mean—after the “Nightmare” and Argos—a third threat had arisen.

The order didn’t matter; such coincidences could not be mere chance.

“I wonder if he’s read my letter yet…”

Somehow, I must bring Argos to our side.

Many were questioning this plan.

(I know. It’s far too dangerous.)

That monster possessed the power to devour even the Nightmare, and the intellect to manipulate elves.

Most of all, Argos’s learning speed was astonishing.

He absorbed language and culture from the ruins of the old Empire, imitated them, and displayed intelligence unbefitting the word “monster”—tempered with caution rather than arrogance.

(He’s likely grown more wary of humankind as a result of the knowledge he’s acquired. Judging from how he’s taught himself, that’s natural.)

If he continued to gain tactical understanding, how great would his threat become?

No—perhaps he already had.

(Open hostility is unthinkable. If that isn’t made clear, how many will die? Will it even stop at casualties?)

So I stood.

The Council of Sages was now in turmoil over the information we’ve brought.

A conclusion must be reached soon.

Before negotiations become impossible, I must unify the village’s will.

“Is anyone there?”

I called for a messenger.

Time was running short.

Driven by urgency, I walked.

And prayed this path would not lead to ruin.

+++

# **119 - A Certain Elf’s Perspective (3)**

I exhaled deeply.

Whether it was a sigh of weariness or relief, no one could tell.

By poor fortune, a representative of the Zesat Clan attended the Council of Sages that day.

Normally, his clan rarely appeared at such meetings.

Only when the topic bore urgency or great importance did most clans gather, ensuring that he and I would cross paths.

But for him to attend such a minor session as this was unexpected.

And, as expected, he read my intent and moved to obstruct it.

The result: the “Forshna Clan’s exclusive negotiation rights with Argos.”

Somehow, Kashial Zesat had learned of the matter involving the forbidden art.

I wanted to admonish my clan leader for poor information control, but now wasn’t the time.

Negotiating with Argos itself wasn’t impossible.
