The problem lay in the price.

The monster’s power was far too great for us to force our own terms upon him.

Had things gone well, we might have secured the village’s support—but the Zesat faction’s maneuvering prevented that, twisting the outcome toward “eliminating the monster dwelling in the ruins to erase the forbidden art.”

A masterful manipulation indeed.

I had to admit—we had been outplayed.

Thus, came the decision for a solo negotiation.

“It may fail, but there’s no harm in trying first. Depending on our approach, even failure will cost us nothing. It’s worth doing,” I’d argued, and managed to steer things back on course.

Now, all that remained was to wait for Argos to appear.

When, or if, he would come—no one knew.

All I could do was wait in the quiet rhythm of returned routine.

But the reunion came sooner than expected.

Our encounter by the river had been an unexpected boon—I couldn’t help but smile.

And yet, a concern also arose.

Simply put, “Argos leaned too closely toward the lost Empire.”

It brought both reassurance—that dialogue was possible—and anxiety—that conflict could just as easily arise.

The prediction “Negotiations will be possible but difficult” had been an entirely reasonable one.

At the time, as I watched Argos treat the children gently, I searched for an opening to begin negotiations.

But such deliberation felt almost absurd before a monster this humanized.

His behavior could be rationalized, but even if his knowledge came from imperial texts, his obsession was excessive.

Some suggested “he’s simply impressionable,” or “he just wants to show off what he’s learned,” but whether that was true was unclear.

At least our initial belief—that he was someone we could reason with—proved accurate.

So, instead of resorting to deceit, I chose a direct approach.

Whether that was wise or not, I said all that needed saying.

Then, silence reigned.

Argos’s answer—“I accept, under certain conditions”—was within the realm of what I’d expected.

Yet those conditions were strange: “Inform me if you find any similar facilities,” and “Allow me to accompany you during their destruction.”

For one who was giving up his home, those demands seemed… too light.

Still, questioning them risked failure.

Rejecting those terms would mean failed negotiations—and likely a violent clash with Argos during the purging of the forbidden art.

So I agreed, and asked him to remain in our territory for a time.

That was when I discovered he apparently didn’t sleep—spending nights reading endlessly.

Was it a trait of his kind, or simple hunger for knowledge?

Whatever the reason, his intellect far surpassed what I had imagined.

I never expected what came next.

The Sword Saint Shuvard visited—obviously, concerning Argos.

He wouldn’t have moved at the words of a Zesat envoy; this had to be a direct order from Chief Corner.

But to summon the old master despite his unhealed wounds—it meant our prior proposal had been ignored.

“Worry not. I have Tostalite’s consent,” said the Forshna clan leader, confirming authorization.

I couldn’t help but glance up at the sky in exasperation—we’d been outmaneuvered again.

“Relax. I just want to test him a little,” the old Sword Saint chuckled.

And he did exactly that.

I was unfit to describe the result myself, but according to the guards, “Neither fought seriously; it was more like a friendly spar.”

I sighed in relief.

Once the forbidden art was purged, peace would return—and perhaps we’d gain Argos as an ally.

Clearing every obstacle, I pressed preparations forward.

Meanwhile, Argos continued his training, seemingly enamored with the magic he’d learned.

Anyone watching might feel threatened, yet he remained a being who could converse and negotiate.

As a solitary monster, he bore no territorial ambition; when he wanted something, he preferred exchange to theft.

Some called him “a human imitator,” but for us, that was only to our benefit.

Everything was going smoothly—until Shuvard’s latest report made my head spin:

“That monster may gain the abilities of those it devours.”

At that line, everything clicked.

Why had Argos fixated on those ruins, and on others like them?

Because he had already obtained something from the relic there—and was seeking more.

If only I could have dismissed it as impossible.

But we elves knew of precedents.

Perhaps this was already beyond my control.

Still, the decision had been made.

Even if we were being used, we had to confirm the truth.

Sensing my urgency, everyone pushed themselves harder, completing preparations sooner than expected.

We set out for the ruins.

The lack of any trouble along the way was surprising—until one escort raised his hand: “Maybe monsters avoid this area because of Argos?”

I’d completely overlooked that. Regardless, we needed our fighting force ready for anything.

The next day, we arrived, fully prepared.

Then, Argos stopped us.

He said, “I want to see for myself what’s inside.”

My fears began to take shape.

Still, I had to confirm the truth.

I accepted his request—and unveiled the forbidden art before all.

Its very sight was agony: a grotesque fusion of man and spider, its desiccated remains causing several to gag.

“This is the forbidden art,” I declared.

I spoke fervently—how such an existence must never be allowed again—and hoped that my conviction reached them.

Then Argos approached the husk, studied it silently, and snapped off one of its legs—placing it in his mouth.

Reality struck.

But the next instant, he spat it out.

When I asked what he was doing, his answer was absurdly casual: “It’s rare. I figured if I didn’t try it now, I might never get another chance to taste it.”

Was that truly the reason?

Or could he steal abilities just by consuming?

As I wondered, he began talking about his dietary habits.

His attempt to deflect only deepened my anger—beyond the point of relief.

I vented a bit of that anger, but his reaction was strangely un-monsterlike.

One thing became clear.

Argos likely had no desire to become an enemy of the elves.

What was this newly born species thinking, wishing, and seeking in walking among us?

Would the day come when we learned the answer?

When the destruction of the forbidden art was over, we returned without understanding anything.

If you called it naïve, I could only agree.

Whether human or elf, nothing in this world is more terrifying than resentment—and I had forgotten that.

+++

# **120**

On the second day after entering the Kingdom of Canaan’s territory.

I kept to stealth as a rule and moved along smoothly without being seen.

My destination was the same Canaan–Seizeria trade route as last time.

Using the same point over and over would surely cause problems later, but for now it was the only place I knew.

I slipped past the mercenary town and skulked east.

On the way I spotted a worrisome group—an armed column heading south with a mass of wagons—which could only look like they were concentrating troops in the town.

“Has Canaan finally come to blows with Seizeria?”

I muttered, tilting my head as I watched them go.

By the way, over half of their gear didn’t match, so the core was mercenaries, not soldiers.

There were also a fair number in uniform armor that looked like regulars, so perhaps it was a transport detachment or something similar.

In any case, after letting that grim procession pass, I reached the same spot as before without incident.

I hid my pack and began scouting to check the surroundings.

It was still high noon, so I couldn’t push it, but as long as I didn’t step out onto the open grassland it should be fine.

Threading slickly through the trees, I caught a smell that pricked my nose.

(…Blood. Probably human.)

I judged it different from an animal’s by the odor and the sound.

For blood to hang in the air and yet things to be this quiet—it even made me think the person was already dead.

The guess hit the mark: following the scent brought me to a man’s corpse lying in a pool of blood.

“Hm… no sign his belongings were taken…”

His shoulder bag was still on him; his throat had been cut.

There was blood on his legs too, so they likely slashed his legs first to stop him running, then cut his throat.

(A corpse left here with its belongings untouched, huh.)

By the look of things, it was a body with… circumstances.

Nothing had eaten it, so I figured it had been a day or so since death, but a body left all alone in a place like this was a mystery.

I decided to rummage the belongings and stepped closer.

One pocket was soaked in blood, so I didn’t touch it.

I found a few copper coins that looked Seizerian, some rations and water, a few daily items, and a letter.

Amusingly, the letter was written in the Canaan Kingdom language.

(Aha—so, a person who went back and forth between both countries and had ties in each would be…)

Either a spy, or a criminal doing smuggling and the like.

Given the times, it could also be a failed attempt at illegal entry.

Since the valuables were intact, that seemed the likelier read.

“In that case, there’s a route like that around here…”

I murmured and looked around.

For caution’s sake I focused my hearing, but picked up no human sounds.

Still, if there was a path people used nearby, leaving my pack close to it would carry some risk.

Forced to change my stash spot, I grudgingly headed back under my hidden pack.

I confirmed my rucksack was intact, took a breath, and read the letter I’d just taken.

Even if I wasn’t great at listening comprehension, reading text like this was no problem.

So I thought—but there were more unknown words than expected, and in the end all I grasped was that the dead man had tried to smuggle something and failed, which led to this.

He seemed to have hired a guide to slip out illegally; their exchange was in the letter, and the line “I don’t take payment after, but I might accept half of your sale price when you move the goods” suggested the cargo had been high-value.

“I see—so they didn’t bother with pocket change,” I nodded.

I didn’t have time to play detective, but I passed a while analyzing the scene like reading a short mystery.

Keeping alert, practicing magic, I reached the twist that “the culprit had been a con man after the contraband all along—not a guide!”—and then the sun went down.

Once dark, I could move boldly.

Campfires and the like made easy beacons, so finding targets got easier.

In other words, night was the time to act—and after lecturing myself with confidence, I achieved nothing.

“Yeah, there are days like that.”

At dawn I trudged back into the copse and, weaving through tree gaps too tight for this body, casually bagged a few wild birds.

It had been a while since I’d had bird meat.

In the forest they were mostly small birds and not easy to catch.

Canaan, though, had limited habitats for that wild bullet, the flying stakefish, so I could nab birds of decent size like this.

I’d love to find chickens again, but wild ones required very specific conditions.

For today, I’d be content with these.

Plucking—right, that was actually pretty hard—so after the sun was well up and breakfast finished: good morning.

And good night.

For a few days I’d be active mainly at night, so I’d likely sleep mornings.

So when I woke, noon had long passed.

All right—time to train with my recovered mana and hunt to make up for the modest breakfast.

I hunted through the grove, lighting alternating flames on my fingertips.

The close spacing of the trees made it a pain, but I managed to take two rabbits.

While grilling the cut meat, I felt a presence at a distance.

(…Human voices.)

The direction matched the corpse I’d found yesterday, so maybe someone had discovered it and shouted.

I hesitated over checking it out… but I was mid-lunch.

Hasty cleanup only leads to sloppy work.

“Tch, none of my business,” I decided, and kept grilling.

Rabbit was plain, so I preferred a heavy seasoning.

I was pondering whether I could get spices in the Council-State while focusing on the meat when I noticed one sound heading my way.

“Ugh, what a pain,” I swallowed the honest complaint, pinched the tongs between two fingers, and stared that way.

Then I heard a voice bawling like a scolding roar.

It was in the Canaan tongue, so I caught part of it; it was probably saying,

“Don’t you use fire out here, you idiot!”

A fair point, I had to admit.

But I didn’t stop the barbecue.

If I stopped just because a human said something, they’d realize I understood their words.

I had no intention of revealing information to this side’s people yet.

So I ignored him and kept grilling, and sensed the sound I’d been tracking drawing closer.

I stood once and used my telescopic sight; I saw a middle-aged hunter with a bow.

It would be inconvenient to have my presence exposed here.

I grabbed a handy stone from the ground, wound up big, and threw it so it wouldn’t hit the hunter.

The rock slammed into a tree trunk in front of him; seeing that, he panicked and ran, and I ignored him as I laid the last slice on the griddle.

It missed the mark by a lot, but since it didn’t hit him, fine by me.

I hadn’t expected things to go so perfectly to plan, but when they did, it felt good.

I even started hoping I might find a caravan tonight.

After lunch, I cleaned up and lay down in the grove again.

One more nap until night—once in a while, lazing around like this wasn’t bad.

+++

# **121**

I devoted myself to honing my magic while staying alert to my surroundings.

Given that I now trained whenever I had a spare moment, I could not help thinking that, if I had been this diligent back when I was human, my life might have turned out very differently.

I had finally managed to light flames on the tips of three fingers at once, and I stared at the fruits of my efforts with the same stupid grin plastered on my face.

“What I’m aiming for is efficient use. What I want is a practical single shot.”

I had gone over it in my head countless times since then, but my conclusion never changed; all I learned was that there was a strict limit to what I could do with such a meager amount of mana.

People loved to say that “hard work always pays off,” but what was impossible was still impossible.

With that in mind, I cut my morning training short and started moving to secure breakfast.

Yesterday I had driven off a human, but that also meant I had effectively confirmed for them that “something is out here.”

In that case, it was inevitable that people would be sent either to investigate or to exterminate.

Dutifully going out to hunt for breakfast also served as a way to look for a better location.

I praised myself for my efficient routine, but I had to admit it was simply “bad luck” that I still had not found terrain that met my key conditions.

After finishing breakfast, I let out a satisfied “burp” and took a breath.

Bad manners were hardly a problem out here.

While practicing my mana control, I cleaned up and then headed further east toward the mountains.

A great mountain separated Canaan and Seizeria—back in the Empire, it had been called the Hedai Mountains, though I could no longer remember what name the two nations themselves used.

It would have been ideal to find a spot from which I could look down on the key routes, but from where I was, no such vantage point was in sight.

I hoped that getting closer might reveal something, but aside from a huge bear—one I felt I had seen somewhere before—blocking my path, nothing worth mentioning happened.

After one slap sent the bear running, I continued deeper in the direction it had fled and stumbled upon an unexpected encounter.

“…Another corpse, huh.”

This time it had already turned to bare bone, and from its eye sockets, encroaching plants were sprouting straight out.

It went without saying that a great deal of time had passed, and for the moment I searched the area around the sprawled skeleton for anything it might have owned.

I yanked a vine-covered bag out by brute force, and when I checked inside, I found a discolored potion and a ring set with a gemstone.

And then—

“A dagger with a crest…”

Muttering that, I carefully searched a little more around the skeleton.

From the remaining scraps of clothing, I concluded the bones most likely belonged to a woman, and from the belongings left behind, that she had very likely been a noble.

Lastly, I found another, much smaller set of bones beside the woman.

In other words, the woman had had a child, and the two of them had died here together.

It was only natural to think, “This clearly has some kind of backstory,” and that gave rise to hesitation—if I took anything from here, would I raise some weird flag?

(Ugh, even back in Imperial days I never wanted anything to do with nobles. And that crest with a butterfly and flames…)

At that moment, something came back to me.

“Butterfly and flames?”

Something snagged at my memory.

I was certain it was sitting in one of the drawers of my mind.

A particular memory from my student days slowly took shape.

“I remember now.”

Butterfly and flames—that was the crest of a count’s house belonging to the Kingdom of Seizeria.

The fact that it was here suggested that the House Orselem still existed.

Back in my student days, there had been an image of a nude woman in an art reference book.

In my mid-teens, when getting my hands on an actual dirty magazine had been a real struggle, I had stumbled across that erotic material in the library by chance.

It had started from something that trivial, but I still vividly remembered the results of the “research” my friend and I did together.

(The House Orselem! An old noble family in Seizeria, famous for producing beauties generation after generation. To flaunt that, every daughter was said to have a nude portrait painted once she came of age!)

I remembered my friend and me excitedly talking about that whole setup, asking each other, “Isn’t this ridiculously sexy?”

Overcome by nostalgia, I found my hand reaching for the crested dagger.

It was not that I wanted some connection to a beautiful woman.

It was the nostalgia for that time and the memories of talking with my friend that moved me.

Incidentally, after much discussion, my friend and I had concluded that the model, Lady Silserim Orselem, ranked as a solid “Number Five” in our private system.

As for her herself, in keeping with a magic nation, she had apparently been a very research-minded, scholarly woman, and by the time I joined the military she was supposed to have become the mother of a son and a daughter.

I wrapped the dagger in cloth and carefully stowed it in my pack.

The sense that I was setting up a whole tangle of new flags somehow filled me with excitement.

Just in case, I checked for anything else, but the only items of note were that dagger and the ring.

Part of me wondered if there might have been a diary or something, but unfortunately I was utterly ignorant of the Seizeria language.

Still, for someone who wanted to know the details, items like these would be things they absolutely had to have.

If even one such item had been lying around, then collecting those flags would probably have been unavoidable.

“Well then, I wonder just what these are going to call down on me.”

I reassured myself that, as long as they were connected to a beautiful woman, things could not turn out that badly, and I looked back on my dealings with the various beauties I had met so far.

Probably fine—coming to that vague conclusion while staring off into the distance, I left the spot behind.

In the end, I spent the rest of the day just wandering around the foot of the mountain.

Thanks to that, after the sun went down I became a bit unsure of my bearings and wasted a lot of time before I finally emerged onto the grasslands.

And once again, I had nothing to show for the day.

It was possible this route simply was not being used anymore.

Just as I was thinking that, I thought I saw something faintly flash.

I straightened up to get a bit more height.

Then I confirmed that there was a light some distance away.

It flickered on and off like a signal—no sooner had I thought that than, when I looked around, I saw another light near the stand of trees I had just come out of.

(…They’re definitely using those to communicate.)

It was certainly unrelated to the earlier skeleton.

If it was connected to anything, it would be yesterday’s corpse.

If you asked whether I should get involved… honestly, I did not see much profit in it.

But I did at least want to know what they were doing.

So I used my mimicry ability and melted into the night.

If the people exchanging signals decided to meet up, I did not want to miss a word of their conversation.

I lay flat on the ground and focused my senses on my hearing.

The lights went out, leaving only silence and darkness.

I stayed like that for a while… but nothing happened.

(Was it just the signal?)

No sooner had that thought crossed my mind than another flickering light appeared from the same spot.

As I watched, I realized the timing of the flashes was different from before.

In other words, they were sending coded signals with light.

“Great, what a waste of effort,” I cursed inwardly, but there was one thing I had learned.

This was a place those people used, which meant it would be difficult for me to accomplish my own objectives here.

If it was ill-suited for ambushing a caravan, then I had no reason to linger.

Letting that hunter escape had put me on a time limit anyway, so in a sense this might actually have been for the best, and I gave up on the spot without much regret.

So I deactivated my mimicry.

I lumbered to my feet—and heard a sound.

I spun around on reflex, and there was a person standing where I looked.

It was not as though I had dropped my guard.

I had been focused on my hearing, true, but I had not neglected to keep track of my surroundings with my sense of smell either.

Which meant the human now in my field of vision had managed to get this close without making a sound or giving off a scent.

I had never even imagined there could be a human capable of that.

But I did know of a means that could make such a thing possible.

(Magic is way too convenient, damn it!)

I moved at the exact same moment the person who had spotted me did.

Which side they belonged to did not matter right now.

But if there was even a chance they had seen my mimicry ability, I had no reason to let them live.

From their perspective, they would think not “I failed to notice him,” but rather “He appeared out of nowhere.”

And what they would think next went without saying.

The distance between us was about fifteen meters—in other words, there was no way they could outrun me.

(Judging from the small build, I could not help wondering about their sex… Ah, if it was a woman—no, what if she turned out to be a total beauty!?)

And so our nighttime game of chase began.

+++

# **122**

We both started at the same time—the sharp click of a tongue and a man’s voice yelling, “Damn it!” rang out at once.

Relieved that I could now deal with him without hesitation, I closed the distance in one burst.

But my moment of satisfaction was cut short when a dull pain shot through the soles of my feet.

(Whoa! He scattered metal fragments!)

The soles of my feet were not that tough, so they were more than enough to injure me.

The tools were clearly meant for use against humans, so they did not quite pierce my feet, but they certainly fulfilled their role of hindering my movement.

Thinking, “What a nuisance,” I swept my hands in front of me to clear the obstacles away, then pushed off hard and leaped.

I could have just stomped him from there, since I had no intention of letting him escape, but the small-built man moved with extraordinary agility and barely dodged with an acrobatic twist.

However, as I landed, I spread my arms and swept my left arm across, and the man, unable to dodge, was sent flying.

From the feel of it, I was pretty sure I had broken several of his bones.

On top of the damage from the fall, he probably would not be able to move for a while.

The man glared up at me between ragged breaths as I strolled up to him.

“You frog-eaters are using monsters now, are you!? You—!”

I tilted my head at that last part, but still finished off the small man.

At the same time, from the sounds around me, I judged that the group who had been exchanging light signals earlier had noticed what was happening here.

(Still… I had absolutely no idea what he was saying.)

There was not a single word I could pick out.

No, more than that—this was not even the Canaan Kingdom language.

(Huh? So this guy was from Canaan, and the people over there were from Seizeria?)

Unable to get a grip on the situation, I felt a bit confused.

Meanwhile, the two groups sensed something was wrong and were already moving away from this area.

The man’s last words came back to me.

“You frog-eaters”—that was a phrase Canaan’s people often used to mock Seizeria’s food culture.

The part I had not caught was probably just more insults, and not understanding it would not really change anything.

So those people had been from Seizeria, and this man had been watching them?

For what purpose?

(All I knew for sure was that I was completely lacking information. I had no clue what was going on.)

As a rough guess, if they were exchanging signals in the dark like this, they were probably intelligence agents of some sort.

If I assumed this man had been watching them, then he was likely in the same line of work—in other words, I had stumbled right into the middle of a counterintelligence operation.

(I see… If that was the case, then it made sense he would think the timing was too perfect and mistake me for a “monster controlled by Seizeria.”)

It was just a provisional scenario I had thrown together, but it was surprisingly plausible.

As I was patting myself on the back, thinking, “Maybe that really is how it is, I’ve been pretty sharp lately,” the Seizerian agents scattered and began to flee.

By the time I noticed, it was already too late.

Even if I wanted to deal with it, they were escaping in exactly the way I least wanted, making it practically impossible to silence them all.

Unless I went all out there was no chance, and if I went all out and still let even one escape, information would leak.

“Ah, crap,” I blurted out before realizing that, when I thought it through, I currently had no real reason to attack people from Seizeria.

At the very least, they had not done anything so critical that it warranted me taking the initiative against them.

(I have barely had any dealings with Seizerians to begin with, right? At most there was Boobs Lady, and I returned her sword properly.)

They might have reason to see me as an enemy simply because I was a monster, but if they knew what I could do in a fight, they would surely avoid a pointless battle.

Unless Boobs Lady turned out to be someone with noble-level authority, they were not likely to try to subjugate me without caring about casualties.

I watched them scatter like startled spiders while I just stood there, then lumbered off across the night grasslands.

My current objective was to improve the quality of my diet.

I had absolutely no need to chase them.

I definitely was not saying that just because, before I knew it, they had taken the one option I could not counter.

However, it was clear I could no longer make use of this route.

(If I was going to change locations… where would be good?)

Naturally, a highway with caravans going back and forth would be ideal.

But in such well-maintained places, there were almost no spots where I could hide this huge body of mine.

That was why I had chosen such a remote area in the first place, but—

(If I could not use this place anymore, then my next options were… yeah, I had one.)

There was a town that fit the bill perfectly.

It was a town that did not exist in my old knowledge, probably built for southern development.

Given its purpose, people and supplies were likely being sent there in droves.

Looking back, I had overlooked that convenient spot because I was worried about “hate control”, but now that our mutual hostility was beyond doubt, I had no hesitation about shifting my activities there.

(Although this did mean my actions would end up benefiting Seizeria even more…)

“Well, no need to worry about it,” I decided, and started moving toward my new destination.

Since I was less restricted at night, I figured I might as well get as close as possible.

And so, by the time dawn broke, I was in the forest.

For the time being, I had headed almost straight south toward the forest of the former Imperial territory, but that land was now claimed by both Canaan and Seizeria.

Obviously I could not just stroll right into such a place, so I veered west and ended up here.

(Man, that fortress really had a heavy atmosphere.)

Apparently, Carnash Fortress, which the Empire had once seized, was now in the hands of Canaan.

It looked like the reinforcement work would take a bit longer to finish, but as a frontline base against Seizeria, the number of people there was on a whole different level.

Even if I had become a monster, I had absolutely no intention of actively interfering in human conflicts.

So, being the socially aware monster I was, I avoided the fortress and stuck to the forest.

For the moment, I searched the area to secure breakfast and a place to sleep.

I did not find anywhere suitable as a bed, but I did manage to catch not one but two chickens.

There was no reason for them to be out here, so they were definitely livestock that had escaped.

I had let them get away last time, but this time I was going to enjoy them properly.

Wild meat had its own charm, but meat raised by human hands was something else entirely.

With that settled, I quickly processed and butchered them.

Plucking the feathers was a real chore, but by the time the meat was roasting, that effort would turn into a pleasant memory.

I secured a spot to build a fire, piled up stones to make a simple stove, then fed in dry leaves and branches as fuel and lit it.

I set a metal plate over it, oiled the surface, and waited for it to heat up.

The moment arrived—I laid the big cuts of chicken onto the hot plate.

The sound of the meat sizzling and the smell rising up put a smile on my face.

The seasoning was only salt, but the meal was thoroughly satisfying.

The difference in meat quality really was obvious.

Meat raised as livestock by humans simply tasted better.

After a short rest following the meal, I began cleaning up and felt someone’s gaze on me.

In reality, I was not actually sensing their gaze; it just felt like I was being watched from the sounds and smells, but saying I “felt a gaze” sounded cooler, so I went with that.

(Not human… but definitely bipedal.)

Given those conditions, it was probably that green guy.

They were not the type of creature that should have been active this early in the morning, so it was probably just a nearby individual drawn in by the smell of my cooking.

For those who lived in the forest, exterminating goblins on sight was basic manners.

I had just come up with that, but there really was no benefit to letting them live, and the longer you left them alive, the more the disadvantages piled up.

Just then, the goblin caught sight of me and bolted.

I let out a small breath and paused my cleaning.

With the metal plate in one hand and my pack on my back, I set off after the fleeing goblin.

They basically never acted alone.

There was almost certainly a pack waiting wherever it was running to, so I would let it lead me there and then wipe them out.

+++

# **123**

Lumbering after the fleeing goblin, I arrived at a small pack.

There were only seven of them in total, a small group that had been screeching noisily but fell silent the moment they saw me.

I calmly set down my baggage, unfastened the large saber tied to my pack, and gave it a light swing.

The goblins bolted.

They tried to scatter, but because they had made their base in terrain chosen to guard against raids, their escape routes were limited.

Using it as a defensive position without knowing their place had sealed their fate.

The green filth was annihilated before it could fully scatter and run.

“Hm, having a weapon really was nice.”

I swung the saber, shaking off the blood clinging to it.

My technique was little more than copying what I had seen in comics, but with this weight and the hardness of the metal, just smashing and cutting with it was more than strong enough.

It was a rare weapon that could withstand my full strength—this had been its first real outing, but… goblins as opponents were far from satisfying.

If anything, it barely felt like I had used it at all.

Even as a test cut, I should have chosen my targets a bit more carefully.

As I maintained the saber, I checked the goblins’ nest.

The result was nothing.

As expected from the small numbers, it seemed to be a newly formed pack.

We were not that far from either Canaan or Seizeria, so I had hoped there might be some loot, but for something freshly formed, this was about all there was to it.

Leaving the spot and retracing my path, I quickly found the place where I had cooked my meat.

I resumed cleaning up and scrubbed the area so thoroughly that not even a trace remained.

If I went that far, there should be no chance of a forest fire.

Now I could head to my target town with an easy mind.

I moved through the forest on alert, and by the time the sun was overhead, I picked up the sound of footsteps.

(Headcount… three. Hm, one of them was a bit heavy? A heavily armed warrior type mixed in?)

Maybe I was just getting used to distinguishing sounds, but lately I had become able to estimate weight like this.

They were definitely bipedal.

And when people walked normally, I could tell by the sound of branches snapping underfoot.

If someone moved as cautiously as a scout it would be harder to tell, but this time I would use the three I had locked onto to see how accurate I was.

I activated my mimicry ability, blended into the surrounding scenery, and moved quietly so as not to make a sound.

(Seeing it in practice, knowing human hearing really was a huge advantage.)

Because I used to be human and knew those specs in detail, I could pull tricks like this; information was everything.

I predicted their direction of travel, got ahead of them, and hid while I waited for them to enter my field of vision.

There was a slight discrepancy, but within about ten minutes I had them in sight.

(Two lightly armed warrior types and one heavily armed one—just as I thought.)

I was honestly pleased my prediction had been right, but it was disappointing that they were all men.

And it made me wonder what a meatheaded group with clearly no designated scout was doing in this forest.

I tried to gather information by listening closely, but no one spoke.

(For people supposedly searching for something, their formation was weird. I could tell they were wary of their surroundings, but…)

In a word, the three of them were jittery.

They kept glancing around, and their faces clearly showed fear.

(…I see, they got separated from their comrades.)

That explanation fit all the bits of information neatly.

In that case, I did also want to know the cause, but there were any number of reasons why a bargain-bin mercenary band in cheap-looking gear might end up like that.

Still, there was also the possibility that some unexpected powerful enemy was lurking nearby.

“This is all from a human-standard point of view, so none of it applied to me, though,” I thought, striking a pose in my head with a grin—when the three of them did something I had not expected.

They pointed in the opposite direction from where I was and let out screams that were almost shrieks, then started running straight toward me.

Since I was still using mimicry, I broke eye contact for a moment, then slowly stepped out from behind a large tree.

One of the mercenaries noticed me and let out a pathetic scream, yet still did not stop running my way.

In other words, whatever was chasing them right now was more threatening than I was.

(Oh… so I was the better option, huh…)

I watched them, hand on my chin, as one of the heavily armed men tripped.

Tears streaming down his face, he reached out and begged for help, but the two lightly armed men completely ignored him and kept running straight toward me.

That was when I saw it.

Or rather, I supposed I should say I ended up seeing it.

The instant I witnessed the mass of black “things” swarming over the fallen man, a chill ran not just down my spine but through my entire body.

At the same time, the two who had been sprinting flat out blew right past me.

(These bastards were trying to use me as a decoy!?)

Even for me, the odds were bad against those things.

Actually, there was no way I ever wanted to fight them without preparation.

With the screams of the man who was probably still being eaten echoing behind me, I started running away as well, a bit late to the party.

The moment I caught up to the two in front, I grabbed each of them firmly by an arm.

I could have used them as bait, but that would not even buy me much time.

So I threw the two of them off in different directions.

If either one survived and made it back to town, they would definitely report what they had seen.

(I would leave dealing with those things to humans. Two hundred years ago, every country had a way to handle them; they should be fine.)

I had the feeling one of them ended up stuck in a tree where I had thrown him, but as long as the other survived, that was all that mattered.

Shaking off the swarm chasing me, I finally managed to catch my breath and checked the sun’s position.

Apparently I had kept running east, so I decided I might as well keep going that way and head for a river.

“I never thought it would be a magic insect swarm…”

Given the conditions, it had certainly been possible for one to form, but I had never expected to actually run into it.

Basically, even when those things appeared, in most cases they naturally died out through cannibalism and almost never made it into inhabited areas.

But if you ever let them into a town, they would eat everything in sight and their numbers would explode.

In the past, they had been feared as something that could cause damage on a scale that went far beyond a single nation.

Nowadays, though, every country had firmly established countermeasures, so it was standard practice not to let them anywhere near, let alone into, a town.

At the end of the day, they were still just insects.

There were endless ways to deal with them: lure them, burn them, blow them to pieces.

Unfortunately, I currently had no means of facing those things myself.

So my only move was to run.

(Still, that did mean I would have to make a detour to reach my target.)

I scratched the back of my head and let out a long sigh.

First, I would get to the river and refill my water.

I might as well catch some fish while I was at it.

So I kept pushing eastward through the forest, and by this point I was close enough to the old base where I had met Boobs Lady before that I was tempted to swing by.

I did not think I had missed anything, but there might be some wonderful new encounter waiting there.

By the time the sun dipped low and it was evening, there I was, busily exterminating the goblins that had sprouted up again around the base ruins.

+++

# **124**

After finishing off the goblins that had sprung up around the base ruins, I looked around to see if there was anything else.

What I found was a human skeleton that still had some meat clinging to it.

(They got eaten again… aren’t the hunters’ standards dropping a bit lately?)

Still, if someone had recently died here, that meant there had to be some kind of item left behind.

So I searched a bit more carefully and found a backpack that seemed to belong to the corpse from earlier, who looked to be male based on the clothing.

Unfortunately, there was nothing impressive—just a fire-starting magic tool and some rations.

I should have realized beforehand that “this was about what you would expect from a beginner hunter who lost to goblins.”

While I was checking if there was anything else just in case, I confirmed that the spot where I had once found the hunters’ gear was now completely empty.

Maybe some newbie, tempted by relics, had come solo all the way out here and then been gang-beaten to death by goblins.

If things had already been mostly looted by the time he arrived, then the fact that his sad end came after a completely wasted trip was a pretty harsh story.

Anyway, given all that, I should have been done here already… and yet I was still wandering around the base ruins like this.

Why?

In a word, because there was a strange smell.

Sweet, maybe? In any case, that oddly cloying smell was bothering me, and I was roaming around trying to pinpoint its source.

There was one more thing—it was the certainty that “something is here” that kept me pinned to this spot.

(…Was it hiding and watching me? My sound-based detection was not picking it up, and the smell was useless thanks to this scent. It even felt like my senses were being precisely suppressed… was that just my imagination?)

It was entirely possible it was just a coincidence.

I had been drifting around the base ruins to see how the other side would move, but there had been no approach from them at all.

The idea that the smell was actually a poison and they were just waiting for me to collapse was plausible enough that it was starting to creep me out a little, but it seemed the other side ran out of patience first.

I heard a faint sound—at least enough to tell that it was footsteps.

The problem was that it clearly was not human, and also did not seem to be an animal.

In that case, there was only one answer.

(A monster… and an insect-type at that.)

Judging from the weight, I guessed it was about human-sized, so there was no need to run, but if I chose to fight, it would be a dangerous opponent I could not afford to underestimate.

Anyone who knew how insects had evolved in nature would inevitably be shocked by their diversity.

Now imagine those insects made into monsters—larger, tougher—of course I had to be cautious.

“Insect-type monsters,” the Empire had once said, “have many weaknesses, but in exchange they were overwhelmingly strong in certain environments or conditions.”

And not just insects—living things always settled in environments suited to them.

I set down my pack and reorganized its contents so I could easily grab my potion bottles, then unhooked the sword and shield attached to it and equipped them.

Even if it barely retained its original form, this had once been a military base.

With so many obstacles, this terrain was by no means a good place for me to fight.

(I would at least grant it the advantage of the terrain. If this was a good opponent to test this gear on, that much disadvantage was acceptable.)

I waited in silence.

Since the opponent was an insect, there was no point trying to play mind games.

My only options were to steadily flip over each card in its hand and figure it out as I went, or to flip the whole board by brute force.

And then the enemy, unable to restrain its instincts, moved.

It was a sound like the air being torn—something almost impossible to see lunged at me from behind.

I easily knocked it aside with my shield and caught a glimpse of the attacker out of the corner of my eye.

(…A stinger? No, a tail!)

It was thin and long like a thread, extending toward me from a single point.

I turned in that direction and managed to briefly catch sight of something as it slipped out of my line of sight.

“That shape… a mantis?”

The distinctive silhouette made me say it out loud, but if that was the case, then what had that previous attack been?

I was thinking, “A new species, or maybe…” when a certain possibility crossed my mind.

“This clearly went beyond the Genetically Enhanced Soldier Project framework… if anything, it would fall under the Chimera Project, right?”

Once I had seen it even once, I was not about to lose track.

I raised my shield, inched closer, and readied myself for the next attack.

“Hey, can you hear me? If you can, say something—anything.”

I called out, allowing for one particular possibility—what I got in return was another strike from that tail-like thing, once again from behind.

I used my shield to bat it away and reclassified my opponent as a simple monster.

Now I could fight without holding back, though there was also a strong sense of disappointment.

There was a chance I had just seen my biggest cause for concern resolve itself in the most unexpected way, so there was no helping that feeling.

(So, it seemed to be a monster that was not in my knowledge at all… a new species, or did I just not know about it?)

“Probably the latter,” I said with a self-deprecating chuckle at my own lack of knowledge, and took a step forward.

In that instant, something lunged toward my ankle—I stopped it on reflex with my saber, but could not land a counter and it slipped away behind a pile of rubble.

It had been a strike with a scythe-like limb; if my reaction had been any slower, who knew what would have happened?

(It was faster than I expected. And even when I blocked it with this, I did not cut through it.)

My opponent was extremely hard.

On top of that, its speed and power were more than adequate, making it a perfect training partner for a fully armed me.

It seemed to favor hiding and ambushing, but it was clearly not the kind of enemy that relied mainly on tricks like poison or webs the way Spider-Man-type monsters did.

In that case, there were any number of ways to force it into a straightforward fight.

(First, I would close the distance in one go!)

No matter how good it was at hiding, if I charged straight at it, I could catch it before it hid.

That long, needle-like tail it could extend freely was easy enough to deal with—the sound gave away its position completely, and my shield could handle it just fine.

Like an idiot repeating the same move, I knocked aside three more attacks from behind with my shield and thundered forward at a run to close the gap.

And then, up ahead, I grinned and said, “Found you.”

In simple terms, it was “an insect that crossed a wasp with a mantis.”

A monster shaped roughly like that—wasp abdomen with mantis scythes and head—though the details differed from any insect I remembered, was once again trying to hide behind a collapsed structure when I spotted it.

It was on the far side of a single wall.

In that case, there was only one thing to do.

I took a short running start, jumped—and with a flying kick, smashed through the wall and leapt in front of my target as I swung my saber.

Unfortunately, I was just out of range to land a direct hit on its body, but I did manage to knock off the arm bearing the scythe and throw it off balance.

As if in retaliation, it thrust its abdomen forward and fired the stinger at its tip straight at me.

It was an attack so quiet it was almost eerie, but my tower shield met it with a ringing clang of metal on metal and stopped it cold.

“I see… so that was what you looked like.”

I finally got a full frontal view of it, and it matched my first impression exactly.

Which meant there was a high probability that the stinger on its wasp abdomen was poisonous.

There was another point I had to watch out for, but actually dealing with it would not be that hard.

As I stood there, composed and ready, my target spread its arms wide in an attempt to make itself look bigger and took on a threatening stance.

Did this little bug really think it could beat me, the crystallization of Imperial technology?

Oh, but swarms were a hard no.

Those things tried to pour into every hole in your body and tear you apart from the inside, so I really did not ever want to deal with them again.

+++

# **125**

It turned into a not-so-epic game of tag between me, who wanted a head-on fight, and the bug, which wanted nothing to do with a frontal clash.

Just when I was all fired up, thinking “All right, the real fight starts now that I’ve seen your whole body!” it turned tail and ran at full speed.

I could not help thinking, “So what was that intimidating pose of yours supposed to be?” but I was practically near the top of the natural food chain here.

Man, being strong really was a crime, I thought, remembering how something else had run away from me just like this before.

Caught completely off guard, I shouted, “Don’t run!” and gave chase, but every time I closed in, that stupidly long, string-like tail with the stinger would lash out, forcing me to stop and block it and keeping me from forcing it into close combat.

To make things worse, despite being just a bug, it made excellent use of the terrain.

Plainly put, my body size was working against me.

Even in ruins, this had once been a military base, so there were still a fair number of structures, with plenty of spots I simply could not squeeze through.

It used those cleverly, pulling away from me every time.

However, once a spot had been used once, I “expanded” it enough that I could pass through as well.

It could not reuse the same routes, and the distance between us steadily shrank, which made me grin; at last, I knocked aside the tail’s attack from behind with my tower shield without stopping and leapt with my saber raised.

With a blow that landed the instant I touched down, one of the bug’s legs ended up noticeably shorter when it failed to dodge in time.

Going straight into a follow-up, I stepped forward as if canceling my landing motion and swung my shield like a punch, but my strike met its counterattack square on, so I switched to defense.

I blocked the scythe strike and, as I knocked it upward, I brought my saber up in a rising cut.

As I pressed the attack, driving forward and forward, the now familiar tail with its stinger came lunging in from behind.

So I answered with my own tail.

Instead of stupidly knocking away just the tip, I smacked down the string-like thin part and pinned it to the ground.

Even then, it tried to correct its course and come flying at me, so I found myself wondering if this stinger was some kind of wired autonomous weapon.

Remembering a scene from a robot anime where wired and wireless weapons zipped around trading blows, I lopped off its scythe-bearing arm.

While I was at it, I tried to finally deal with that annoying tail and sever it with a series of follow-up strikes, but it felt like rubber and I could not cut through.

(I see… that trajectory came from its elasticity. Of course it would be like this.)

I analyzed my opponent as accurately as I could and steadily inflicted damage.

My saber swung again and again, but because it was made of a material that could withstand my strength, its stability was incredible and I could clash with not even a single chip in the blade.

With my sword or my shield, I knocked aside the last remaining scythe, and handled the occasional tail strike the same way.

This time I took one of its legs as well, to make sure it could not escape.

Carefully, without inflicting any fatal wounds, I kept the practice dummy in front of me from breaking as I guided the fight exactly the way I wanted.

Once I judged I had gained enough experience, I knocked aside its damaged scythe with my shield and, at the same time, brought my sword down.

Its final arm spun through the air—and the mantis-wannabe tried to flee on its three remaining legs.

I took one big step forward and cut off its head.

In the end, it all felt a bit anticlimactic.

Its coloring made it look like it might be poisonous, but if it did not sting me, it did not matter.

Even if it had managed to sting me, it would have been meaningless against me, stocked up with potions as I was.

There was even a chance poison simply did not work on me at all, so it had never had any real chance of winning.

“Good experience,” I said, satisfied, and checked the state of my surroundings.

I felt like I might have gone a bit overboard with the demolition, but… well, it was not as if that caused any particular problem, so fine.

“And after a bug, I ran into yet another bug…”

Tilting my head, I wondered if some set of conditions for a mass outbreak had been met.

With that many magic insects, you would expect some sort of warning sign, but nothing came to mind.

(Well, I did not come to this area that often. At most I took out goblins…)

That was when I remembered.

Insects were one of goblins’ main food sources.

And how many goblins had I culled?

“I wonder… was that my fault?” I thought for a second, but there was no way such a dramatic shift would happen in just a month or two.

Of course, it was possible I had been one of the causes, but I doubted that alone would trigger something like that.

More likely, the conditions had already been almost completely met, and the drop in goblin numbers had been the final factor that let their numbers explode like that.

In other words, I had only given the last little push, which meant I was innocent.

If anything, the fact that this had happened not far from a town meant it would not be an exaggeration to say the real blame lay with the authorities for neglecting regional management.

Having won my acquittal, I raised both arms high.

Case closed.

That said, not even the Empire’s wisdom had fully clarified the conditions under which monsters appeared.

Patterns had been identified to some extent, but they were far too flimsy to serve as a solid basis.

Because science could not explain it, the Empire had enforced strict policies toward monsters—ironically, that very land was now a monster paradise.

With no meaningful results to show for it, I finished my investigation and left the former military base, now little more than wreckage and rubble.

The fact that I ran into another insect-type monster almost immediately afterward made it harder and harder to believe this was just a coincidence.

“Should I be looking into this?”

Muttering that, I walked past a horned grasshopper whose head had been cut off, leaving it motionless.

For now, as planned, I gave the magic insect swarm a wide berth and headed toward the town that served as the Kingdom of Canaan’s southern development frontline base.

Come to think of it, I wondered if that mercenary had managed to escape safely.

The next day, as I made a wide detour through the forest, I had an additional encounter with an insect-type monster.

It was mid-sized, so the fight ended quickly, but I was starting to worry that their numbers might be increasing on the eastern side of the forest.

While I was thinking about that and heading north, I also ran into a standard animal-type monster.

It was a monkey-type I had never seen before; when it looked down at me from a tree and laughed “gehya-gehya,” I knocked it down with a stone and then cut it cleanly in two.

My first throw made it move, and the second landed perfectly where it came down.

Even if it was a fluke, a win was still a win.

Incidentally, monster meat was generally bad, so if you were going to eat it, you had to process it thoroughly and put real effort into cooking.

Some monsters simply could not be made edible no matter what you did, so you had to be careful.

Remembering a note about monsters in a cookbook I had read recently, I buried the corpse and set off running again.

One thing and another later, I arrived safely to the west of my destination.

From here, the question was how to target the supply shipments… but first I needed to find a place that could serve as a bed.

Near the border between the former Imperial territory and the Kingdom of Canaan, it would not be surprising to find similar abandoned bases.

The sky was unfortunately overcast today, so I needed to look for somewhere with a roof.

+++

# **126**

Whether this really deserved to be called “a sheltered bed” was debatable, but I did find a spot that looked like it would be fine even if it rained.

In short, it was a ramshackle hut with about half its exterior boards missing.

There were traces that someone had once used it as a storage shed, and it had probably been built several decades ago.

(Judging from the state of the interior and the dust, it felt like it had been abandoned for at least ten years.)

I had no idea what purpose the Kingdom’s people had in mind when they built a storage hut way out here, but if no one was using it now, then I could make free use of it.

Its durability and appearance left a lot to be desired, but my luggage and at least half my body should have been safe from the rain.

Thinking that, I set down my pack and lowered myself to sit—only for the floor to give way.

My backside fit neatly into the hole, and I flicked my tail around in the space beneath.

My tail smashed into something with a sharp whip—and a shattering crash rang up through my rear at the same time.

I pulled my butt free for the moment, straightened up, and checked the space underneath.

“So that was a jar that broke, huh…”

It had been an empty jar, so there was no real problem, but from the remains it might have been a water jug or something similar.

Further inspection turned up a ladder and what looked like household items, so I guessed this had once been a living space.

Which raised the question of why a storage shed that needed such a space would be out in a place like this.

(It really was starting to feel like I was ticking all the boxes for “this was connected to some criminal organization,” wasn’t it.)

Driven by curiosity, I searched thoroughly and found a box that was heavily sealed and covered in dust.

I forced open the metal lock and peeked inside; what came out was a bag of unknown material that felt like it would be water-resistant—when I turned it upside down, fine white powder poured smoothly out.

“Yeah, that’s a total no-go!”

No matter how you looked at it, it was some kind of hard drug.

That confirmed this place had been part of a smuggling route for that sort of thing.

The fact that the goods were still here suggested the perpetrators had probably been arrested, I thought optimistically, and carefully buried the white powder in the soil to dispose of it.

I checked for any others just in case, but that seemed to be the only one left, which was a relief.

I smashed through the floor in grand fashion and used scrap wood and the like to create a space big enough for my massive body to sit.

I decided that was good enough for basic rain protection.

It was a truly shabby makeshift base, but the mere fact it had a roof put it above a lot of options.

“Luxury is the enemy,” I said with my mouth, while attacking people for the sake of luxury.

I was a monster, after all; it could not be helped.

In any case, by messing around with materials from the surroundings, I managed—not perfectly, but well enough—to hide the underground section.

Now I could leave my pack here and move around the area without worry.

There was still time until sunset, but I wanted to finish hunting for dinner early.

(Looked like it was going to rain. Come to think of it, I had not experienced rain in the forest since taking on this form.)

I remembered several days of rain back when I was in the elf village—or rather, when I was pretending to be under the control magic—but I had not experienced it in the forest itself even once.

It would not be a bad idea to observe what changes the rain brought.

The next morning—half my body stuck into the underground space, lying there like I was soaking in a bathtub—I woke up and looked up at the sky.

It was still overcast, but my ears caught the faint sound of raindrops falling and popping lightly.

It had rained as expected, but only as the lightest of drizzles.

“This much shouldn’t have much effect,” I said as I stood up—and then I saw, right where I was looking, a writhing mass of bugs.

I reflexively jerked back with a “whoa,” then realized the insects were swarming exactly where I had buried the white powder yesterday.

“…So that dangerous ‘medicine’ had been the bug-attracting kind, huh?”

Or maybe it simply had some smell they liked?

Watching it was not pleasant in the least, and the swarm was uncomfortably close to my makeshift base, so I mercilessly breathed fire and blew the whole clump of insects away.

Confirming that my magic training was going well, I grabbed a shovel, dug a hole, and moved the soil from where I had buried the drug over to that spot.

Then I covered it with more dirt and tamped it down with my feet to finish the job.

“Well then, let’s go get breakfast”—the moment I left my temporary base, I got hit by a full-on downpour.

I had figured, “The trees are all dense and leafy, it’ll be fine even if it rains a bit,” but the rain was much heavier than I had expected.

(At this rate I won’t even be able to start a fire… and what about the gear in my base?)

My worries proved justified, and I found a bit of water had seeped into the underground section.

For a moment I thought, “So this base was useless after all,” but on closer inspection I realized the real cause was that I had damaged the area around the floor when I smashed it open to make it easier for myself to get in.

I had not left my gear directly on the floor, so it was fine for now, but once the water started pooling, this place would become unusable.

Honestly, the world really did not go your way.

Just as I was thinking, “If only I had something waterproof right now,” I suddenly remembered the blue tarp I had tossed out for being in the way.

Was this the curse of the blue tarp I had written off as useless and thrown away?

Come to think of it, I dimly recalled deciding to bring it as a substitute rain cover.

For the moment, I moved only my luggage to a safe spot where it would not get wet and headed out to see if I could find something—or somewhere—better.

My hunt had been interrupted, so I was operating on an empty stomach, but skipping one meal did not really matter.

Being soaked by the rain only made me feel a little chilly at most, and in this body, even that was not something I truly needed to worry about.

However, I did discover a problem caused by the rain.

It was a decline in my ability to explore.

The sound of the rain interfered with sound-based detection.

Naturally, the same applied to smell, so in this state it was hard—almost impossible, really—to use my usual capabilities.

It was only because the downpour was this heavy, I thought, but it was still something I needed to keep in mind.

It might even be worth measuring just how much my abilities dropped at different levels of rainfall.

Since it was a perfect opportunity, I experimented with various things as I moved closer to the town—when suddenly, voices reached my ears.

(The distance wasn’t that great. Normally I would have picked them up much earlier.)

Keeping myself hidden, I crept toward the source of the sound.

The hammering rain made it impossible to make out what they were saying, but there were definitely two female voices.

I crouched low and moved slowly.

In my line of sight, beneath a large fallen tree leaning against the neighboring trunk, I saw two women.

They had made a fire in that rain-free spot; both removed the leather breastplates they were wearing and set them down next to their propped-up bows.

They looked to be hunters.

I hunched down further so they would not notice me and watched from behind a tree.

Downpouring rain, women soaked by it, a campfire at their side—in other words, there was only one thing that could happen next.

My chest swelled with anticipation as I watched their movements… and, just as I had expected, the two of them began taking off their wet jackets.

I pumped a tiny fist in a quiet victory pose and kept watching as they held their jackets out toward the fire with both hands.

But the two of them simply stayed there, practically frozen in place, warming themselves by the flames.

(…Huh? They’re not taking off their underwear?)

As I sat there disappointed, raindrops continued to fall mercilessly through the gaps in the branches and along the leaves.

+++

# **127**

After spotting the two women who looked like hunters, I watched them for a while… but nothing changed no matter how long I waited.

(…Strange. From the looks of them they did not seem like mercenaries, so I figured they were hunters, and with a two-woman team there should have been some deeper backstory or relationship going on—but nothing happened at all.)

If someone who made a living hunting wild animals chose to work not alone but in a more conspicuous group, I figured there had to be some reason.

However, judging from the fragments of conversation I could catch, they really did seem to be just coworkers.

In that case, it was only reasonable to assume they had just happened to end up together in this downpour.

There was no scene of friendship or affection that transcended gender like I had been hoping for; watching the two calmly exchange information, I let my shoulders sag in disappointment.

For the record, by my estimate, they were both a solid three on my personal scale. \[TN: the narrator rates women on his own 1–5 attractiveness scale.]

Their looks were slightly on the pretty side—one with short red hair and the other with long brown hair—but otherwise very ordinary.

Maybe it was inevitable, but thanks to my now overly refined tastes, I did not really feel like messing with them at all.

So I chose to behave and stick to stealth, slipping out of their line of sight and heading elsewhere.

Taking the chance to test my abilities under the handicap of bad weather, I moved closer to the town while observing things as I went.

Heading a bit farther north, I saw a road where the forest opened up.

Apparently I had ended up near the northern highway leading to the town.

(Well, that works in my favor, doesn’t it? If I walk along the highway I might find something, and if there are wagons I have a few things I want to try.)

Because I could not see the sun, my sense of direction had drifted a bit more than planned, but this was not a bad outcome in its own way.

On the highway, with nothing to block the rain, visibility was terrible and I could barely see the forest ahead.

That meant my ability to scout with sight, hearing, and smell was all restricted.

In this situation, the only real concern I had about being discovered was magical detection.

Even though I had started training in magic, I was still a novice.

The knowledge I had picked up in the elf village was far from sufficient, and since I had no clear idea how such magic might detect me, there was nothing I could do but react after the fact.

Incidentally, I had found that when I used my mimicry ability in the rain, my body became invisible but its outline still stood out.

Using that, I came up with the idea of tucking my tail between my legs, wrapping it around my waist to hide it, and walking on two legs to make it look like “some invisible giant is stomping around!”, but in the worst case that might expose my ability, so I decided to restrain myself.

In any case, I continued north along the highway for about ten minutes—when the trees began to thin out, I came to a stop.

(Any farther than this and I’d be at risk of being spotted…)

It was the combination of forest terrain and bad weather that created this terrible visibility.

Once I stepped out of the forest, there would not be that big a difference between my detection capabilities and a human’s.

If someone was moving loudly, like a wagon, or shouting at the top of their lungs, that would be a different story, but there was not much point planning around rare cases like that.

On the contrary, the existence of magic might even give them the advantage.

So this time, I decided to focus on grasping positional information and put risk avoidance first.

Realizing just how troublesome bad weather could be was enough of a gain all by itself.

I shifted my course south, retracing my steps while keeping an ear on the highway, and before long the town came into view without me finding anything in particular.

Even at this distance, I could only just make out the faint silhouettes of sentries.

Even using my telescopic vision did not change much, which reminded me again how important the environment was.

Keeping the trees as cover, I circled the town a little.

To be safe, I activated my mimicry ability.

At this distance, even if someone looked my way, they would not notice me.

When I made it halfway around to the south gate, there was a crowd of people and what looked like an argument.

(Oh, maybe news about the magic insect swarm already reached them?)

I hoped things were going the way I wanted and left the area.

Having seen what I needed to see, I headed back to my temporary base.

I just hoped my gear had not gotten wet… but more than that, the sudden realization hit me that someone might come use the place for shelter from the rain, and I sprinted through the forest in a panic.

The next morning, the weather still had not changed, and the rain was coming down in an annoyingly heavy pour.

“Well, when it rains, it rains. That’s just how it is,” I thought, having reached a state of resignation.

When I had hurried back the day before, my luggage was safe and there were no signs of anyone.

Using scrap wood, I did some quick repairs and managed to secure a space where I could start a fire, then grilled up a rabbit I had caught on the way.

I spent the night burning the dry scrap wood as fuel.

My magic training was going well, but this base was too uncomfortable to devote another whole day solely to that.

So, even if it was inefficient, today’s plan was exploration and training under adverse weather conditions.

(If I found a spot better suited than this one, I’d move. If I didn’t, I’d just keep training—that was how it would go.)

That was what I had in mind, but before I knew it, I was attacking a lone wagon on the highway.

Crouched over the cargo bed and rummaging through it, I felt like I was wearing a wicked grin that said, “Heh heh heh, traveling alone is dangerous, kid.”

Ignoring the young man who had collapsed on his backside and could not move, I rifled through his cargo.

He was wearing rain gear, but since he was sitting directly on the ground, he was soaked through.

As for the cargo that mattered… the smell when I stuck my head in told me instantly it was a dud.

He was transporting materials.

Among the parts stripped from monsters, there were some that could be processed into weapons or turned into various tools.

He was probably hauling items that could not be processed in that town.

The food I found was all rations in the form of dried meat and hardtack, nothing that made me want to take it.

(I’d rather grill fresh meat than eat Canaan’s rations.)

With a deep sigh, I walked away without taking anything.

I was not worried about silencing him.

The truth was, I had turned back because there was a magic insect swarm blocking the highway, and that was when I ran into him.

Unfortunately, he and the horse pulling his wagon would end up as bug food.

“Seriously, hurry up and move, Canaan guy. With those things around, my options get limited too, you know.”

Grumbling like that, I went in search of prey for lunch.

Just like yesterday, finding anything was almost entirely down to luck.

Wild animals carefully moved to avoid me.

I knew I was making noise thanks to my massive body, but… it seemed I still was not wild enough.

Whenever I tried to focus on separating out sounds, my other senses got sloppy and I would bump my shoulder into a tree or something, making more noise.

It was pretty difficult, but if I could pull it off, I would definitely be leveling up.

Just as I psyched myself up and started searching for prey, what I found instead was clothing soaked in blood—and still not dry.

(Though with this rain I guess it wouldn’t dry anyway… but does this mean even the bones got completely eaten?)

The faint traces remaining around the area, a bow, a short sword, and a familiar-looking leather breastplate—it might have belonged to one of the two women I had seen yesterday.

(…So they had come over to this side. Or there were people on this side too.)

If that was the case, I had to consider another possibility.

Namely, that the swarm had been created artificially.

If so, I would have to ask, “Why did someone need to do something like this?”

(No, I didn’t need to ask.)

The answer was obvious.

“So they used bugs as a countermeasure against me, huh.”

In that mutter, admiration outweighed disgust.

“If you can’t beat something with humans, just throw something non-human at it”—a bold decision.

I did not know if this was their primary plan or just a long shot, but the information the human side would gain from the outcome was by no means small.

“Ha! Not bad at all. There are no treaties when you’re dealing with monsters. You don’t have to be picky about your methods!”

If this really had been done on purpose, I would have to reevaluate the Kingdom of Canaan.

I had thought of them as nothing more than something to be trampled.

But a country was still a country, however rotten.

At this point, I revised that understanding.

“I’ll gladly accept that challenge.”

Why did the simple knowledge that I had an enemy make my spirits soar like this?

Considering how I had been born, maybe it was only natural—but that also meant there was a chance the time I could remain human was being eaten away.

Things were not going my way, but my mood was sky-high.

I clearly needed an immediate mental checkup.

“Is there a beautiful woman in her mid-twenties with a killer figure, loads of sex appeal, and a white lab coat in the house?”

+++

# **128 - A Certain Former Mercenary’s Perspective**

“When did things go this wrong?”

Maybe things had just been going too smoothly up until now, but my life was currently in the middle of a very clear downward spiral.

I remembered a fellow mercenary once telling me, “Rock bottom’s got two, three floors under what you think it is. As long as you can still say ‘I’m at rock bottom right now’, you’re not there yet.” Imagining that I could still fall further from here made a long sigh escape me on its own.

“Quit looking so damned gloomy, prisoner.”

Yeah. Right now, I was not a mercenary, but a prisoner locked in a cage.

I had disbanded my mercenary company, divvied up the money (left that part to someone else), and thought I had made a clean getaway—only to come to my senses shackled and rattling along in the back of a wagon.

When I ignored the order to report in and tried to run for it with everything I had, they had already cut me off ahead of time. Funny how they were only competent at things like that.

I tugged at the chains on my arms, but all I got for my trouble was the sound of metal scraping metal. I could not break them, and even the cheap-looking brackets that held them in place did not so much as twitch.

My main charges were obstruction of duty and destruction of property.

They were the end result of me thrashing around trying to escape.

(If any of the others were here, they’d be saying “What are you doing, boss?” right about now.)

Barely three days after parting ways with my comrades, I was already in this sorry state.

I did not even have my prized magic sword, bought with money I had saved up bit by bit, in my hands anymore; no wonder my face looked so grim.

“Hey, where are we headed, anyway?”

I tried asking one of the soldiers riding alongside on horseback, but he did not answer.

We had been heading straight north from Lecole, so I figured the destination had to be either Aquia or Tore… I doubted it was the royal capital.

(No way they’d haul me all the way to the capital. And there’s no reason to… right?)

I knew I was not educated, and I knew full well I was not that smart either.

In the end, I decided thinking about it would not change anything and gave up on it.

I settled in to try and get some rest in the rattling, uncomfortable wagon until we reached wherever we were going.

The next day—we arrived at a port town called Eilke.

While my anxiety about “what on earth was waiting for me” simmered in the background, they took me out of the caged wagon with only my hands still in shackles and marched me through the gate into town.

Following my escorts, I reached a needlessly huge mansion—the sort of place people would call a “guesthouse.”

Which meant whoever had business with me was some big shot.

“A noble, huh… just my luck,” I muttered, and a soldier behind me snapped, “Watch your tongue.”

(Chances are… no, almost without a doubt, I was about to be roped into something unpleasant.)

With a feeling that was more like near-certainty than a hunch, I let out a loud, theatrical sigh, which they completely ignored.

Saying anything was not going to improve my situation anyway.

As the iron gates opened and we stepped into the grounds, I felt eyes on me.

Probably from inside the building—most likely someone sizing me up from a safe distance.

At the very least, I could say for sure they were not going to kill me on the spot.

(The real question is “what are they going to make me do?”… and I can pretty much guess.)

With nobles involved, that meant their authority and ability to force people’s hands got added to the mix.

The men on either side of me were swapped out—from the soldiers who had brought me here to the mansion’s own guards—and as soon as we went inside I could feel the chill of the building materials on my skin.

Stripped of my gear, wearing only a shirt and trousers, the cold floor bit more than a little.

I went out of my way to slap my bare feet against the stone with loud, wet smacks as I walked, but nobody reacted.

The guy next to me was stomping along even louder, so it was only natural no one cared about my little bit of attitude.

After walking barefoot over that cold floor for a while, we stopped in front of a door.

One of the men flanking me jerked his chin toward it and ordered, “In.”

There was no point in resisting now, so I quietly obeyed, opened the door with my shackled hands, and strode in like a proper mercenary—head held high.

Inside were a well-fed, well-dressed man who was clearly the master of the house—and one other man who matched him as a “guard” about as well as a silk glove on a butcher’s hand.

The second man had blond hair and blue eyes, a handsome face, and a build that was downright delicate for someone expected to fight. He barely looked older than a young man.

To call him a “bodyguard” felt… generous, to put it mildly.

(But the very fact that someone like him was here, out of place as he was, said everything about what he really was…)

He looked nothing like the master of the house, which made it unlikely they were blood relatives.

Which meant he had to be someone directly related to why I was here.

Once we finished sizing each other up, the first to speak was the plump, middle-aged man in the very noble-looking outfit.

“Well, I imagine you have some idea why we went to the trouble of bringing you all the way here,” he began, scratching his slicked-back, greying hair with one hand and making no effort to hide the disdain on his face, as if “I don’t want to bother with some mercenary scum” were printed across his forehead.

“The new species, I take it?”
