“Correct. This new monster they have named ‘Argos’… it seems the southern part of our kingdom lies within its hunting grounds. We intend to slay it.”

“With all due respect…”

“I have no interest in your opinion. Just do as you are told.”

I swallowed the sigh that nearly escaped and looked up at the ceiling.

By the third time, I really did not feel like I had any chance of coming back alive.

“…Understood. So… will that gentleman over there be the commander?”

I shifted my gaze to the refined-looking man, and our eyes met.

He smiled softly and just watched me in silence, saying nothing.

“Allow me to introduce him. If you’re a mercenary, I’m sure you’ve at least heard the name. This is Alberto von Raguel.”

At those words, the refined man took a step toward me and bowed with practiced grace.

“As introduced, my name is Alberto. People in the streets insist on calling me a ‘hero’, but if you must address me, I would be delighted if you would use this instead—”

“Dragonbuster,” he added, the corners of his mouth curling up into a grin.

“Dragonbuster”—not a title meaning “one who slew a dragon,” but “one who drove a dragon off.”

Three years ago, during the attacks in the north, mercenary bands famous and unknown alike had gathered on that battlefield, and even within our own ranks there had been a bitter struggle over who would claim that title.

They had dangled money and glory in front of us—bait about as simple as it got—and drawn people in from far and wide, only for the end result to be a battlefield so horrific that a casualty rate of over thirty percent was considered “fitting.”

My own company, Dawn’s Battlefield, had taken part as well, but because of a job we were already on at the time, we arrived late—whether that was good luck or bad luck, who could say.

Thanks to that, we did not suffer much damage, but it had still hit me a little hard to find out that two mercenary companies we knew well had been wiped out.

We only had the chance to join in the fighting twice.

Even so, based on the information we gathered, we were able to predict the dragon’s movements and ram Giant-Killer into it over and over, so our contribution had to be higher than many of the lesser mercenary bands.

As the wounds on the dragon’s body multiplied and its movements started to lose their sharpness, the army and the mercenaries together succeeded in forcing the fight into a stalemate.

Everyone believed that if they just kept up the pressure, the dragon would eventually retreat—we all fully expected victory.

And that was when the long-awaited moment finally arrived.

The dragon spread its wings, lowered its body, and braced its legs.

The instant it went into the preparatory motion for takeoff, right before the cheers of victory could erupt—this man smashed a blow into the side of its head.

You could praise it as a magnificent surprise attack—so clean that even the dragon, which had already been cut more than once, had not sensed his approach.

But on that field, it had been an incredibly foolish act, one that could have led to the fighting continuing.

It was a reckless strike that could easily have reignited the dragon’s will to fight; in the end, the fact that its massive body rose into the air and flew northward allowed everyone to let it go without comment.

We celebrated being alive and praised the victory with everyone else who had survived.

But what came next was betrayal, enacted in the middle of those celebrations.

The army announced Alberto, who had delivered that one last dangerous blow, as the greatest hero of the battle—and then cheated the mercenaries out of their pay.

Naturally, the backlash had been enormous.

Things had gotten so tense that the bigwigs present at the time had fled for their lives; that was how ready to kill we had been.

Then, one young noble died, and one already half-destroyed mercenary band was erased as the supposed culprit.

Whether that story had been true or not did not matter.

What mattered was that they had used it as an excuse to force through their own demands—that was undeniable fact.

In other words, for the mercenaries who had been on that battlefield, this Alberto was “the man who used us as bait and only landed the final blow as the dragon tried to pull out,” and at the same time, “the man who cooperated with the nobles’ schemes and helped wipe out multiple mercenary companies.”

He was nothing more than a Dragonbuster manufactured for political convenience—the one who stole the honor and rewards of an entire battlefield.

The mercenaries and their guild did not recognize Alberto’s achievements.

If anything, they openly treated him as an enemy.

‘And now I was going to be used to give that man a new feather in his cap.’

“Man… Lloyd’s prediction hit the nail on the head.”

I remembered the words my longest-serving comrade had said to me on the day I disbanded the company.

“Captain, they are definitely going to use you up. If you don’t want to die, you have to run and keep running, no matter what.”

Letting out one long sigh, I swung the beloved sword that had been returned to my hand.

The armor they had prepared for me was better quality than what I usually wore as well.

“How much longer are you going to take…? We’re going,” said the man every mercenary had been taught to despise, hurrying me along.

I left the room.

“Can’t believe I ended up with a collar on…”

I ran a finger over the magic device locked around my neck.

This time, I really might be done for.

+++

# **129 - A Certain Hero’s Monologue**

I had to admit it—even saying it myself, I was a genius.

It wasn’t arrogance or anything; it really was true.

Ever since I was a kid, whatever people had me do, I was above average.

And if I got serious, people showered me with, “He’s a genius! A child prodigy!”

On top of that, this face—yeah, being popular was such a pain.

I could do anything and I was this handsome… honestly, I scared myself.

When I strutted around like that, a noble set his sights on me.

Luckily, he only noticed my talent and thought, “If we train him early, maybe he’ll be useful for something,” that sort of casual idea, so I was treated pretty well overall.

So for about five years, I trained at House Harks—the marquis family that took me in.

Well, within the first year there was no one left who could beat me.

From around then, the way they treated me improved dramatically.

I figured out pretty fast that they intended to keep me tame and under their thumb.

I had no intention of letting that happen, but given who they were, I couldn’t rebel carelessly.

That was one thing I just resigned myself to, thinking, “Maybe there really is nothing I can do about this.”

Oh, but I still tried various little things to stay in the marquis’s good graces.

But come on—the result being “your first real campaign is against a dragon” was just ridiculous, right?

I had cut down a few bandits and such before, you know?

Even so, finally getting my big debut on the real stage and having it be a dragon was a bit much.

“Wait, so I’m already disposable?”

I was shaken enough that the words slipped right out of my mouth.

When I asked for the details, it turned out they wanted to make me a Dragonbuster—or, if things went perfectly, even a Dragonslayer.

But becoming a Dragonslayer was clearly impossible.

If you asked whether I could really pull off a feat that, up to now, only existed in fairy tales, I could only say, “It would be difficult.”

I didn’t say, “I can’t do it.”

But I had no reason to risk my life for it.

I understood the desire to “prepare a hero” for the coming war with Seizeria, but I figured we should be realistic instead of greedy.

The adult world really sucked… well, I was going to be twenty soon anyway.

I’d long since been an adult, but being dragged around for someone else’s convenience was something I still couldn’t learn to like.

Even so, I had no right to refuse.

They had let me live in luxury for years; I owed them, so I decided to steel myself.

Except—there was a twist to the story.

“We’ll wear it down enough using the army and the mercenaries. If possible, finish it off. If that’s impossible, then one way or another, you must claim the final blow.”

I understood what he meant instantly.

I was a genius, after all—of course I understood.

No matter what, the mercenaries were going to hate me for it.

I was going to swoop in and steal all the glory they had fought and died for—that was how it looked. When people throw their lives away and lose comrades, only for someone else to snatch the prize at the end, of course they are going to hate that person.

And I was the one who had to do it.

I wasn’t thrilled about it, but I had no choice.

We arrived on the field—and it was pandemonium.

Bodies were scattered everywhere, and heavily wounded who couldn’t move lay all around.

In the middle of that, I slipped forward in hiding alongside the “associate” the marquis had saddled me with.

Apparently the marquis didn’t actually want me to die, because he sent someone truly skilled to guard me.

I heard the man had fled from Seizeria, the magic kingdom… he’d apparently been a disciple of a pretty famous master and was extremely gifted at concealment.

I mean, when I realized we could get close enough to touch the dragon and it still didn’t notice us, I couldn’t help blurting out, “Magic really is amazing.”

Sadly, it seemed I had no talent for using magic myself.

According to the old man, bloodlines mattered a lot in that regard, and that was just how things were.

Once in a very great while, someone with innate talent appeared, but unless that talent was discovered in early childhood and trained properly, it rotted away easily.

In other words, as an adult, I no longer had any real connection to magic.

Anyway, from a safe zone, hidden from sight, we watched the battle between the dragon and the humans.

Stray attacks came flying our way several times, but the old man handled those too.

He called it “arrow-warding magic,” and it supposedly worked on pretty much any kind of projectile.

Magic-users were terrifying.

We kept slipping in and out of ruined buildings—changing positions to match the dragon and the flow of the battle—when my turn finally came.

The scout who had been guiding us this whole time pointed up at the roof of a house that hadn’t collapsed yet.

Understanding his intent, I leapt onto the roof, at which point the concealment spell fell away.

At the same time, I closed the distance to the dragon, sprinting at full speed to land a blow on its neck.

There, I got a boost from the old man’s magic.

A tremendous gust of wind—like a forceful shove in the back—propelled me as I jumped.

The dragon noticed me just before my sword reached it, so instead of its neck, I slashed across its face, but the magic sword’s edge was phenomenal. It felt like a solid hit—good enough that I couldn’t complain.

The dragon had always known when to pull back, and it was a good thing this one did as well. It simply took off and flew away. If its temperament had been more vicious, it might have gone on a rampage, ignoring its own survival.

“Well, based on its behavior up to then, I was confident it wouldn’t do that.”

I said that and convinced the higher-ups to let it go… but honestly, I wondered if anyone really found that explanation satisfying.

And so, just as the powers-that-be had planned, I earned the title of Dragonbuster.

At the same time, I heard that the reward money for the mercenaries had been cut.

I protested that putting us in a situation where a night-time assassination wouldn’t be surprising, all because of “We never heard about that,” was going too far.

“In any case, no amount of gold will be enough to rebuild a ruined town. We already intended to reduce their pay somehow. If that’s the case, better that you bear all their resentment and gain a strength that can crush it.”

That was why nobles were such a headache.

They deliberately created situations where even your life might be targeted, just so I would grow strong enough not to be crushed by my own fame.

They did things like that without the slightest sign of guilt, which was exactly why people hated nobles.

Still, if I was going to call myself a genius, I had to accept the challenge.

I was confident in my strength.

And I was sure I was either the strongest human alive or close to it.

“I’ll show you what I can do.”

I spoke in a way that made it sound like I might very well end up dead, but the marquis only answered with a fearless smile.

After that, I was buried in post-war cleanup for quite some time—about a year and a few months.

One day, while I was practicing my sword within the grounds of the marquis’s estate, doing my best to avoid getting ambushed, a royal messenger arrived carrying a document.

Summed up in one phrase, it was a “grant of knighthood.”

I wondered if it was really okay for something like that to be this casual, but apparently a knight’s title was basically just a name with no actual authority, so this was normal.

Because of that, my name changed from plain “Alberto” to “Alberto von Raguel.”

At the same time, they handed me another slip of paper and explained that it was an invitation from the temple.

The end result was that the holy sword was loaned to me.

For the first time in decades, a new Dragonbuster had appeared—and he was handsome to boot. If they were going to use me as a standard-bearer for the coming war with Seizeria, then this level of spectacle made sense.

The problem was the cardinal who handed me the holy sword. His expression was all smiles, but his eyes weren’t smiling at all.

Seriously, Marquis—just how many people did you expect me to be hated by?

Anyway, I now held the strongest weapon of all—the holy sword—and once I used it, I understood.

(This wasn’t a weapon you were supposed to use against human beings.)

If I had to sum up my impression in one phrase, it would be: “This thing is insane.”

On top of its terrifying sharpness, the magical power leaking from the blade aided both offense and defense.

They said it drew on the wielder’s mana, but it didn’t feel like a burden—in fact, it actually felt like my body was lighter.

A sense of omnipotence wrapped around me—as though I, Alberto, could do absolutely anything. That was how outrageously powerful it was.

(So this was the holy sword… this was what a holy sword truly was.)

They said everyone who had wielded it died a tragic death… and honestly, with a weapon like this, I could see how someone might get carried away and die in some stupid way.

For a while, I was drunk on the fact that I had the holy sword, but in any case, it handled completely differently from any weapon I had used before.

Even for a genius like me, adapting to that difference wasn’t easy, and I ended up needing a long period of training.

During that time, I tried learning all sorts of things from that old mage, but in the end, I really did have no talent for magic.

Even so, what I learned about handling mana as the wielder of the holy sword turned out to be incredibly useful.

After a year of training, what waited for me was classes in etiquette.

Basically, the marquis’s plans for using me had grown beyond what he first imagined, thanks to how strong I had become, and now he wanted to exploit me in a different sphere as well—or so I guessed.

Naturally, I found those days unbearably dull, with less and less time to swing a sword.

But that daily life ended abruptly.

“I’ve kept you waiting, Alberto. A monster has appeared that I want you to exterminate.”

+++

# **130 - A Certain Hero’s Point Of View**

In a rattling, bone-shaking carriage with terrible suspension, I was talking with a former mercenary.

Though to be precise, I was the only one chattering away, completely ignoring anything he tried to say and just keeping the conversation going on my own.

“—So anyway, I told them, ‘Give me the youngest girl you’ve got.’ And what did I get? A fifteen-year-old priest as my chaperone, just barely an adult, and a twenty-one-year-old old la—uh, big sister. I don’t mind the old man; I know exactly how strong he is, so he’s no problem at all—in fact, I’d have asked for him myself. But that old la—big sister, no matter how you look at her, she’s just not up to snuff. I mean, if that’s the case, they could’ve just sent someone even younger, right?”

“…No, they could not.”

“Look, I’m being modest here, but I’m pretty handsome, right? On top of that, I’m one of the strongest humans around. Plus I’ve got a marquis house backing me—every possible ‘popular guy’ trait jammed in there. So women flock to me, as many as you like. But the thing is, not one of them is actually looking at ‘me.’ You get that?”

“…No, I do not.”

“So I wanted a pure girl, completely untouched by that kind of thing… but apparently they didn’t quite grasp my intentions, which is a problem, you know? Ahh, if only that chaperone girl were five years younger… Her face is pretty, she’s slim—she would’ve been right on target.”

“…What exactly were you planning to do to that kid?”

“Obviously, educate her. If I’m going to raise her to suit my tastes, she has to be young. I mean, I said ‘a young girl,’ so how did they not realize I wasn’t asking for someone with combat value?”

“More to the point, if you can hear what people are saying, how about shutting your mouth? Don’t assume a mercenary can keep suppressing the urge to punch that smug face of yours forever.”

When the collared former mercenary tried to glare me down, I raised my hands in surrender and said, “So scary, so scary.”

“Don’t be so cold. I was just about to get serious.”

Watching the collared man spit out, “Should’ve started with that,” I held up one finger.

“First point. About the ‘Dragon Buster’ title… Honestly, I couldn’t care less. I probably could have earned it on merit, but at the time, the marquis house wouldn’t allow that. Understand that they wanted a heroic figurehead for the war they expect will break out with the neighboring country soon, and that’s why things ended the way they did.”

“You really think anyone’s going to be satisfied with that?”

“Probably not,” I replied with a laugh, then raised my middle finger alongside the first to indicate, “Second.”

“I’m not counting on you as fighting strength. I know how you fight, and there’s no doubting your skill…”

“But,” I added, and looked at him with something like pity in my eyes.

“You just don’t meet the standard I require.”

A dangerous tension hung in the rattling carriage.

And I poured oil on the fire by continuing.

“Let me be clear. As long as you cough up everything you know about that new species of monster, that’s enough for me. After that, all I need is for you not to get in the way.”

A cheap provocation—but you could never have too many “walls” out on the battlefield.

First, you irritated them and wounded their pride.

You made them so angry they lost their ability to think straight—some other emotion would work too, but in this case anger was probably optimal.

In fact, he gave me exactly the answer I wanted.

“Big talk, huh… If you’re going to run your mouth that much, you’d better make sure your prey doesn’t get snatched away by the likes of us.”

“I’ll keep that in mind,” I said, allowed myself a fearless grin, and jumped down from the carriage just as it came to a halt.

Now he would never skip the fight out of personal resentment.

It was certain he would lie in wait like a hungry tiger, searching for openings in the new species during the battle.

(I’ve heard this monster is highly intelligent. In that case, it will go for the easiest point to break.)

I liked to raise my odds of victory as high as possible, I thought with an inward smirk, and since I’d had enough of that stifling, bone-jarring carriage, I had it stop once so I could switch to another one.

As soon as we arrived at our destination, the town of Lecole, I stepped down from the carriage.

The first place I headed for was the mercenary guild—where I had the guild master called out and addressed him in a thoroughly arrogant manner.

“I’m willing to overlook the mercenary guild’s blunder and even clean up after you. Naturally, I trust you understand your position… so I expect your fullest possible cooperation. Am I clear?”

The reaction went without saying—he looked like he had just swallowed a bitter bug, and his murderous aura was practically visible.

I was well aware I’d said enough to make anyone feel that way—“Well, that figures,” really—so I quickly took my leave of the mercenary guild.

The rulers probably wanted the mercenaries under control before the war broke out, but having me do it seemed more likely to backfire.

I would do exactly as I was told so no one could complain later, but I wanted to corner whoever came up with this idiocy and ask what they planned to do if it got me stabbed in the back.

“Not that I consider myself enough of a fool to actually get stabbed.”

I added aloud, and headed for the manor where the members of this extermination party against the new species were gathering.

“Are you people out of your minds?!”

And the moment I stepped inside, that enraged shout from the collared man hit my ears.

After listening for a bit, it seemed they had used magical insects to deal with the new species of monster.

Even I could only give a wry smile at that.

Handled badly, the swarm could have moved north and devoured several villages.

As I was thinking, “I can’t believe they even got approval for that,” I heard that the lord of this territory was apparently furious and no longer inclined to be picky about methods.

Surprisingly, though, the lure seemed to be working very well, and for the moment everything was proceeding according to our side’s plan.

“Huh. Our apothecary’s not half bad.”

I couldn’t help voicing my admiration, but apparently the situation was, “We have no idea why this is working.”

So much for the compliment.

In short, the people on the ground wanted to exterminate everything before the situation dragged on and disposing of the magic insects became dangerous.

However, the higher-ups showed no sign of budging from their stance of “Maintain it until the new species is dead.”

(Ah, I’ve finally figured out why they dragged me out here.)

No matter how strong this new species of monster was, why go this far when it wasn’t even a dragon?

Once I had the answer to that question, I let out a sigh.

(If I remember right, the lord here is… ah, yes. This should be the family of the chancellor’s son’s wife… I think?)

I’d had all those marriage connections drilled into me, but they were already starting to slip my mind.

Seriously, noble society was such a pain.

In any case, I settled things there by boldly declaring, “I’ll bring back the head of that new species myself.”

I added a request that they dispose of the magic insects as well while they were at it.

Honestly, having bugs interfere in the middle of things would be the last straw.

Dealing with nobles really was a hassle—especially when I had to say lines like, “Who do you think I am? Are you doubting the strength of the Dragon Buster?” to the lord’s reluctant representative.

With those nuisances handled, I checked over the members chosen for this monster hunt before we set out.

First, the mage Kaijis—probably the one I would end up relying on the most.

He was sixty-eight, quite advanced in years, but I knew well that his magical prowess more than made up for his diminished stamina.

No matter what we were up against, having his support was tremendously reassuring.

Next, the mage Sesaira—and unfortunately, I expected nothing from her.

She was apparently outstanding for a young mage, but based on the information I had and my appraisal of her abilities, she was unlikely to be of any real use.

She was a slender woman with striking long red hair, but the shoulder-baring red robe-dress did not suit her build at all.

Apparently she would be mocked if she didn’t wear the latest fashion, but I couldn’t help thinking, “Is that really necessary right now?”

The chaperone priest, Shrin—she fell short in various ways, but she would at least fulfill her role as a walking medicine box.

With her neatly arranged short blond hair and good looks, I could more or less see what they were aiming for, but she was still far off from what I had requested.

And finally, the collared former mercenary Orland—a skilled fighter wielding the magic sword known as Giant-Killer.

I had already set the stage, so all I could do now was hope he would move as I intended.

I gave them a brief once-over and then nodded, small but firm.

“Let’s go.”

We had already discussed everything that needed saying in the carriage.

So with that one brief word, I turned my back on them and began to walk.

+++

# **131**

Even though I was fully motivated, the sky continued to pour rain as if saying, “Not my problem.”

And as long as the rain kept falling, it was certain that humans would struggle to exterminate the magic insects; the most realistic measure would be simply preventing them from getting close to the town.

In other words, as long as the rain continued, I would have to keep running away from those troublesome insects.

(The more I think about it, the more annoying this tactic is.)

I wasn’t sure whether the humans had considered potential losses on their own side, but I had no choice but to acknowledge that it was a highly effective method.

The bad weather made the situation even worse, since it was possible we wouldn’t notice the insect swarm approaching in time and end up sustaining damage.

Even if it was just a coincidence, it was quite a harsh situation.

“Do I take a defensive stance…? Attacking now would be foolish. Since I already understand the terrain, wandering around and bumping into insects would be a waste.”

In short, I had nothing to do.

I sighed and looked up at the sky. “When is this going to stop?”

It turned out to be a day that drove home just how agonizing boredom could be.

And so the night passed.

With the risk of the insects approaching, I couldn’t exactly sleep soundly, so I spent the night training in magic instead—but the results were mediocre at best.

It couldn’t be helped; training while distracted would only lead to this sort of outcome.

Still, the rain had noticeably weakened this morning, which was a good sign.

“If it stops today, that would be ideal,” I thought, deeply appreciating the value of weather forecasts.

Truly, people ought to live alongside the tools of civilization, I grumbled inwardly as the monster that was me reluctantly got moving.

Even if I didn’t need to eat to survive, not eating at all felt unsettling.

So I went hunting, secured prey on the western side, and returned to the temporary base for breakfast.

After that, I headed out for a boredom-fueled patrol around the area—only to find something unexpected.

A large number of insect corpses—taking cannibalism into account, it was almost certain that an entire swarm had been wiped out.

(Ah—right, there’s also the poison-bait variety.)

The Empire usually used gas-spraying types, so I had completely forgotten this variant existed.

Whether the humans chose to eliminate the swarm before it grew too large, or deemed it ineffective and disposed of it, I didn’t know—but either way, the fact that movement was easier now was certain.

There was still a possibility that other swarms existed, so I couldn’t relax entirely, but things were clearly beginning to shift in a favorable direction.

And if the insects had been fully cleared away, it wouldn’t be strange for merchants to resume traveling.

Which meant this was the best time to strike.

It was common sense that halting logistics couldn’t last forever.

I doubted people would start moving before confirming safety, but there was still a chance I was already behind. So I threw on my pack and hurried toward the highway.

Getting my belongings wet wasn’t ideal, but there wasn’t much I could do about that.

I ran through the forest, keeping close enough to observe the highway while maintaining cover, and headed north.

Using my telescopic vision, I looked around but found no signs of anything passing through.

(In this rain, the traces could have washed away… meaning I’ll just have to check manually.)

I slowed down to avoid missing anything, scanning both the forest and the road as I moved northward.

But I found nothing.

Even after reaching the point where the forest thinned out, there were no traces on the highway at all.

(No—attacking wagons exiting to the south wouldn’t get me what I want. I need to target the ones coming in, not going out.)

I chastised myself for losing my cool after the insect incident and reaffirmed my goal of improving my living conditions.

Of course, hostile actions would continue.

After all, they picked this fight, so I had no reason to hold back.

And I wanted them to stop thinking they could deal with me using something as trivial as insects.

I had already given up on making any sort of arrangements inside the Kingdom of Canaan, so I no longer needed to hesitate with these brute-force solutions.

“How will the Kingdom of Canaan overcome this crisis? Find out next time!”

I struck an exaggerated pose, imagining a nonexistent camera dramatically zooming in.

(…No good. My excitement is totally out of control.)

I took a deep breath to calm myself.

Truthfully, I was a little curious to see what they would try next.

They were a good opponent to test the limits of what I could handle now—and also someone humanity might have to face in the future, meaning it was a record worth leaving behind.

I thought it sounded like a good line, but chose not to think about what would happen if I didn’t actually leave any record.

Someday I would have to seriously consider that—but today was not that day.

As the rain cooled my head, I turned back toward the temporary base—when a flicker at the edge of my vision made me stop.

Instinctively using my camouflage ability, I slipped deeper into the forest and hid, staring down the highway.

(It wasn’t my imagination…)

The visibility was terrible, but the shadow I saw was definitely a carriage.

“But only one… no, there’s a second one behind it, but they’re both small. A miss, then?”

They were more like passenger carriages than merchant wagons.

It was safer to assume an important person was traveling, so I decided to let these two pass.

Whatever they were planning, it was something I wanted to see.

So I remained hidden in the forest, watching them pass by.

But when the first carriage drew close—I sensed something from it.

(What is that? I feel like I’ve sensed it somewhere before…)

I tilted my head and tried to recall the sensation, but as the carriage moved away, the prickling, unpleasant sting faded.

Then I finally remembered the word and murmured it aloud.

“…A spirit blade?”

I wasn’t certain.

But I was sure it was some kind of magical presence.

(It’s possible. But it could also just be a very powerful magic sword. Since ordinary weapons don’t work, they must have sent a strong weapon—and someone who can use it.)

I let my shoulders slump.

The reason was simple—if what I felt was a spirit blade… could its wielder surpass that elf?

I immediately answered myself.

“So they still think they can defeat me with honest, straightforward means.”

I muttered while watching the now-vanished carriages.

Were they refusing to accept reality?

Or was this genuinely someone strong enough to defeat me?

If it was the latter, I would gladly see what they could do—but if it was the former, then they would suffer for clinging to that delusion.

It was the least I could do as someone who had once been human—a kind of insurance.

…Or so I claimed, but in truth, I was just throwing a tantrum over being disappointed.

Wryly amused by my own pointlessly rising excitement, I slowly left the area.

The humans’ preparations would be done soon.

(A surprise encounter wouldn’t be bad, but lying in wait also had its charm. And if they don’t come, I can always attack first. Hm, so many choices!)

My original goal had long since evaporated—so when I returned to the temporary base in high spirits and remembered it, all I could do was hold my head in my hands.

+++

# **132**

Once I got back to my base, I decided to assess the situation.

My objective this time had been to acquire spices and such, and improve the quality of my diet.

However, whether the Kingdom of Canaan had caught wind of my movements or not, they were clearly trying to keep me in check by artificially creating swarms of magic insects.

Given that they had resorted to such measures, it was only natural to assume that distribution between the frontier town on the southern development line and the northern regions had been cut off, leaving almost no chance of achieving my goal from my current position.

As for the insects themselves, one of the swarms had already been wiped out by poisoned bait, so I could safely assume that particular threat had been removed.

Almost as if to replace it, a person who seemed to be a spirit blade user had arrived from the north.

Right now, I was thinking about how to deal with this, and if I wanted to achieve my original goal, I would have to set this issue aside temporarily and simply wait for distribution on the Canaan side to return to normal.

Unfortunately, there was no way to accurately predict when that would happen, and I had no choice but to admit that this mission had failed.

I did technically have the option of shifting my area of operations northward, but since that was fully under national control, there was nowhere for me to hide—and combined with my massive body, I could hardly perform any stealth actions at all.

That meant my only viable tactic would be hit-and-run attacks, “day trips” so to speak—and with my stealth weakened, they would quickly pin down my position. From the distance to their points of attack, they would also be able to deduce my mobility.

In other words, I would be handing them two crucial pieces of information: my speed and my endurance.

For all these reasons, if I wanted to achieve my original objective, I would have to move a long way either east or west.

(Which left the spirit blade user as the final issue…)

Honestly, with that veteran elf warrior as my point of comparison, I could already see how this would end.

I had no intention of letting my guard down, but when your benchmark was an elf with blatantly broken specs, it was inevitable that you would underestimate the threat level of anyone else.

I would have liked to see the hidden potential of the human race on display—but I myself had once been human.

Knowing all too well what humans were capable of, I couldn’t expect much from the modern Kingdom of Canaan, which showed no sign of two hundred years of evolution and progress while I slept.

“When I calm down and think about it, ignoring them is actually an option.”

My excitement had been a little off, but once I assessed the situation again, lines like that started slipping out of my mouth.

“But the truth is, I still want to take a swing at them at least once.”

I also wanted to gauge the strength of this spirit blade user, supposedly the Kingdom of Canaan’s greatest asset.

I stayed silent for a while.

Then my answer slipped out in a small murmur.

“All right. Let’s do this.”

Once I decided, I would act.

First, I would show myself and let them know what direction I was in.

If they were ready for a fight but wandered around in the wrong area, it would all be for nothing.

They would need time to prepare, so they were unlikely to come charging out right away, and by then the rain should have stopped as well.

Once I gathered my thoughts, I immediately moved to put them into action, first making sure my belongings were arranged so they would not get wet.

Then I stood up and left the temporary base.

Rain fell through the gaps in the thick foliage and beat down on me as I headed east.

I walked instead of running so I could get a better grasp of the surrounding terrain and pick out locations suitable for battle.

With a body this huge, dense stands of trees alone could obstruct my movements and put me at a disadvantage.

I knew the enemy would understand that as well, so I intended to take my own measures to counter it.

Wandering in a slightly zigzagging path toward the town, I spotted several potential battle sites along the way, and finally exposed myself to the guards on the town wall.

One of the soldiers was in the middle of a yawn and kept chatting with his colleague, not noticing me at all.

The first two soldiers who came into view, closest to me, had their backs resting against the wall and didn’t even give the slightest sign of turning my way.

(…You’re getting a bit too relaxed, aren’t you?)

I ended up just standing there in exasperation, when suddenly the sound of metal being struck rang out.

Apparently a lookout in the watchtower had spotted me and was desperately hammering at a metal plate with a mallet.

Once I saw the soldiers on the wall point in my direction, confirming that I had been noticed, I turned my back and walked away.

This time I did nothing else and simply returned to my base.

On the way, I found some prey and took it down.

That settled tonight’s dinner.

For a moment, I considered making a big show of things with smoke and an open-air feast, but even I knew that would be suspicious.

So I behaved myself again, spent the day training in magic, and let it end.

At dawn, when I woke up, I checked the weather—or rather, I didn’t even need to check to know it was still raining.

(It’s not that different from yesterday… no, it’s a little better.)

It would have been best if it had stopped entirely, but the rain was definitely lighter, so it wasn’t bad.

I wanted to hope it would stop before the fighting started, but there was nothing I could do about that, so all I could do was leave it to fate.

I headed west to hunt and secure breakfast.

I left my pack at the temporary base, so I was careful not to stray too far as I moved.

I managed to spot a rabbit, but unfortunately it darted back into its burrow.

The rain really did have a big impact.

A little while later, I felt I had gone too far from the base, so I shifted my course to the southeast.

I focused on moving in a roughly circular pattern around the temporary base as my center.

Along the way, I discovered several more potential battle sites, but they were a bit too deep in the forest, so I doubted I would have any chance to use them.

After wandering in search of prey for some time, I came upon a patch of terrain I recognized.

(Ah, this is the place I saw when I came back from the town. So I’ve already made half a loop.)

With the sun hidden, I had no sense of the time, which probably made it feel like I had been roaming in search of prey for quite a while.

“I’ll give up on breakfast,” I muttered, and was about to head back to the temporary base when a human voice reached my ears.

I instinctively activated my camouflage ability and glared in the direction the voice had come from.

Using my telescopic vision to identify the source, I picked out a lone man.

(…Light equipment. Very alert to his surroundings. Is he searching for something? No, he’s following a trail… I’d thought their response was faster than expected, but it seems they’ve sent a scout ahead to find my position.)

That, however, made the earlier voice I’d heard more concerning.

The one I could see was a man.

But the voice had sounded like a woman’s.

(Is the main force behind him, or…?)

The distance left many details unclear, but since they had come all this way, I might as well give them some hospitality.

If the scout was following my traces, I could subtly guide him in the direction I wanted.

There was still a bit of distance to the point I had picked out beforehand.

Nothing ventured, nothing gained—so I left clear tracks and led him to a slightly open area with a small mound of rubble.

The trees were a little sparser here, and I had judged that I could expand the battlefield with my raw strength if needed.

I climbed over the rubble mound and dispelled my camouflage behind it.

If I crouched, I could just manage to keep my whole body hidden, so I waited patiently for the scout to approach.

Standing there in the rain, I eventually felt someone arrive.

He probably muttered, “So this is as far as it goes,” under his breath and came to a stop.

Once he crossed over the rubble and the tracks suddenly ended, his level of alertness rose sharply.

It seemed he had no intention of going any farther, so it was time for the reveal.

The moment he saw me suddenly appear on top of the rubble mound, he shouted and bolted back the way he had come.

I couldn’t help but be impressed by how cleanly he chose to run—when a smoke screen went up. Unfortunately for him, the rain weakened it, and by the time I got closer, it was practically gone.

Still, the faint acrid smell was more than enough for me to guess its intended purpose, and the fact that it had failed to take effect was something I had to call good luck.

(Whatever the reason—weather or the man’s mistake—they do have a plan to deal with me. Which means I should assume this won’t end with a simple scouting mission, but lead into a real fight.)

I crushed the little sphere still leaking a trace of smoke underfoot, then stood with the rubble mound at my back and stared toward the direction the man had fled.

There were four of them—one of whom I recognized.

He led the way straight toward me.

If there was one puzzling point, it was the complete lack of that spirit blade pressure.

(So this is just a stalling action until the real one arrives.)

From what I could see, the greatsword-wielding captain was there, and the earlier scout now held a longsword.

Those two were the vanguard, with a mage and a priest acting as combat medic in the rear—a balanced party composition.

(…They’re level one or two at best.)

They were far too young to be facing me.

I looked at the two women—a priest in clerical garb and a mage in a shoulder-baring dress robe—and muttered that impression to myself.

It was clear they were only meant to buy time until the real threat arrived.

To greet the approaching party, I also started walking toward them.

They showed every sign of bracing to receive my charge, but I simply closed the distance with confident strides.

When we came close enough that I could read their expressions clearly, I noticed the young priest in the rear slip a tiny smile onto her face.

I stopped.

A faint current in the air—sound vanishing from one side with unnatural completeness.

And from high behind me, I clearly felt that pressure.

“Grah!”

I whipped around with a backfist.

I made no effort to hold back, but the blow cut through empty air as something shot past me at high speed with the wind.

I heard a click of the tongue, followed by the sound of a landing.

In front of me I saw an arm ending in a severed wrist—and a right hand spinning through the air.

My mouth opened on reflex, and just before a scream could burst out, something inside me went click as the switch flipped.

+++

# **133**

I closed my open mouth.

I calmly caught my right hand with my tail just before it was pulled down by gravity and hit the ground, held it with my left hand, and pressed it hard against the severed surface where blood was spurting out.

I kept my gaze fixed on the pretty-boy who had ambushed me, stayed silent through the whole sequence, and then let go of the hand that had been holding my right hand in place.

“Monster…!”

To an onlooker, my right hand looked like it was reattached.

But it still did not move.

Having been shown that terrifying regenerative power—even if it was just for show—the spirit-sword wielder muttered bitterly and glared at me.

(It was a surprisingly normal reaction. Well, getting your hand cut off would make anyone scream once or twice, but if I silently stuck it back on, of course they’d be shocked. Still… even in that state, I could forcibly hold back my voice and move on my own will…)

Even I was surprised by how calmly I could move.

For now, my right hand was only “stuck on,” and since it gave no response when I tried to move it, I wanted to use a potion as soon as possible.

But before that, I needed to deal with them.

(I underestimated their fighting strength a little too much. I need to reflect on that.)

The only thing I truly needed to watch was the spirit sword.

And this was the result—but I never expected them to launch an ambush straight at me from the front.

At the tactical level, I had to admit it was a complete defeat.

(But in other words, they also missed their biggest chance.)

They must have planned for what would happen if they failed.

Let’s see how they intended to fight me.

“GRAAAAHHH!”

I raised my voice in an intimidating roar, stepped forward hard, and drew back my left hand.

The spirit-sword wielder reacted—yet I had no intention of charging in honestly from the front.

Their weapon could cut me.

Then the best option was never entering its range.

I kicked up the ground as if gouging it out, blasting a huge spray of dirt and stones.

Because his attention had been drawn to my raised hand, his delayed reaction led to him taking the dirt and gravel head-on.

With his vision blocked and his movement hindered, he could not even properly brace for what should have been the real attack.

A mercenary with a greatsword and a scout stepped forward.

The young mage raised her staff and began chanting.

The young priest took the gravel directly and toppled backward.

And I jumped backward.

Maybe no one there understood what I did, because not a single person chased after me when I retreated.

(Ah… so none of them had even considered why I chose to fight here.)

What lay where I jumped—was a pile of rubble.

Was it too primitive of an attack to cross your mind?

Too bad—I’m operating at the “wild animal” level!

I grabbed rubble with my tail and hurled it, then lightly caught it with my left hand.

“Dodge!”

The mercenary finally understood and shouted, but he was a little too late.

I had practiced throwing endlessly.

The fastball I launched smashed the scout’s left shoulder; his left arm tore off at the root and spun into the air.

Seeing the man roll on the ground while dyeing it red with blood made it obvious he was incapacitated.

That made one… but the young priest still lay there without even twitching.

Then that left three.

As the mercenary advanced, I hopped backward in sync with him.

More rubble was already prepared in my hand and tail, and though he somehow blocked a delayed double-shot with his greatsword, he was shoved far back.

(Did you plan for close-quarters combat? Too bad! I can do ranged combat too! At this distance, you’ve got no way to kill me, right!? How will you close in? How will you attack? You can see I’ve got mountains of ammo! Come on—show me how you’ll turn this around from here!)

Just as I replenished my “ammo,” I heard chanting.

With the situation changed, their first move was magic—but I deliberately ignored it.

My knowledge of magic was not perfect, but it was real.

So from the words I heard, I could tell—more or less—what she was trying to do.

And with the spell she was about to cast, she could not even scratch me, let alone kill me.

The spirit-sword wielder brushed off the dirt and returned to the front line, saw the rubble in my hands, and spat, “Damn it!”

The wielder advanced, and a lump of fire was released in tandem.

Ignoring the approaching flame, I watched the wielder leap toward me atop the rubble pile.

The fire hit me directly, but I showed no sign of caring and threw rubble anyway.

He cut it down and pressed in, but then a flat slab of rubble fired from my tail spun as it flew toward the man who had fully committed to his swing.

It hit—or so I thought for an instant—but the rubble was deflected by something invisible and drifted past him to the rear.

“Oh, right. That was a thing.”

I realized—the same defense the elven wielder had shown before was confirmed to come from the spirit sword’s power.

That let him swing again, forcing me into a choice: meet him head-on or abandon my advantageous position.

The flame that had not even stung me vanished, and the pretty-boy raised the spirit sword and closed in.

The man who had set up that ambush had no reason to throw himself into a suicidal attack.

Facing a charge that felt almost like certainty—“He has a plan”—I lowered my hips and chose to intercept.

In that instant—something yanked my right arm hard, and one of my feet lifted off the ground.

(There was another one!)

If that reckless-looking leap had been meant to set this up, then it made sense.

Now that I thought about it, I had been uneasy that the mage seemed too young to pull off concealment that perfect—but I never expected someone to have stayed hidden until this very moment.

(So the ambush fails and you still aim for another ambush!)

My posture was broken, and the spirit sword was coming—there was no time to evade anymore.

So I leaned my upper body far back, arched until my back nearly touched the rubble, and stretched my tail.

And before the wielder could bring the spirit sword down, I lifted his legs.

By giving myself height with my tail as a foothold, the downward swing only left a thin cut across my chest.

The wielder’s eyes tracked me as I lay on the rubble, even as his body carried him past.

Of course, I was not kind enough to let his landing go—

…but the mercenary provided solid support.

Just as I went to throw rubble, he slashed at me; I reflexively clamped the blade between my right elbow and knee, but when that greatsword did not break, an involuntary “Huh?” slipped out.

(Right—this was that strangely hard, unbreakable sword.)

Even so, there should have been a limit, I grumbled internally at the unfairness of magic swords, then struck with my tail and forced him back.

I praised his decision to let go and withdraw cleanly, and when I knocked the greatsword aside, it flew back toward the mercenary.

(…Ah. So she used magic.)

The female mage I had completely forgotten about had a smug look on her face.

Her face was pretty, but to pull off that outfit, didn’t she need a bit more… volume?

Putting appearances aside, this was a reset.

The situation was: me on top of the rubble pile, flanked by the spirit-sword wielder, the mercenary captain I had been seeing far too often, and the female mage—plus one more capable mage whose position I still could not pinpoint.

By the way, up to now I had not used my severed right hand even once.

Since I dealt with the mercenary’s strike using my elbow and knee, I should assume they had noticed my real condition.

It had been a short exchange, but the information I gained was enough.

(It’s time to pull back.)

Having decided that, I turned my back on the spirit-sword wielder, who was barking some kind of instructions.

If I was going to break through, it was better to choose the safer direction.

That captain could not stop my charge.

I knocked him aside, ignored the mage who had started chanting far too late, and sprinted deep into the forest.

The escape was so easy that I felt something was off, so I stopped and looked back down the path I had come.

“I see. So that’s how it is.”

Now that I could sense magic power, I understood.

A thin, thread-like line of magic power—was extending from me.

It was probably the work of that skilled mage who never showed themselves.

(If they can’t stop me from escaping, they’ll chase me anywhere… huh.)

It wasn’t a bad choice, but it was foolish for humans to try to match me in stamina.

“Or do you still have another plan?”

Muttering with my hand at my chin, I sent my gaze toward the direction of my temporary base.

More than anything, I needed to heal this hand with a potion first.

I returned to my temporary base while being careful not to lose them completely.

I looked back many times along the way, but I could not tell whether they were pursuing me.

Still, as long as that line of magic power was extending from my body, some kind of decisive conclusion was inevitable.

When I reached the temporary base, I immediately took out a potion from my pack and poured it over my right wrist.

I had braced myself for “I might have to pour it directly onto the cut,” but that fear proved unfounded, and I slowly formed a fist with my right hand.

I clenched and opened it again and again.

“…No problem.”

I muttered that softly and let a thin smile rise.

In reality, nothing had changed much—but that was how I felt right now.

I reached into my pack and grabbed the saber attached at my side.

Then I reached for the tower shield too, hesitated for a moment, and took it.

(No carelessness. I’ll fight them as enemies.)

Now then—let’s follow this thread of magic power and announce the start of round two.

+++
